THE RED PAGE.
The Ballad of Heading Gaol.
For The Bulletin.
CARL.
I.
He did not wear his scarlet coat,
For blood and wine are red,
And blood and wine were on his hands
When they found him with the dead,
The poor dead woman whom he loved,
And murdered in her bed.
VII.
Yet each man kills the thing he loves,
By each let this be heard,
Some do it with a bitter look,
Some with a flattering word,
The coward does it with a kiss,
The brave man with a sivord.
VIII.
Some kill their love when they are young,
And some when they are old;
Some strangle with the hands of Lust,
Some with the hands of Gold:
The kindest use a knife, because
The dead so soon grow cold.
IX.
Some love too little, some too long,
Some sell, and others buy ;
Some do the deed with many tears,
And some without a sigh ;
For each man kills the thing he loves,
Yet each man does not die.
XXX VI.
With slouch and swing around the ring
We trod the Fools’ Parade !
We did not care : we knew we were
The Devil’s Own Brigade :
And shaven head and feet of lead
Make a merry masquerade.
XXXVII.
We tare the tarry Tope to shreds
With blunt and bleeding nails ;
We rubbed the doors, and scrubbed the floors,
And cleaned the shining rails :
And, rank by rank, we soaped the plank,
And clattered with the pails.
XXXVIII.
We sewed the sacks, we broke tlie stones,
We turned the dusty drill :
We banged the tins, and bawled the hymns,
And sweated on the mill:
But in the heart of every man
Terror was lying still
XXXIX.
So still it lay till every day
Crawled like a weed-clogged wave :
And we forgot the bitter lot
That waits for fool and knave,
Till once, as we tramped in from work f
We passed an open grave.
XL.
With yawning mouth the yellow hole
Gaped fora living thing;
The very mud cried out for blood
To the thirsty asphalte ring :
And we knew that ere one dawn grew fair
Some prisoner had to swing. - ,
' XLI.
Right in we went, with soul intent
On Death and Dread and Doom :
The hangman, with his little bag,
Went shuffling through the gloom :
And each man trembled as he crept
Into his numbered tomb.
XL VIII.
The gray cock crew, the red cock crew,
But never came the day :
And crooked shapes of Terror crouched
In the corners where ice lay :
And each evil sprite that walks by night
Before us seemed to play.
XLIX.
They glided past, they glided fast,
Like travellers through a mist :
They mocked the moon in a rigadoon
Of delicate turn and twist,
And with formal pace and loathsome grace
The phantoms kept their tryst.
L.
With mop and mow, we saw them go,
Slim shadows hand in hand:
About, about, in ghostly rout
They trod a saraband :
And the damned grotesques made arabesques,
Like the ivind upon the sand.
LVII.
At last I saw the shadowed bars,
Like a lattice wrought in lead,
Move right across the whitewashed wall
That faced my three-plank bed,
Ami I knew that somewhere in the world
God’s dreadful dawn was red.
LVIII.
At six o'clock we cleaned our cells,
At seven all was still,
But the sough and swing of a mighty wing
The prison seemed to fill,
For the Lord of Death with icy breath
Had entered in to kill.
_ LXXXVI.
They hanged him as a beast is hanged ;
They did nob even toll
A requiem that might have brought
Rest to his startled soul,
But hurriedly they took him out,
And hid him in a hole.
LXXXVII.
They stripped him of his canvas clothes,
And gave him to the flies :
They mocked the swollen purple throat,
And the stark and stating eyes :
And with laughter loud they heaped the shrouc
In which the convict lies.
LXXXIX.
Yet all is well; he has bub.passed
To Life’s appointed bourne :
And alien tears will fill for him
Pity’s long-broken urn,
For his mourners will be outcast men,
And outcasts always mourn.
XC.
I know not whether Laws be right,
Or whether Laws bp wrong ;
All that we know who lie in gaol
Is that the wall is strong ;
And that each day is like a year,
A year whose days are long.
XCI.
But this I know, that every. Law
That men have made for Man,
Since first Man took his brother’s life ,
And the sad world began,
But straws the wheat and saves the chaff
With a most evil fan.
XCII.
This too I know—and wise it were
If each could know the same —
That every prison that men build
Is built with bricks of shame,
And bound with bars lest Christ should see
How men their brothers maim.
XCIV.
The vilest deeds like poisoned weeds
Bloom well in prison-air :
It is only what is good in Man
That wastes and withers'there :
Pale Anguish keeps the heavy gate,
And the Warder is Despair.
xcr.
For they starve the little frightened child
Till it weeps both night and day :
And they scourge the weak, and flog the fool,
And jibe the old and gray,
And some grow mad, and all grow bad,
And none a' word may say.
XCVI.
Each narrow cell in which we dwell
Is a foul and dark latrine,
And the fetid breath of living Death
Chokes up each grated screen,
And all, but Lust, is turned to dust
In Humanity’s machine.
XCVII.
The brackish water that we drink
Creeps with a loathsome slime,
And the bitter bread they weigh in scales
Is full of ohalk and lime.
And S.uep will not lie down, but walks
Wild-eyed, and cries to Time.
XCVIII. .
But though lean Hunger and green Thirst
Like asp with adder fight,
We have little care of prison fare,
For what chills and kills outright
Is that every stone one lifts by day
Beoomes one’s heart by night.
XCIX.
With midnight always in one’s heart,
And twilight in one’s cell,
We turn the crank, or tear the rope,
Each in his separate Hell,
And the silenoe is more awful far
Than the sound of a brazen bell.
C.
And never a human voice comes near
To speak a gentle word :
And the eye that watches through the door
Is pitiless and hard :
And by all forgot, we rob and rot,
With soul and body marred,
CL
And thus we rust Life’s iron chain
Degraded and alone :
And some men curse, and some men weep,
And some men make no moan :
But God’s eternal Laws are kind
And break the heart of stone.
CVII.
In Reading gaol by Reading town
There is a pit o] shame,
And in it lies a wretched man
Eaten by teeth of flame,
In a burning winding-sheet he lies,
And his grave has got no name.
CVIII.
And there, till Christ call forth the dead,
In silence let him lie :
No need to waste the foolish tear,
Or heave the windy sigh:
The man had killed the thing he loved,
And so he had to die.
CIX.
And all men kill the thing they love,
By all let this be heard,
Some do it with a bitter look,
Some with a flattering word,
The coward does it with a kiss, V
The brave man with a sword 1
“The Ballad of Reading Gaol, by C. 3. 3.”
(Smithers, Bond.), is tlie work of Oscar Wilde,
whose previous performances stamp him a Man
of Talent, not a Man of Genius. “ The Ballad ”
in nowise alters the classification. It is a very
strong piece of work, but it is not inspired. Hood’s
“Dream of Eugene Aram,” which has probably
modelled the manner and metre, takes higher
rank: so does Coleridge’s “Ancient Mariner,”
which may have helped in the shaping. Yet
“ Eugene Aram ” has only 36 stanzas to “ Read-
ing Gaol’s ” 109. I here is certainly no gain in
effect commensurate with the gain in extent;
and one thinks a stronger effect would have come
by compression.
But it seems that Wilde has endeavored to com-
bine the ethical appeal and the esthetic appeal,
and has failed naturally and necessarily. Just in
proportion as the denunciation suecet ds, the poem
fails as a piece of literature, a piece of Art. To
reach its highest potency, the artistic emotion
should be a purb emotion, should not be compli-
cated by references to anything rotten in the state
of Denmark. Eor such references are suscepti-
ble of intellectual doubt and argument, and
when argument comes in at the door Art flies
out at the window. In other words, to
get the greatest possible reaction, Art
must appeal to the mind undivided against
itself : there must be no hesitation, no reserves.
Hence it is that pure Beauty is a priori likely to
produce the greatest artistic effect : to Beauty one
surrenders unreservedly. But pure ugliness, ac-
cording to European conventions, may also pro-
duce an artistic effect : there may be undoubted
aesthetic charm in a Satsuma idol.
Yet there are some human ideas of such general
acceptance that they do not complicate the mes-
sage, and these may be so introduced as to heighten
the artistic emotion. Millet’s landscapes would
stand alone, but Millet’s peasants wonderfully
increase their artistic significance. But here
there is no jarring note. We yield with
a whole mind to sympathy with the toil
of “The Sower,” the prayer at “The Angelus.”
Homer’s battles are free to produce their whole
artistic effect because Greek and Trojan are no-
thing to us But if one used the same words in
the same way to describe the current war, ob-
viously either a Spaniard or. an American would
miss much of their force. His racial or individual
preconceptions and prejudices would fill his mind
to the exclusion of the artistic appeal. Yet
Homer’s description of a moonlit scene can be
freely accepted at any time.
Hood, in “Aram," if he denounces anything,
denounces murder; his poem exhibits the Nemesis
that follows murder. Well, there is a generally-
accepted convention that murder should be de-
nounced ; and so the mind remains free
to accept the artistic message. But Wilde, in
“Reading Gaol,” denounces the English prison
system ; and indicates some of that system’s
brutal blots. But are all the points which he
stresses abominable? Is it possible to mend all?
A flood of controversy surges up, overwhelming
the artistic effect of the poem.
These lines are written to show that “The
Ballad of Reading Gaol ” is not on the highest
literary | lane, and to show why it is not. Other
reasons are the ephemeral character of the system
attacked, making against artistic permanency;
and the want of vagueness, of mystery, in the
attack, making against artistic illusion. Re-
mains that “The Ballad ” is a magnificent piece
of work for humanity.
Yet it is only when Oscar Wilde goes to gaol
that he realises the misery and brutality of gaol.
The appeal of that victim of N.S.W. judicial
maladministration, Thomas Suffield, makes no
impression on the minds of thousands of unim-
aginative persons. To understand his case they
need to suffer so themselves. But that is another
sermon.

THE ILLUSTRATED LONDON NEWS of
Sept. 28, 1895, says:

Various Verses.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Adieu, Summer!
Where is our lusty Summer,
Whose wanton smile and sunny brow
Dwelt long upon the hillsides ?
His scented breathing mingled with
The dawn’s.first whispers ; at noon
The green fruits blushed within his hand ;
And evening lulled with song his slumber palm.
Not Winter’s windy riot,
Nor Autumn chills, nor bitter rain
To mar his golden presence ;
The Spring’s white cataracts were still;
Sweet Silence o’er the rivers
Strayed, and spread her silvery wings
Along their shining courses day by day.
Prom the bright, quiescent sea
But faintest murmurs and deep sighs,
As the waves kissed his warm lips
Where saffron margins lined the shores,
And sleepy winds came slowly
Prom cooler latitudes to paint
Its sunlit crest a truer, deeper blue.
He strode along the meadows
Thro’ grasses green and clover sweet,
And left his golden imprint
Trembling upon the fields of corn ;
In the drowsy insects’ hum
He simmered his hot life away
Against the dim horizons day by d ly.
The far old mountain-ridges
He folded in his purple robes—
They held him sweetly sleeping—
And there wild Winter smothered hi n
In an avalanche of snow ;
Nor feeble wish may waken him
’Till o’er his grave the maiden Spring doth wepp.
Tasmania.
BARKIS.
Resurgam.
Ah, do not weep ! for vainly death is trying
To tear my captive soul away from yours.
There is no sting, no bitterness in dying
While you are with me, while your love endures.
The Darkness cannot hold me in subjection
Till you forget, till —but it cannot be !
For you are all my hope of resurrection :
Remembrance, sweet, is Immortality.
If in the glory of some dew-clad morning,
When all creation thrills with song and glee,
Your yearning heart, the wooing breezes scorning,
Should turn away and softly call for me ;
Then deathless Love, the mighty necromancer,
Will echo through the graves the name you
cried,
And I shall 1 ear, I shall awake and answer,
And you will feel me walking by your side.
Or if the sadness of a sunset hour
Should touch—encompass—hold you, till at last
You drift away in Fancy’s subtle pow’r,
And gazing dreamily into the past
Your wandering thoughts should suddenly dis-
. cover
Some hidden trifle, just a glimpse of yore—
Then I arise and am again your lover ;
Then lam with you; then I live once more !
Q.
VON KOTZE.
Three in a Thicket.
This is the thicket ; now to hide.
God ! what a hell is yonder light:
Her chamber —he is by her side ;
This blade shall end it all to-night.
Ah, they are moving. I will crawl
A little nearer—so, ’tis well—
Pools ! shadows blab upon the wall;
Your feast to-night shall end in hell.
Now I must think—if this poor brain,
That seems all blood and rage and fire,
Can strike on some new path again
Prom this delirium of desire.
What said she, near this very place,
A few hours since ? I see her now;
A haughty coldness in her face,
As she laughs back each burning vow.
List! she is singing. Let her sing ;
And let him drink her words like wine,
And dally and toy, and kiss and cling,
And snatch down heaven; I’ll soon have mine!
But oh ! to crush her royal breast
In one fierce grasp, and quivering feel
Passion’s white lava unsuppressed
Surge through us as our bodies reel.
But, hush ! They come—from bush to bush
I’ll crouching steal with tigerish pace,
And laughing leap, and with a push
Send them to hell when they embrace !
Q.
ARTHUR A. D. BAYLODON
Pekin Gazette prints an announcement
of the commutation, by order of the Emperor, of
the sentence upon one Wong Tzi from quartering
to simple decapitation, in which privilege his
children are to share. Wong’s offence was the
omission, in historical mention of certain Chinese
Emperors, of part of their prescribed style and
title. That’s the sort of Emperor who would have
just suited the late Burdett Smith, C.M.G., &c., of
Sydney.
Mr. T. C. Allen (member Eng. Assoc, of
N.S.W.), Consulting Engineer, having entered
into partnership with Mr. Arthur Griffith
(M.L.A.), International Patent Agent, 41 Castle-
reagh-street, Sydney, the firm will henceforth be
in a position to supply to intending patentees
without charge expert engineering opinion on the
merits of their inventions.*
Queensland boasts a little over 5000 J’sP.,
and every quarter sees a few dozen more put on
the list. The fat alderman with a 60-inch paunch,
who always signs himself “esq.;” the bullocky
bucolic who signs his name with a cross, and the
high-falutin sugar-planter who has a private
Kanaka-cemetery, constitute nine-tenths of the
Great Unpaid in Bananaland. As to the decent
residuum, they get on by accident and off by
intention as soon as possible.

whether in the form of Political, Social, or other Articles,

SATURDAY, MAY 21, 1898.
The Best Constitution on Earth.
The Victorian anti-Federal party, of which
ex-Minister Allan M‘Lean is the most
vociferous luminary, has issued a manifesto
in which it gives its reasons why Victoria
should lie low and keep dark on June 3. In
some features this document strikingly re-
sembles similar publications which strew the
surface of New South Wales ; in others it
doesn’t. The Want and Rosa and Norton
and Tommy Dodd faction in Sydney is against
Federation because, so it alleges, Victoria
will still be allowed to grant bounties ; the
M'Lean faction in Melbourne is against it
because Victoria won't be allowed to grant
bounties. The Want crowd is hostile be-
cause, it says, New South Wales is going to
be the sole loser and Victoria is to be en
riched at its expense ; the M'Lean following
implores every good Cabbage-gardener to
come down on the Convention Bill with the
axe of just indignation because, it says, Vic-
toria will lose £600,000 a year, and won’t
have a chance to make it up at anybody’s
expense. Want, Hogue, aiid others in
Sydney say bitterly that under the Common-
wealth the Riverina trade will go to Mel-
bourne ; and the Victorian anti-Federal push
demonstrates quite as successfully that the
great object and the certain result of the Bill
is to force the Riverina trade to Sydney.
Also Ashton, M.L.A., when he isn’t in
Sydney helping Want to show that the
Riverina trade must go to Melbourne, is
wailing through the Riverina about how the
Bill will cut off that district from Melbourne,
and force it to trade, against its will, with
Sydney. And the Melbourne anti-Federalists
solemnly appeal to the Victorian Democracy
to reject the Commonwealth scheme because—
If you accept the Bill you will be exchanging: one 0 f the
best Constitutions that the world has ever known (that
which we now enjoy) for one which, according to its
most ardent supporters, is blurred with grave and serious
errors.
Which is so very like the appeal of the New
South Wales Fat Man to the New South
Wales electors to reject the same Bill in de-
fence of the principle of One-man-one-vote
—a principle that no New South Wales Fat
Man was ever concerned about before—that
this resemblance also is quite remarkable.
* * » *
Among all the shoreless seas of gall which
ljave foamed over the proposed Constitution,
this appeal to the Victorian Democracy to
reject Federation, in order to preserve its
own dreadful Constitution in all its native
horror, is about the largest breaker of the
lot. To begin with, however, there is no
immediate question of exchanging Constitu-
tions at all. The Federal and provincial
Constitutions will exist side by side, and the
worst political system on the Australian
mainland will still be preserved—until the
people revolt, or the Federal authority grows
sufficiently strong to tear up the infamous
House of 48 Rich Landlords by the roots.
Therefore the Melb. Anti-Billiouspushdoesn’t
put the case quite fairly. In any case, the
intellect or the honesty of the four politicians
who put their names to a deliberate asser-
tion that Victoria’s utterly smellful political
system is one of the best in the world must
be something beyond description. The only
things against the Victorian Constitution are
that it is about the most ingenious dodge
on earth for preserving the outward sem-
blance of liberalism with the inward reality
of bigoted conservatism and class tyranny,
and that the famous House of 48 Rich Land-
lords makes it one of the worst constitutions
now existing.
* * * *
The Victorian Assembly is still largely
dominated by the property-suffrage, every
attempt to establish the democratic principle
of One-man-one-vote having been contemp-
tuously trodden on by the House of 48 Rich
Landlords. Therefore, some men have one
vote and some men have a great multitude of
votes. Also, a large number have no vote at
all ; a rigid residence-qualification, rigidly
interpolated, does good service in (his direc-
tion. Where the Victorian has a vote, a
beautifully haphazard system of allot-
ting the representation makes its value
very uncertain. For instance, at the 1897
election the 1499 electors of Windermere
had one member; also the 1743 at Port-
land ; 1784 in Johnny Isaacs’ constituency
of Ovens ; 1781 at Maldon ; 1685 in Pea-
cock’s electorate of Clunes ; and a dozen
more showed about the same figures. Also
6000 electors at Donald and Swan Hill had
one member ; 3572 at Dandenong ; 3333 at
Ballarat East ; 3776 in Hancock’s demo-
cratic borough of Footscray, where the
bone-crushing mills are ; 5108 m Deakin’s
constituency of Essendon ; and so on. And
while 6000 agriculturists of Donald and
Swan Hill have one representative—in East
Melbourne, where the Tory Zox hangs out,
4322 electors have two representatives. The
3384 electors of Castlemaine have two also.
In Victorian Assembly elections, therefore,
some men have no vote, and some have one
vote, and some have 18 votes ; and among
the people who have one vote the difference
is that some votes are worth four times as
much as others. As the result of this weird
system that awful Tory, Jonas Levien, is in
Parliament as the representative of 932
votes, and that other Tory, Staughton, is
there by virtue of 865 votes ; and MTntyre
represents 819 ; and the slow and useless
Duncan Gillies was returned by 1355 votes.
Yet grand old Graham Berry was the
choice of 2284 Victorian citizens, and Hen-
nessy had 2126 votes, and they are both
out. Further, that loud and sturdy Demo-
crat, Prendergast, with 1096 votes, and
Lazarus, with. 1972, were among the re-
jected. Barring all these things, however,
the Victorian Assembly may be called reason-
ably democratic in its constitution, and it
is much more democratic than could be ex-
pected, considering its origin.
* * » •
The Victorian Council is also elective,
which is probably the reason why the Mel-
bourne anti-Billious party regards it with
such pride. There is an elaborate and com-
plicated property suffrage, however—so
elaborate and complicated that at last year’s
election there were only 133,000 Council
electors against 245,000 for the Assembly. A
very large part of the Victorian democracy
has no voice in choosing the 48 Rich Land-
lords. Only a man aged 30 or upwards, and
possessed of real property worth a net annual
rental of £lOO is eligible to sit in the Coun-
cil ; this qualification, at present values,
means that he must possess “lands or tene-
ments” worth about £4OOO over and above
the mortgages. All men in their compara-
tively early youth are shut out; ditto, all
poor men ; ditto, all rich men whose wealth
is solely in Government or other securities,
ships, machinery, stock-in-trade, cattle,
sheep, share, or deposits, or is the mere earn-
ings of their brains—the Horn e of 48 Rich
Landlords is confined to landlords only. Also,
as the members are unpaid, and the Council
holds out practically no prospect of political
advancement, it is further confined to rich
landlords of leisure who are willing to take
a hand in politics with no reward except the
joy of throwing out any Democratic measure
that comes in their way. Therefore, if the
electors who vote under the Council’s pro-
perty suffrage were Democratic, which, in a
very large degree, they are not, they are
absolutely prohibited from choosing anybody
save the members of a very small class, and
that the most bitterly Tory class in Victoria.
Also, the members hold their seats for six
years, and the Council can’t be dissolved—
it is an absolutely permanent institution,
and never dissolves. Even if it were pos-
sible, despite the narrow suffrage and the
fact that there is hardly anybody but the
existing gang of fossils to choose from, that
a wave of popular indignation could sweep
the 48 Rich Landlords from their seats, it
never gets a chance to do so ; the House of
Landlords doesn’t all go for re-election at
once, but a third of it retires and comes back
again every second year. That is to say, it
walks out of the House, stands outside a
minute, and comes in again. In the absence
of any general election, and any body of
Democratic electors, and any possible Demo-
cratic candidates, the retiring fossil is simply
re-elected without opposition, and he goes
out and stands outside the House for a
minute to signify that he is appealing to the
free and independent electors.
* * * *
The Victorian House of 48 Rich Landlords
deliberately rejects nearly every Democratic
measure sent up to it, and it blocks progress
continuously and hopelessly. There is no
possible remedy for deadlocks in the Vic-
torian Constitution, and the 48 rich bigots
over-ride the people every time. There is no
hope of the Council becoming Democratic for
an indefinite period; the purely-landlord
class isn’t built that way. It is an elective
body only, so it can’t be swamped by Demo-
cratic nominees. Its narrow and mainly
Tory .constituency can’t be widened save
with the Council’s own consent. The
property qualification which makes its mem-
bers almost all Tory can’t be altered save
with the consent of these same Tory mem-
bers. And the chance of getting that consent
is about the deadest thing that has yet con-
trived to escape burial.
* * * •
This is what Victoria has. To all intents
and purposes only one-half its adult male
population have votes—the half which has
votes for the Council; because the represen-
tatives of that half have, and use, the most
absolute power to block every desire and
aspiration of the rest of the people. And
that half which has effective votes isn’t
allowed to use them as it pleases,
but is compelled to select members of
one small class only. The 133,000
Council voters have also votes —many of them
six or eight or more—for the Assembly ;
they elect a full half of one House and the
whole of the other. Therefore, they control
both the House which initiates legislation
and the one which blocks it, and the other
112,000 electors are present on sufferance
only. What the proposed Federal Constitu-
tion offers to Victoria is a Legislature where
both Houses will be paid ; where there will
be no plural vote for either House ; where
there will be no property qualification for
either House ; and it also guarantees that
every man or woman who has a vote for the
Legislative Assembly of his or her own State
shall have a vote for each House of the
Federal Legislature. Both Houses of the
Federal Parliament will be purely and abso-
lutely Democratic ; both Houses will rest
solely on the will of the people ; and both
Houses will be dissolvable when they come
in collision with popular sentiment. This
means that a large number of Victoria’s in-
terests and affairs will be removed from the
control of the House of Landlords and placed
under the control of a purely Democratic
Legislature. And the one hope of mending
the awful Victorian Legislative Council which
has glimmered on the political horizon for
30 years or more lies in the fact that the
Democratic Federal Legislature, as its influ-
ence expands, may ultimately get power
enough to abolish that institution and supply
Victoria with something respectable in its
place.
* * * #
The one real complaint which the Vic-
torian Democracy has to make against the
Federal scheme is identical with that of
the Democracy north of the Murray. The
representation is not wholly equitable, and
one Tasmanian democrat has about as much
voting power as 2b Victorians. But, even
with this drawback, Victoria has as much
voting power as Tasmania and Westralia
combined. And, at the worst, the proposed
conditions are very many times better than
those under which the Victorian lives now,
and very many times better than anything he
is likely to live under should Federation be re-
jected, unless he mends his House of 48 Rich
Landlords by main force. For a community
which peacefully endures the present Vic-
torian Constitution—a Constitution which is
one vast and complicated outrage on every
principle of Representative Government—to
seriously complain of the small discrepancy
in the representative system embodied in the
Federal Bill is straining at a gnat and swal-
lowing the planet J upiter.
“The Bulletin’s” Financial Curse.
In view of the fact that a new session of the
Victorian Parliament starts next month, Pre-
mier Turner again receives positive orders
from his proprietor, David Syme, of the Age,
that he is to give up his policy of economy and
break out into a violent eruption of public
works. If he doesn’t, David Syme will this
time certainly holler down the spout and have
a new Premier sent up, and George Turner
will be wanted no longer. The warning is ac-
companied by a statement for the last three
years or so, showing the large sums spent out
of loans on public works in N.S.W., and the
very small ones spent, mostly out of revenue,
in Victoria. And Turner is distinctly informed
that this sort of thing won’t do. So far as
concerns the latter part of the statement, there
is no doubt that the Syme organ speaks the
hard and gritty truth. Victoria needs more
railways, especially in Eastern Gippsland and
the Wimmera ; more roads and bridges and
culverts and irrigation works, and many mil-
lions of pounds worth of other improvements.
Its population has increased very, slowly
in the last few years, simply because the
supply of these things stopped short and
the process of opening up the empty
parts of the province was not carried on with
sufficient energy. The future increase will be
very slow also if the same cautious policy is
still adhered to. It is all a question of money.
If the needful funds are raised by loans the un-
stable Age may bless Turner in the present,
but it will assuredly curse him by-and-bye when
the inevitable panic follows. Also, whatever
the people of Victoria may say tq-day they
will also curse him by-and-bye ; and just by
way of variety The Bulletin will curse him
in the immediate present and keep on doing it
all the time. If, on the other hand, he raises
the needful cash out of taxation, and the Age
chances to take a favorable view of the case and
doesn’t blast him out of existence at once, and
he can hold office long enough to demonstrate
the great advantages of a self-reliant policy,
and the Victorian democracy has sense enough
to support him in the meantime, then there is
a magnificent future ahead of Turner.
* * • *
By the time Parliament meets next month
the Turner Government must decide whether
it will go on the old, thriftless insane borrow,
or whether it will have the courage of its
opinions and be honest. The Bulletin appeals
to it once more on this matter, mainly because
it is the only Government in Australia which
has the elements of honesty about it. Within
the last year or two, the custom of raising loans
in England has gone somewhat out of fashion,
and three provinces—N.S.W., Victoria, and
S.A.—have done their latest flotations in their
own cities. No one has noticed that this was
any burden on the community ; instead it has
been hailed with delight by many as affording
a convenient form of investment. And, so far
as concerns the community at large, it would be
just as little a burden if the same sums had
been raised for the same purposes by extra
taxation, and spent in the same fashion. This
may seem a startling assertion to a community
which has been so accustomed to the old ways
and the old groove that it has forgotten the
very meaning of self-reliance, but it is capable
of easy demonstration all the same.
* * * *
If the money is borrowed locally at 3 per
cent., and spent locally on reproductive works
that yield 3 per cent., the situation is simply
that the State gets, say, £1,000,000 per annum
from a small number of the people, and they
get 3 per cent, for it in cash. If the same
million per annum is raised locally by taxation,
and expended on the same works, it means that
the State raises the same amount from all the
people, and they get the same 3 per cent, for it
in the shape of reduced taxation or other bene-
fits. The amount of money taken from the
people is the same; the works are the same;
the dividend is the same, only it is paid differ-
ently and divided among the whole community
instead of going to a small part of it only.
There is no more drain on the total wealth of
the community one way than the other. But
the difference lies in the fact that instead of the
bulk of the community gradually becoming the
bond-slaves of a small section through the
accumulation of paper liabilities and the pro-
cess of compound interest, there is no liability,
and no section of the community holds a lien
on the bodies and souls of the nation at large.
The only other difference lies in the fact that
under the system which The Bulletin
advocates certain pieces of paper called
bonds—paper of no intrinsic value whatever,
and causing a great expense in iron safes to
save it from being burnt or mouse-eaten —would
not be in existence. The money which they
represent, and the public works which repre-
sent that money, and the profits of these works,
would all be there just the same.
• • * •
If the money is raised locally, and spent
locally on useful and profitable works, then
after it has been raised and spent the amount
of money in the country is the same, and the
average wealth of each individual inhabitant is
the same. Only the province is this much the
richer that after raising a million of cash locally,
and spending a million of cash locally on public
works, and having the million still left, it has
also the public works to the good, and the
profit of them, and the extra land values or
other indirect profits which they have created.
And while the money has been changing hands
in paying wages to all manner of people it has
vastly stimulated trade, and made the country
prosperous where it was once stagnant. It is
not money that Victoria wants —there are
<£2,200,000 more of actual gold in its banks
now than there were in the glorious boom days
of 1886 when almost every Victorian was
flourishing, and the country was one golden
dream of affluence. The whole difference
between the bad days now and the good ones
then lies in the fact that then a sovereign, or
more likely the note or cheque which repre-
sented it, changed hands perhaps a dozen limes
in the week, while now it lies in a bank vault
from one year’s end to the other. If the
Turner Government would draw up a list of
£10,000,000 worth of profitable public works
which are urgently needed—such as the great
irrigation works and railways in the mallee
country, and the Gippsland lines which are so
much required to make Victoria’s Lost Pro-
vince saleable and habitable—and would set to
w ork to raise £1,000,000 a year extra taxation
for their construction, the lost boom would
come back again. But this time it would be a
boom with no fearful load of foreign liabilities
behind it. There would be more employment,
and a large increase of population as the result
of increased employment, atid the lands that
were opened up would bring in more population
with money in its pockets, and the influx of
people would make the value of the Fat Per-
son’s property go up again. The boom that
is made with foreign loans grows continually
smaller because the increasing interest bill eats
away its foundations. The boom that was


made with the country’s own money would
keep growing bigger, because the increasing
revenue from the works constructed would sup-
port it and make it more and more substantial.
* * ■ *
The paradox which The Bulletin desires to
lay before the Australian public is this—Aus-
tralia can be made rich and prosperous by
heavy taxation, if the proceeds are wisely ap-
plied. (The paradox which every Government
has gone upon hitherto has been that Aus-
tralia could be made rich and prosperous by
being more heavily in debt than any other place
on earth.) The reason why such heavily-taxed
countries as Spain and Italy do not grow rich
is that the proceeds go in armies and navies
and ammunition and foreign war and interest
on foreign debt, instead of in reproductive
works. Undoubtedly a certain strain would be
felt during the first year or two of the new
policy when its effects had not yet had time to
make themselves felt, but Australia is rich
enough to stand the initial effort. There are
vastly poorer countries than this which carry
income-tax ranging up to 4s. in the £, and
tremendous land-taxes, house-taxes, tolls, and
other exactions as well, and where the State
makes revenue out of lotteries and monopolies
and absentee-taxes on top of all. And no effort
at all resembling this would be required, even
in the first year of the new departure. More-
over, the money would remain in the country,
and, as soon as the new policy got started, the
increased employment, increased trade, and in-
creased circulation of wealth would counter-
balance the preliminary trouble.
* * * •
Australia just now is full of pessimists who
talk of the iniquity of fixing an (allegedly) cast-
iron Federal Constitution upon posterity. The
Bulletin rises up as one lone pessimist to say
something about the far worse iniquity of
fixing an absolutely cast-iron interest bill on
posterity, and it asks some of these other pessi-
mists to join it in this lament by way of being
true to their own principles. Australia is also
full of gaudy amateur-warriors, with beautiful
clothes, and swords which have a tendency to
get between their legs, who allege that they are
ready to go out shooting at a foreign enemy in
order to preserve posterity from a foreign yoke,
and they all propose to let future generations toil
in grimy mines and in grimy plough-furrows to
pay for the guns andammunition theyshoot with,
and for the monuments that go on "top of them
when they are shot themselves, and for their
wages while they are shooting, and for the in-
demnity which will have to be paid to the enemy
if he does the more scientific and successful
shoot of the two. And The Bulletin suggests
that it is equally essential to save posterity
from the yoke of the foreign money-lender—
which is much the more certain and probably
the heavier yoke of the two. The readiness of
the modern Majah to die for posterity may be
very patriotic, but his readiness to pay for
himself and not leave his public works bill to
posterity would be a great deal more practical.
Also it would do posterity a lot more good.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Great Papuan Grab.
The completeness and vastness of the great
Papuan land-grab soaks into the public com-
prehension but slowly. It is so complete, and
so vast, and so infamous in its greed, and the
imbecility or dishonesty of the Administration
which sanctioned it are so much beyond the
ordinary run, that it takes some time to
assimilate the matter. The British syndicate
which runs the affair appears to have been
engineered by Somers Vine, the Prince of
Wales’s special fat man, well-known as the
boss fakir of the Imperial Institute. The
syndicate has power to take up 250,000 acres of
land at 2s. an acre. It has eight years to do it
in, and if, at the end of eight years, 250,000
acres have not been thrown open, its monopoly
lasts indefinitely until the required area is
available. For eight years, anyhow, and possi-
bly for 16 or 20 years, it has a prior claim
against all comers. It may pick small and large
areas anywhere, at any time it pleases. It has no
need to hurry. Until it is filled up noone can buy
land off the Government because the syndicate
has an option over everything, and the Govern-
ment can’t sell land to anybody lest the syndi-
cate should want it. Therefore, the syndicate,
though it is only pledged to buy 250,000 acres,
can block settlement all over the province, and
whoever wants to buy must buy from the syn-
dicate at its own price. If anyone attempts to
squat on a valuable piece of land without legal
title, the syndicate can fire him out and take
the property at 2s. an acre. It can pick out
the river frontages, and by cutting off the land
behind them from access to water gradually
freeze out private owners. It is allowed to
take up the freehold of mineral lands, and has
the sole right to the minerals in them, despite
any laws to the contrary—it is expressly pro-
vided that its charter overrides the law.
1 herefore, the awful syndicate has the right to
grab every goldfield, coal-field, or other mineral
deposit which may be discovered. Apparently,
too, it has the right to grab the waters as w’ell
as the land, for it is specially mentioned that
it has the right to “sublet its lands, mines, or
rivers, ’’ &c., &c., so it seems that the private
owner or trader or agriculturist will have to pay
for the privilege of sailing up the syndi-
cate’s river, and may be shut out altogether if
the syndicate pleases. Further, the syndicate
may grab more soil in addition to its 250,000
acres in the shape of a “ land subsidy from the
Government for every adult introduced”—
which, in practice, will probably mean every
adult Chinaman or Indian coolie. And on
top of this there is a provision by
which it may buy land from the natives
(which the private settler isn’t allowed to
oo), so its acreage may easily run into many
millions, and it may ultimately grab all the
good available soil in the province. It is allowed,
as a further addition to all this, to occupy
Grown lands, so that when any private indi-
vidual finds valuable mineral or vegetable pro-
ducts on soil which isn’t open for sale the syn-
dicate can apparently jump these also, either
by leasing the land from the State or buying it
°ff the local nigger And, in addition to
several hundred other things, the syndicate is
permitted to maintain “ land and sea defences,”
so apparently it may run an army and a fleet
of its own ; the insane Papuan Administration
would actually appear to have authorised the
creation of a force of arrried and legalised free-
booters who may overawe the law and the
Government. The three provinces of Queens-
land, N.S. W., and Victoria bear the largest
part of the cost of the Government, which
has thus, in effect, thrown all Australians out
bodily to make room for this great Bull steal.
It is also understood that the Governor of
Queensland has some shadowy authority over
the infuriated Scotch understrapper who runs
British Papua ; and if he has, a pressure of
many tons to the square inch should be brought
to bear on him with a view to squashing this
infamous agreement. If he hasn’t any such
authority, and if the British Government won’t
move in the matter, it is high time to stop the
subsidy. The .£15,000 a year which the Aus-
tralians pay to keep Papua afloat is too high a
price for the privilege of being thrown out by
Vine’s syndicate.

The Federal Capital.
It is getting about time that the anti-Billious
party dropped one A r ery crude and stupid lie
from its list—the one concerning the Federal
capital. It deserves a rest. A few weeks ago
Sydney Daily Telegraph announced, as a
sudden and awful discovery, that the Federal
city was to be in Victoria. Then Hogue,
M. arose, the other day, and flapped down
the bald statement that the capital would be in
Victoria. Premier Reid said that he feared the
capital would be in Victoria. Want roared
that N.S. W. was going to be swindled out of
the capital and that it was going to be in
Victoria. Last week the Telegraph discovered
or invented a violent old sea-captain who, it said,
had been heard to announce in a restaurant that
there was a wicked conspiracy on to defraud
N. W. out of the capital and plant it in Vic-
toria. Yet the short and simple explanation is
—the capital won't be in Victoria. Neither
will it be in Tasmania, South Australia, Wes-
tralia, or New South Wales ; and, if Queensland
and Maoriland join the Federation, it won’t be
in either of them. Nor will it be in the
Northern Territory or Fiji. It is specially
provided by the Convention Bill that the dis-
trict selected for the capital must be separated
from the province which now owns it, and
made into a separate Federal State independent
and outside of all the existing States. The
town and district which are selected as the site
of the capital will, doubtless, gain—but the
province which is selected will lose heavily, for
a considerable slice of territory and, probably,
a good deal of population will be sawn off it.
The N.S. W. anti-Federationist who informs the
people that the Victorians are a greedy and
earth-hungry race with a bitter craving to
annex Riverina, and at the same time that
they want the Federal capital (in other words,
that they are yearning to lavishly give away a
considerable slice of the little territory they
have got) is the kind of liar who ought to be
ashamed of his own inefficiency. The Vic-
torian, being just like anybody else, will almost
certainly vote, when the time comes, to plant
the Federal city and the Federal district
attached to it just on the N.S. W. side of the
Murray—probably the Cornstalk county of
Wentworth or Tara would suit him down to the
ground. Then Victoria would be just as close
alongside the capital as N.S. W. would be. It
would get just as much of the benefit to
be derived by increased trade and increased
population as N.S. W. would do. It would be
just as much benefited in every way as if the
capital were placed on its own side of the pre-
sent border—and the loss of territory would
fall, not on it, but on N.S. W. And S.A. will
almost certainly develope the same hunger to
put the capital just inside the Vic. or N.S.
Wales border. If N.S. W. wants the capital,
and is willing to plant it where the three
provinces meet, it will probably be surprised at
the enthusiasm with which both Victoria and
S.A. will assent. The one thing, however,
which the terms of the Convention Bill make
absolutely certain is that the capital won’t be
in Victoria—nor in N.S.W., Tasmania, S.A.,
Westralia or the Northern Territory. But
if The Bulletin were a follower of the
Cabbage - Tree push, and had a little
more horse-sense than Want and Hogue
and the Daily Telegraph, it would vote for
the selection of Melbourne as the Federal city.
Then that city would be separated from Vic-
toria, and become joint property, N.S. W.
having the biggest share of the ownership. And
Victoria would be left with 700,000 population;
no big city, very little commerce, very little
manufactures, about 19 members in the Federal
Legislature against N.S.W.’s 31, and all its
glory would be departed. But there is no
horse-sense about the firm of Want, Hogue
and co.
Those “ Sacrifices.”
Premier Reid explained at Goulburn last
week that he had really only two serious ob-
jections to the Convention Bill. One was the
fact that it doesn’t fully provide for the rule of
the majority—and Reid was one of the men who
“ burst up ” the rule of the majority in
N.S.W. by supporting the rascally 80,000
minimum vote. Also, the Cornstalk Fat Man
says he is troubled in the vast caverns of his
inside by the feeling “ that this province is
called upon to make much larger sacrifices than
any other province.” But Reid, like every one
else, failed to give the slightest definition of
these alleged “sacrifices.” No one ever does
define them in any coherent fashion. N.S.W.
is to pay the same taxes as the other provinces,
and to get back its own surplus, just like the
other provinces. It keeps its own lands and
railways, like everybody else. It makes prac-
tically the same sacrifice as Victoria, in
giving the smaller provinces a little extra
representation. It gets a little ahead of Vic-
toria in the matter of the Riverina and western
trade, for the reduced rates with which that
province attracts trade from N.S. W., and carries
it long distances at a loss for the mere glory of
the thing, are prohibited, while N.S.W. is
allowed to keep on making reduced rates “ for
the development of her own territory.” Also
N.S.W. has the principal voice in fixing the
capital, and, as the capital means a serious loss
of territory to some State or other, this pro-
bably means that N.S.W. will have the prin-
cipal voice in fixing it just outside its own
borders, so that it will have all the advantages
of being alongside the Federal city without
losing the territory. Altogether these painful
sacrifices are very difficult to define, and all
the shadowy Jeremiahs who lament in a
wilderness of tangled figures make them no
clearer.
The Genus Irritabile Histrionuni.
“As bees bizz out wi’ angry fyke,” so did the
Wilson Barrett mummers break forth with
one accord into clamorous vilification of the
critic who recently ventured to hint in Sydney
Daily Telegraph that, from Barrett’s stodgy
and unmelancholy Dane downwards, the lead-
ing impersonations in his production of “ Ham-
let,” were not quite the last word of histrionic
art. If “ A.N.R.’s ” identity, indeed, had not
been such an open secret, his effusion might
reasonably enough have been taken as a mere
“ draw,” to give Barrett’s subordinates the
chance of a little cheap advertisement of the
kind that Sydney Telegraph has recently been
allowing their principal and one Lewis Scott to
■wallow in. The ill-dissembled viciousness of
Barrett’s reply to “A.N.R.,” however, gives
badly away the cheapnessof the tin-potpunctilio
with which, as the disputants themselves were
never weary of pointingout, was conducted what
Carter Edwards —“douce man! —calls the
“ graceful encounter ” between “Mr. Wilson
Barrett,” haw ! and “Mr. E. Lewis Scott,”
hum ! The real, bulloeky, bludgeony Barrett
now breaks through the skin of the elaborately
courteous Shakespearian dhfquisitionist; and,
fired by the example of their leader, his merry
men rush frantically to the slaughter of the
caitiff “ A.N.R.” with no better weapon than
an angry vituperation. Their bitterness is
in itself pretty good evidence that the foe
has smitten them, one and all, between the
joints of the harness. Also, in perfect key and
keeping with the splenetic femininity of the
male mummers’ protesting screeds, there was
printed with them, from “A Southern Woman,”
an irresistibly funny squeal to the effect, ap-
parently, either that Maud Jeffries, being
another “Southern Woman,” is ipso facto above
all criticism, or that “ A.N.R.” should be forth-
with cowhided for daring to disagree with
Miss Jeffries’ conception of the part of
Ophelia. Cowhided ! just so ! —after all the
doffing and scraping, the mincing measurement
of swords, the courteous controversial carte and
tierce, the casks of grease—out comes the
coarse cowhide of personal abuse, and Barrett
leads the way with it !
“Chas. G. Hkydon,” lawyer, writes persis-
tently to the daily papers asking :
1. If the Commonwealth were to take over any of the
railways of the other provinces is there anything whatever
in the Convention Bill to prevent it from carrying goods
upon them as cheaply as it pleased ?
2. If this were done at a loss to the Commonwealth,
would not N.S.W. have to pay a very large share of such
loss ?
3. If, to protect herself, N.S.W. were to run her own
railways at equally low rates, would she not have to pay
the whole of that loss herself?
4. Under these circumstances, would not N.S.W. be
heavily subsidising rival railways to compete with her
own, and to carry her business to other provinces?
By way of relieving Mr. Heydon’s mind, there
doesn’t seem to be anything to prevent the
above horror—except that S.A. and YVestralia
and Tasmania have no possible interest in
sending trade to Melbourne at great expense
to themselves, and N.S. Wales has far more
voting power than Victoria. An equally-
dreadful circumstance is that there is nothing
to prevent the Commonwealth taking over the
N.S.W. lines and reducing freights so as to
fetch all the Vic. trade to Sydney. And there
is also nothing at all to prevent the Common-
wealth, urged on thereto by N.S.W., from
buying the Vic. lines and tearing them up alto-
gether ; and there is absolutely no clause to
ptovide that the Commonwealth Government
shall not set Sydney on fire, or bolt with the
wives of provincial M’sL.A. And just in case
the Federal Government should decide to take
over the Victorian railways and bestow the
blessing of such alarmingly cheap freights on
the N.S.W. producer that he will grow too
beastly rich altogether, The Bulletin would
be half-inclined to suggest that Federation
should be postponed. Only it has just dis-
covered that there is nothing in the N.S.W.
Constitution to prevent the local Parliament
destroying the N.S.W. railways, and repu-
diating the public debt, and filling Sydney
harbor with sand, and ordering everybody to
eat horse and have his goods carried per bul-
lock-dray along the dirt-road. And, in view of
these dangers, this paper wants the protection
of a Federal Government at once.
According to the Cornstalk provincialists,
New South Wales will be utterly ruined if she
accepts the Convention Constitution ; the
smaller provinces will simply bleed her to
death. The anti-Federationists of the smaller
provinces, meantime, are frightening their
hearers with dismal stories of what is to befall
them if they accept that same Constitution.
Capt. Miles, M.H.A., a Tasmanian provin-
cialist, breaks out thus :
He felt quite satisfied that, if Tasmania went into Fede-
ration under the terms of the present Bill, she would ulti-
mately be landed in a position of financial disaster. . . .
He wished the meeting to particularly observe that Tas-
mania was giving up to the Federal Parliament the valu-
able services and retaining the service by which she was
losing £120,000 a-year. . . . Under this proposal, New
South Wales would get back more than she gave.
The loss to South Australia would be £55,000, West
Australia £515,000, and Tasmania would lose £199,000.
Those were the figures made public, and he challenged
each or all to refute them. ... He feared that, with five
delegates against 25 from New South Wales, Tasmania
would get very little. The Senate might be knocked out
for all the good it would be able to do. It would only be
an endorsing machine. The Federal Government would
be able to fix rates on railways, and Tasmania would be
saddled with a large annual loss. ... It appeared to
him that under the proposed Federal Bill the position of
Tasmania would not be bearable.
w hen Parkes brought forward his Federa-
tion motion in N.S.W. Assembly on Nov. 13,
’94, the ten noes consisted of nine Labor
members and John Haynes; and the spokes-
man for the nine gave three reasons for throw-
ing the motion out. (1) It was proposed (in
the old and now discarded Convention Bill)
that the Senate should be elected by the State
Parliaments. That has been remedied. (2)
ihe Bill was framed by, among others, “the
men who introduced the hellish system of
Kanaka slavery ” to Queensland. No tv, those
men not having helped to frame the present
Bill, the same critics are inviting Australia to
wait a while so that the Kanaka-friends’ assis-
tance may be got again—and a convenient
reason given for once more objecting to the Bill!
To oppose one Bill because a particular party
helped to frame it, and to oppose another Bill
because the same party didn’t help to frame it,
is not the sort of thing one wmuld expect from
honest men. (3) The war-danger argument was
absurd nonsense,” because “we have seen
the last of European wars ; the people will
refuse to fight at the instigation of those who
happen to be in authority.” Since then, Greece
and Turkey have battered one another, and
now Spain is getting gruel from the U.S., so
argument No. 3 falls to pieces without being
hit. Despite all which, the same party expects
the crowd to take its revised anti-Bill argu-
ments quite seriously; and when anybody
su gg es t s > in view of this internal evidence, that
these gentlemen don’t want Federation at all,
they say they are grossly misrepresented, and
that their critic is a deep-dyed crimson traitor.
Before Sydney Telegraph finally dropped the
exploded State-bounties bogey, it got one really
ingenious piece of misrepresentation off its
chest in big capitals :
New South Wales will pay a larger amount of the Vic-
torian Bounties than Victoria herself.
Which is a perfectly true lie, and looks very
terrible in its raw state. The rest of the story
is this : The Co mi mon wealth may grant boun-
ties, with the consent of its Legislature, in
which N.S.W. will have the largest vote. They
will apply to all the States, N.S.W. and Vic-
toria among the rest. As they will come out of
the common funds, N.S.W., having the largest
number of taxpayers will pay more of
the Victorian bounties than Victoria herself.
Also, Victoria- and S.A. and Tasmania
and W. A. will all pay more towards
N.S.W. bounties than towards their own.
Also, N.S.W. and Victoria will pay more
towards the bounties of the three small pro-
vinces than the three small provinces do them-
selves ; and S.A. w r ill pay more towards Tas-
mania’s or W.A.’s bounties than they do them-
selves ; and those States will pay more towards
S. A.’s bounties than towards their own. And it
all boils down to the fact that the State with the
biggest population pays most, and gets most,
and in the end every man pays for himself. It
is on the same basis that Sydney pays more
towards Goulburn’s share of the N.S.W. taxes
than Goulburn pays itself, and that the highly-
respec table Herald pays more towards the
cost of police protection for John Norton’s
Sunday Truth than Truth pays itself—yet
the taxes are levied on the same basis allround.
When the news of the simmering revolution in
Italy came along, the Sydney Telegrafh arose
to point out that it was the heavy Protective
duties which impoverished the people and drove
them to revolt. Next day, however, it thought
better of it, and said that the state of Italy was
owing to the people rushing into a hasty Fede-
ration in ’6l. Italy never did federate at all;
it unified in ’6l, and allowed Victor
Emanuel’s little kingdom to annex the other
Italian states, but that is quite a different
thing. And it is mainly the burden of the
huge £500,000,000 debt and the appalling load
of direct taxation that is driving Italy mad. In
’97, it raised about £10,000,000 in customs,
which is about one-third as much per head as is
levied in Freetrade N.S.W. But the £4,300,000
of land-tax (the Italian land-tax is about twice
as heavy as that of N.S.W.), the £3,500,000 of
house-tax, £11,000,000 income-tax (the Italian
income-tax goes as high as 4s. in the £), also
enormous succession, registration, stamp and
excise duties, the tolls charged at the gates of
towns on all articles brought in for sale (this
direct tax, in the case of corn, has run as high as
2s. 9d. per cwt.), the taxes on manufactures,
and the abuse of the salt monopoly make Italy
about the most awful case of direct taxation on
this earth. These things don’t go to prove
that direct taxation is bad ; they merely prove
that an unfederated monarchy which is suffi-
cient of a fool can make a mess of anything or
everything.
Van Dobson has won his claim for bush fire
damage from Tas. Government, and once more
has the luck of Fat Man proved itself worth
backing. It is interesting to compare two
cases tried at the same sittings of the Court.
A workman sued for compensation for the loss
of an arm in a bark-chopping mill. Verdict for
the employer, presumably on the assumption
that the workman had been guilty of contribu-
tory negligence in not keeping his hand out of
the cogs. In the Dobson case the matter of
contributory negligence wasn’t considered,
though when a man deliberately, and for
pleasure, builds a mansion in the middle of a big
inflammable scrub, and takes no precautions
whatever against a bush fire in the hottest
summer ever known in those parts, contribu-
tory negligence seems to stick out pretty
prominently. The Crown didn’t even raise the
defence that the bush fire was “ the act of
God,” but under the peculiar circumstances no
storm at sea was ever more chargeable to “ the
actof God” than this particular bush fire. In the
one case water is always somewhere about
when the wind rises ; in the other fire is always
somewhere about. In any case, there seems to
be one law' in Tas. for Dobson and the same
sort of law for the influential employer of a
mangled workman. And Van Dobson is respon-
sible for such law.

destroyed the accuracy of the rifle for anything
except the very shortest range. There were
plenty of dum-dum cartridges available, but the
British arsenals are chock-full of the obsolete
ammunition and it had to be used up somehow !

. “ Moths ” went away and died somewhere
in the cold damp shades of wjnter, and that fine
drama, “ The Middleman,” which succeeded it on
Saturday night, roused the first deafening burst
.P”blic approbation that has shaken Sydney
Cri. this season. At the end of the second act,
when Cyrus Blenkarn fired his famous curse after
the fatted oppressor, it might have rained anchors
and wild elephants on the Criterion roof, and
the noise would have passed unheeded. Old
Cyrus, the potter, with his dim eyes, and
far-away soul, and his starts and jumps and
enthusiasms, and his bitter hatreds, and the dirt-
colored tuft on his chin, is the one great character
in Charles Cartwright’s repertoire. Cartwright
is a decent actor, or a good one, or an ordinary
one, or a mediocre one, in a lot of other parts, but
as old Cyrus, with his picturesque vengeance on
Joseph Chandler, he rises to greatness. Joseph
himself is almost equal to the star. Arthur Styan
is a good and versatile actor, and he is a perfectly
different actor in his different parts. His Joseph
Chandler is a good study of the British coumier-
cial and moral bounder, and his whiskers, his
aggressiveness, his chest, his morality, and
his grovel when he is down, are all care-
fully studied. Story Gofton, Charles Thurs'oy,
and the oldest inhabitant are all good, though the
gigantic Gofton threatens to grow wearisome by
reason of being always the same, or very nearly
the same, Gofton.
* * » •
Probably Cartwright’s leading lady is the
only person in the co. who really loses reputation
over the “Middleman.” Miss Beatrice Lamb’s
painful stiffness, and her mode of hanging her
arms like two defunct objects at her sides, passed
at first for restrained force.; now the writer begins
to suspect that it isn’t restrained force —onlv stiff-
ness. Bright Gethyn Darragh is a pleasant char-
acter as the other daughter of Cyrus, the potter,
and Misses Hope Mayne and Noni Rickards do
their little parts successfully. So do most of the
P 60 ?! 6 - There is a big moral in the
Middleman,” and the said moral is that it is un-
wise to jump on the worm when he is down unless
it is absolutely certain that the animal is extinct.
Joseph Chandler is the man who bounds on the
worm once too often. He buys old Cyrus’s dis-
covery for a song, and grows rich and bloated on
it, and keeps Cyrus on as a workman at a starva-
tion wage, and still the unworldly old potter loves
and reverences his swollen employer. But when
Chandler, junior, seduces the potter’s daughter,
and the father, in the bloated insolence of his top-
hat, refuses redress, the maddened potter wades
in at new.in ventions which enable him to under-
mine the inflated boss’s trade, and ruin him, and
buy him up, and cast him into the streets. He
relents at the last, and gives the humbled bounder
a billet, and finds that his wrath was partly based
on misapprehension—but, all the same, the story is
a solemn warning that the worm which is stood on
all the time may burst in the end. And the
bursting of this particular worm is a very pictur-
esque episode.

A good deal of hard service has worn “ the
Shamrock and the Rose ” a trifle threadbare at the
elbows and a little baggy at the kneps. Still it is
better than the average run of Irish dramas, and
the present revival at Sydney Lyceum gives the
tale a very good show. The stage Ireland is a
place where the whole population is incessantly
being evicted for non-payment of rent, and seeing
that nobody pays any rent, and somebody must
pay the process-server, and the informer, and the
man who throws out Doolan and harries Mulligan,
the landlord s means of support are a mystery.
After a while, however, the Lyceum drama de-
parts from the ordinary shriekful track and goes
out upon a gaudy burst on its own. The flinty
agent is about bolting for America with <£15,000
of embezzled rent, when Mulligan comes in
stealthily at the window and demands backsheesh
as the price of his silence. Mulligan is afterwards
counting his plunder on the edge of a tall cliff in
the moonlight when the agent’s wicked son grabs
at him and heaves him over. The son does
this because he mistakes the back view of Mulli-
gan for that of his own father, and Mulligan is
such an awful wreck at the bottom of the cliff
that he might be anybody. But the crime is
witnessed by Mulcahy, who blackmails the miser-
able son just as Mulligan did the father ; and the
son is also pursued by Dandy, the adopted son of
Bridget, who wants to strangle him because he
seduced Morna ; and the son’s attempt, in which
he is abetted by Mulcahy, to lay the crime on
Desmond, fails miserably. All these things and
many more arise out of the awful death of Mulli-
gan. Altogether a very well-constructed plot is
built on Mulligan’s remains, and the drama goes
strongly all through and ends with a loud bang,
and it isn’t more illogical, even in its worst places,
than the average.
• * * •
With the exception of Desmond, who is really
painful, and Morna, who is loud and harrowing as
she batters at the door of old Flint and calls on
young Flint to come out and make her an honest
woman at once on the public highway, and one or
two very small people who don’t matter, the co. is
remarkably free from " eak points. C. R. Stanford
?®r * piece of wildly-eccentric comedy, and as
Mulcahy, the process-server and evictor, and the
person who puts his red nose to bogus affidavits,
he is,a credit to all parties concerned. J. H.
iS unn s red and bitter Scotch agent, and Diver,
who represents the son of that pleasing person,
are both excellent, though Diver’s tendency to
look rather too much like himself and too little
bke somebody else grows rather conspicuous.
Harry Shine, as Dandy, and Laurence Dunbar
and Miss Iherese Lindahl, and Mulligan and
Jones, and one or two other people, are all viewed
with favor by this influential organ. The
moonlight sea, in which Mulligan meets his doom,
is a restful work of art, though a much cheaper
tomb would be quite good enough for Mulligan.

r r a r>i Sln n^ n ? c °l° r - The painted vestibule
of Phyllis s music-hall and the red carpet therein
strike the audience in the eye as if Rubens had
been thrown at it. The garish hall of sin and
wine, wherein Norah abjures her old bad life and
aims fizz at her betrayer, is a staggering* glare of
light, and is alive with life and movement. Then,
when Bland Holt invades Burmah he does it with
a whole real army behind him—in fact, the entire
drama is lavishly staged and dressed, and is put
on with the copious finish which is B. Holt’s
strongest point. In any case a melodrama which
might look second-rate in the hands of an in-
ferior person often looks well when Holt does it.
I hat wily manager diagnoses the weak points and
spreads expensive Brussels carpet over them, or he
diverts attention from the bad places with a large
stained-glass window or a cataract. The Bulle-
tins and everybody else’s old friend, Chris.
Simonsen, is again in front at the Royal. Simon-
sen quitted management some time ago, and went
into some kind of business connected with horse,
but he discovered that there was a void in his
existence when he was away from “ the front,’’ so
he returned to the old life, and the business that
was connected with horse gets on as best it can
without him.
** * *
Wilson Barrett s season at Her Majesty’s,
according to the arrangements existing at time of
writing, closes to-night (Thursday). “Othello,”
put on for a very few nights, boomed as that
influential brown murderer lias not boomed in
oyuney for a long while, and in between times the
infuriated Moor and his co. and supporters wrote
bitter and undignified letters to the Telegraph
about barrister Nugent Robertson, who hadn’t
admired the co. quite so much as the co. admired
itself. It was a joyful row, and the language
of the combustible co. was loud and fish-like ;
but it helped to advertise the last nights of the
season considerably. And in the opinion of many
people who are not so Very Tired of Shakespeare
as the present scribe, “ Othello ” was an artistic
success, quite apart from any success achieved by
theco. in crying out for the scalp of Robertson.
1 wo performances of “ Virginius” wind up the sea-
son, so that mournful Roman tale has at last
ma,naged to present itself. On Saturday the
Sign of the Cross,’’with Julius Knight and all
the previous co. on deck, resumes business at the
old stand. Considering that there was still a
great and unsatisfied demand for the Cross, and
the gibbering Caesar, and the lion that ate the little
Christian when the previous season came toan end,
the revival should fill a want as large as the
N.S. W. public debt, or even a little larger.

A great event, the re-appearance of Geo.
Wood, the somewhat different comedian, is due
at Sydney Tivoli this Saturday. Miss Marion
Ainsworth, of pleasant memory, returns with
him, and as the Hegehnanns and the Tiller Quar-
tette and Miss Lilian Tree are still at the Tivoli,
the bill for next week is expected to be something
tremendous.

Henry’s Co., at Sydney Royal Standard,
is doing the “ Relief of Lucknow ’’ this week about
as Avell as the size of the premises admits—
or else it intends doing that piece, or has
recently done it, or something. Space is limited
at the Standard, and the rush of the bare-
legged Caledonians is done under difficulties,
but they arrive there with much pibroch about II
p.m., and tilings might be a good deal worse.
Still, the management’s statement that the drama
is staged with “Unprecedented Magnificence”
must be taken with condiment. Even if the co.
stole its furniture it is impossible to have the
Magnificence quite Unprecedented for 3d.

The Kellino Family and other matters
mentioned last week are flourishing at the Opera
House, and the Saturday night rush to Rickards’
snow is rushful above the average. Next Satur-
day (21st) three newly-imported turns will be
turned on with a considerable splash. Meanwhile
Bellman Lottie are going away,
and the tinkling feet of the musical clowns will
shortly follow suit.
** * *
The third of Newbury’s Shilling “ Pops ”
fetched another mighty crowd to Melbourne Town
Hall on Saturday night, when Ernest Wood
played the organ, and Falk’s capital Biograph put
an assortment of views before the public, and the
vocal concert, assisted by Hattenbach and Seherek,
had to endure several encores. The singers sup-
porting Phil Newbury are good, as a rule, and the
performances of that attractive tenor on Saturday
were positively great.

Prof. Bristol and his family party of horses
likewise the comic mule and two or three hums ’
n
acrobats, cropped up at St. George’s Hall on
Saturday. The Bulletin regards Bristol’s horses
with a favorable eye, but finds two hours of them
a somewhat heavy experience ; and the world
hereabouts sepnis to share this lack of enthusiasm.
The placid Prof, had a limited audience to talk at
on Saturday, so, unless a strong demand for tame
horse should set in suddenly, the season will not
be longer than was expected.
* * *
Miss Myra Kemble has arranged to re-open
the Bijou on Saturday with “ Dr. Bill,” the riotous
comedy which she brought from Hingland seven
years ago. “Dr. Bill” and Miss Myra K. are
affectionately remembered in Melbourne, and the
revival at popular prices is likely to get at least a
fair start. Another show billed for Saturday, day
and night, is the Old Colonists’ Carnival, at
the Exhibition Building. This vast collec-
tion of stalls, bicycle races, spectacular
ballet, music, and conviviality in general, will
run for a week. Melbourne is getting about as
much entertainment as it can carry just now.
Marshall Hall will give another orchestral concert
next week; Allan’s Chamber Music Concerts
were to start yesterday (Wednesday) at the
Athenaeum Hall ; and the rival firm of Glen and
Co. is putting some more Elsie Hall recitals on
the market. The Glen-Allan antagonism is a
sweet and pleasant thing and worthy of encourage-
ment.
** * *
“Two Little Vagabonds,” at Melbourne
Royal, is mostly better than the first production
at the Princess’s, and partly not so good. Any-
way, the lower scale of prices in this case makes
it a more deserving show. “ Two Little Vaga-
bonds” is a rivulet of natural pathos meander-
ing through a broad plot of melodramatic non-
sense, and the success or otherwise of the piece
depends mainly upon the two smudgy ladies who
disguise themselves in the name-parts. If they
can hit the feelings of their audience the circum-
stances that lead up to them and revolve around
them afterwards are not necessarily fatal. Miss
Florence Terriss, as the wan boy with a korff on
his chest, seems a fairly real object for sym-
pathy, whilst Miss Lily Titheradge is a brighter,
younger, more attractive little Dick than her pre-
decessor. On the other hand, she hasn’t the
emotional power of the previous Dick, and she
doesn’t lay sufficient emphasis on small points in
the dialogue. The scene where Wally interposes
between his mate and his muvver was an affecting
bit at the Princess's ; here it is less affecting, and,
seeing that wo have the same consumptive Wally,
the falling-off in the situation must be due to the
new Dick and other changes in the cast. Perhaps
Miss Hughes is somewhat hard at this tender
moment, also Mrs. Maesmore Morris, who has to
hover round and say something, is too casual in
her hover and her remarks. But maybe the com-
pany felt tired on Saturday night.
* * • •
“Two Little Vagabonds ” is now played
with a due sense of proportion, which wasn’t
the ease at the Princess’s. Miss Hughes and
Titheradge pitch their grief and woe in a milder
and more domestic key than Miss Ferrar and
Julius Knight adopted, and the foolish portions of
the yarn are less irritating. Mrs. Morris, looking
prettier than ever, is a nice, unobtrusive young
aunt, and several low-comedy people—Sweeney,
Oily Deering, Miss Julia Merton, and a policeman
not mentioned on the bill—give the rough humor
of the piece a much better chance than it got
aforetime. Sweeney and Deering may be said to
revel in the characters that Gaston Mervale and
Caleb Porter didn’t appear to so fully understand.
The company brought W. and M. scenery with
them, and will probably make the “ Vagabonds ”
hum for the allotted fortnight.

Napoleon and Josephine, at the Princess’s,
have been keeping their end up satisfactorily,
although public opinion rc historical tableaux
plays is bound to grow cooler on reflection. Nap.,
posturing in the middle of the Battle of Waterloo,
or at the head of the Retreat from Moscow, is one
of those impressive effects which don’t last long.
Wilson Barrett returns to the Princess’s on Satur-
day, and will rush through a series of revivals.
Two nights each of, firstly, “The Manxman,”and,
secondly, the sorrowful Roman potentate who is
doomed to live on and on, whilst his autocratic
legs never knock-under to rheumatism, will bring
the season level with next issue of this irreverent
newspaper. The noble Roman, by-the-bye, will
add a matinee on Queen’s Birthday.
** * *
The old question re the genuineness of
Kennedy’s lamentable platform outrages was
lately resurrected in Melb. ’Erald by somebody
who offers to let himself be punctured in divers
places with six-inch pins. The scepticism that
refuses to take Kennedy seriously in any of his
misdeeds is a very “ fly-flat ” unbelief. Medical
men have satisfied themselves that the red-headed
Professor is not an impostor. Some of the wretched
individuals he uses are undoubtedly under his
mesmeric influence, and how he got them there is
not the point to consider. Mesmerists’ shows,
taken in the lump, are made up of humbug, per-
haps—i.e., of humbug to a certain extent. The
mummers are usually hired and rehearsed
in their songs and so forth. Neverthe-
less, the presence of the mesmerist is essential
to their fooling, or to most of it. If any specula-
tive unbeliever supposes that he can engage a batch
of professional subjects, at increased salaries, to
do the things for him that they did for the mes-
merist—well, let the speculator try it. The
scheme has-been tried, and was always a dismal
failure. Even among practised hypnotists a
4 swapping ” of subjects is seldom a satisfactory
deal. Kennedys differ in their precious influences,
and the epileptic creature who submits to one
travelling Professor will probably prove insub-
ordinate when handed over to another member of
the “ You’re all right ” fraternity.
** * *
Compared with the taffy served out to
Albani, the hard, unyielding criticism that Phil
Newbury receives from the Melbourne dailies is
palpably unfair. A gallery seat at an Albani
cost times as much as the same seat at one of
Newbury’s Saturday “ Pops,” but whereas the
well-nigh exhausted soprano got nothing but praise
for breathing like a grampus and proving herself
incapable of now singing certain ballads up to the
best local standard, Newbury is told that he
“ makes the judicious grieve,” or, his “ intonation
was very different from what he has accustomed
the public to expect from him.” Did the public
ever get what they had expected from the seven-
and-sixpenny Albani ?
** # *
In his latest importation—Durward Lely,
the Scottish entertainer —R. S. Smythe seems to
have struck real oil. The specialty of the native
of Arbroath is averred to be “Scottish song and
humor,” but, having been principal tenor with
Mapleson, Carl Rosa, and D’Oyly Carte, he has a
wide repertoire, and gathers ’em in from all quar-
ters. He apparently scortis the stoall towns, and
only appears in big halls—in Dunedin, Christ-
church (whete he is at present), Wellington, and
Auckland. Also, he is announced as “ the only
great vocalist who has visited the colonies while
in the zenith of his powers.” Which is fine and
large and Smvthian. A Bulletin man now in
Maoriland writes of Lely as “a hard-featured
Scot who evidently made his escape from the
‘bonny hills’ at a verra tender age. His accent
is in bad repair, and, if put into bankruptcy,
would only pay about a ‘fink.’ However, his
Scottish intonation is in evidence, for nothing but
death can remove that. He has the well-pre-
served remains of a tenor voice, the equal of
which has never been heard in Australia. It has
four distinct qualities as strongly marked as
organ-stops : a beautiful low chest tone, a
mezzo voce like the upper notes of a
‘ cello,’ a lovely falsetto, sweeter than
that of a boy-angel, and last and bpst, a top-note
like the blast of a silver trumpet, which frequently
aroused the enthusiasm even of an audience largely
composed of bald-headed, monkey-shaved Scots.
As a finale, he dances a highland fling, apologises
for the unseemly display, says he was carried awa
by the music—then takes his ‘ encore ’ the same
as the rest of us. Mrs. Lely is the only other
member of the show. She is a beautiful matron,
who plays most sympathetic . accompaniments,
though it would take a small omnibus to carry all
the medals she would win as a soloist. In addi-
tion, she is a really splendid actress ; she listens
with apparent rapt attention to Tonal’— ho, Tur-
ward ; her fine eyes shining, and a sweet, budding
smile is always ready to blossom into a laugh every
time Turward’s chestnuts are ripe;
