Premier Reid said, at Goulburri, the othet
day, that if eight of the G 4 members of the Federal
Lower House were away (or ill, &c.) on a sitting
of the joint Houses, the whole of the rest Of the
Lower House could hot prevail against the Senate,
This is rot. If eight Representatives are absent
from a joint Hotis'e of 04, there will be 86 left, and
three-fifths of 86 is 52-not 57, as G. H. Reid
assumes it to be. And the Representatives, eveh
eight absent, will muster 56. Section 57 provides for
a majority of three-fifths of the members present
and voting. If oh some vital issue affecting
State rights, the small States are absolutely solid
against N.S.W. and Victoria, and six members
are absent from the combined vote of 59 of the
larger States, and all the small States men are
present, the larger States, having a vote of
53 out of a joint House of 88, will still command
the requisite three-fifths majority of the members
present and voting. The working majority-
of the large States in the joint House is,
therefore, not two, but six, and this
even allowing for the absurd assumption that
all bodily infirmities will be found amongst the
representatives of the large states. There is suf-
ficient mental infirmity amongst some of the anti*-
Billites already, without their claiming all bodily
infirmities too.
The indignation of Sydney Sunday Times :
Pray, what chance has N.S.W. of legislating in favor of
the bounties before June 30, if she were so disposed? It
being admitted, as it must be, that she has no chance,
how can it. be held that it is possible for her to retaliate
against Victoria, which is allowed by the Federal Consti-
tution to continue bounties already in existence? Tub
Bulletin bluffs, blusters, and blunders in the custotnary
way. In this instance the quotations from the Bill merely
serve to show the fallacy of its argument, and ihdeed its
utter stupidity.
The Sunday tipster asks a very easy question.
Victoria has five bounties in force, ten having
lapsed or been abolished. They are for the growth
and cultivation of forest-trees, for a vegetable-oil
factory, for encouraging the growth of flax and
hemp, for the export of green fruit, and for the
establishment of wineries. There are no sugar or
coal bounties, as is commonly supposed. The
above five will also lapse very shortly (four of
them probably within a year) for want of funds,
as all the money voted is already spent, except
about £BOOO, so before the Federal tariff is at all
likely to get started they will be gone and out
of the road. The only way Victoria can create
any new bounties, or extend the old ones so
that they will in any way affect the Common-
wealth, is by passing the necessary bill through
Parliament before 30th June. The N.S.W. Par-
liament meets about the same time as Victoria,
and, if it wants to create any bounties —say to
carry out Premier Reid’s favorite idea of
creating a local iron industry by artificial assist-
ance—it can also shove the necessary bill through.
That is all there is about it. The only chance
that any province has of creating bounties that
are likely to be in existence when the Common-
wealth begins is by “ legislating before June 30.”
And if N.S.W. doesn’t know the way to legislate
before June 30 as well as her scoundrelly neigh-
bours, what is the matter with her?

The WILDCAT
COLUMN
As regards the fall in Westralia’s revenue, the
probability is that it will go on with increasing rapidity
now that it is started, till the annual receipts get down
from the £2,842,000, which was last year’s total, to about
£1,500,000, or so. Last year’s revenue of the various pro-
vinces was as follows :
Tasmania .. .. £4 17s. Bd. per inhabitant.
Victoria .. .. 5 12s. lOd. „
Maoriland .. .. 6 15s. Id.
N.S.W. .. .. 7 3s. 6d. „
South'Australia .. 7 9s. lOd. „
Queensland.. .. 7 13s. Id. „
Westralia .. .. 20 12s. Bd. „
Apart from land-sale income, the six reasonably sober
provinces show a comparatively small variation. There
is nothing special about Westralia’s intelligence or enter-
prise to keep it so very much beyond the average, and
when its boom simmers down the revenue will probably
drop gently to not over £8 per head. And even if it only
falls to £lO that means the biggest collapse Australia has
ever seen.
* • • •
Mayor M'Eacharn’s scheme, by which Melb.
City Council is to buy up the three local electric-lighting
companies, and run the whole electric supply of Melbourne
and suburbs, should pay all parties concerned. Melb.
Council already runs a small electric supply, and furnishes
light, with a good profit to the ratepayers, about 17 per
cent, cheaper than the private companies. When it con-
solidates the business, and thus reduces the working
expenses, it will probably be able to sell 20 or 25
per cent, below the old private co’s price, and
yet have a handsome surplus to go towards sinking
fund. Melb. (if the M’Eacharn scheme really comes
off) will have done wisely by taking over the business before
the private monopolies get too large and the vested in-
terests too deeply-rooted. There is much money in the
electric lighting biz. London is illuminated by nine pri-
vate companies, whose dividends in ’97 ranged from 4 to
141 per cent. Every one of these dividends showed a
large increase over ’96, and it looks as if, in a very few
years, the gorgeous Cockney monopolies will be sweating
from 15 to 40 per cent, out of the householders. Even
now it would take a stupendous sum over and above their
original capital to buy them out, and London has practi-
cally thrown away its opportunity by waiting till the
private syndicate grab has grown up, so to speak.

Maoriland railways, for the year ended 31st
March, IS9B, paid £3 4s. lOd. percent, on capital, against
£3 3s. lOd. in 1597, £2 16s. in 1896, and £2 14s. 6d. in 1895.
The province which is “ ruined ” by Socialistic government
still keeps moving steadily to the hunt.

Burns, Pliilp and Co. Limited, the concern
which owns North Queensland, and is commonly mistaken
for God in the Far North, closes its 16th year with a net
profit of £48,289, and £10,849 balance from last year
makes £59,138 available. An 8 per cent, dividend (£21,400)
isdeclared; ±5OOO allowed for depreciation ; £25,000 added
to reserve, making it £125,000 ; and £7738 carried forward.
The dividends of 8., P. and Co, have ranged from 71 to 12
per cent., so it has been a cjnsistently profitable con ern
for its shareholders since it first started as a limited co. in
’B3.
* * * •
The Co-operative Wool and Produce Co. (Syd-
ney), which was established by way of a protest against
the rapacity of the old wool-broking firms and their
hunger after big percentages, completed its first year on
28th February. It did a very respectable business for a
new institution, and on a capital of only £843 the year’s
profits were £1077, or about 127 per cent. This profit is
partly made up by treating £172 of the preliminary ex-
penses as an asset—if all the preliminary ex.’s had been
written off the profits would only have been 107 per cent-
Why a concern which is doing so wonderfully well should
keep a purely paper asset in its balance-sheet, thereby
making it look as if it was doing a little better still,
passes comprehension. The Co-operative Wool Co. pays
its shareholders only 6 per cent, (about £5l) and the rest
of its profits, apart from amounts which may be put to
reserve, is to be divided among the people who consign
their wool and hides and bones for sale, shareholders
getting twice as large a bonus as non-shareholders. When
the consignors fully realise the advantage of sharing in the
profits the Co-operative co. ought to grow rapidly.
* * * *
The Swan Brewery Co., Perth, had a profit of
£24,048 for the half-year ended 31st March. After writing
£5OOO off its leaseholds it pays a dividend at the rate of
34 and a fraction per cent. (£7609), and the balance, with
amount previously on hand, makes £33,826 at credit, of
profit and loss. Also, there is a reserve of £20,000, so
in its very brief career the Swan Brewery, with a paid-up
capital of only £44,384, has accumulated ±53,826. This
achievement, on top of the 34 per cent, dividends, makes
the Melbourne shareholders feel almost as if the boom had
come back again. _ The Swan co, proposes to make a loud
sensation by paying an extra dividend of 71 per cent,
shortly, in addition to the regular half-yearly payment.
* » • *
Victorian Credit Foncier is unpopular with
the banks because it interferes with their monopoly of
cheap money on fixed deposit, and also their up-country
farmers’ overdraft business—secured by mortgage, on
which they exacted any rate up to 15 per cent., the bor-
rowers being unable to check the rate, as it is charged on
daily balance. Solicitors also decry the C.F. because it
puts no fees into their pockets, whereas under ordinary
mortgage they get a big mortgage fee, a share of the
commission, and probably a cut out of the valuation fee,
as well as fees for preparing release when the loan is re-
paid. The professional trustee, who generally combines
the occupation with that of “ guinea pig,” also misses his
share of the spoil ; whilst the financial agent is out in the
cold altogether, as the Savings Bank and the borrower
come into direct contact. So the whole pack of
wolves is howling against this form of security. The
security, however, is certain, sooner or later, to be appre-
ciated by the smaller capitalists, whom reconstruction has
hit so heavily.

A city grocer recently scooped a big ad. (and
apparently a cheap job) by getting up a competition to pack
1501 b. of tea in the shortest time ; prizes, 305., 205., 10s.
Carried out in a front window—crowd looking on. There
were 20 competitors, and the grocer, thus got 30001 b. of
tea packed in lbs. and ilbs. for £3—or less than Jd. a lb.
Which recalls the average newspaper-literary-competition
business.

Grif : Publication of writs and summonses
issued by the Supreme, County, or any other Court should
be prohibited, as it is used to compel unfair settlements.
Ikey Usurer Mo, when he has overdue paper of a small
trader who squirms at the proposed rate for renewal,
shrugs his shoulders and says, “ Veil, I must issue a writ,
and you’ll be in the Circular." This compels compliance.
• * • *
Government, by making it compulsory to ad-
vertise in the Gazette all mortgages, bills of sale, liens,
letting and hiring agreements, &c., and publishing the
judgments recorded in all courts, could make it a paying
venture. If you raise the wind on your household sticks,
it is announced in all trade-protection circulars, but if you
mortgage your land it remains a secret; and, as one only
pawns his sticks as a last resort, this system savors of the
parting kick to all credit. With a Gazette, as suggested,
the transactions would be equally public. Certainly it
would interfere with that bogey, “ private enterprise,”
always trotted out when any reform is suggested, but, as
the average trade-protection society savors to an extent
of black-mail, no sorrow would arise if the genus became
extinct.

Some M'Sharry (N.S.W. railway) arbitration
statistics Evidence for plaintiff occupied 143 days, and
for defendant 92 days. The evidence covers 12,000 letter-
pages of typewriting. Plaintiff’s counsel, opening, occu-
pied five days, and in replying 42 days. Defendant’s
counsel spoke for 36 days. Plaintiff spent 53 days in the
box, and other witnesses two and three weeks. So far
the Crown has paid away £30,000, and the total Crown
costs are likely to be double that. The costs to M'Sharry
are likely to be about the same. The umpire, Mr. Barton,
has received £BOOO, and Crown counsel, Mr. Bruce Smith,
a similar amount.
» * • *
H.F. : The city man out of collar, and unfitted
for manual labor, can do worse than go into the bee busi-
ness. The present price of honey, 2d. a pound—sugar
value—is probably bed-rock. Return 10s. to £1 a hive per
year, according to season and location. Important that
neophyte get previous instruction in established apiary,
and obtain expert advice in selecting his locale. This is
one of the best honey countries in the world, and it is
unfortunate that there is no payable export market. But
the low price has enormously increased local consump-
tion, and the limit in this direction is yet afar off.
* * * *
A Bulletin correspondent: I am authorita-
tively informed that the will of Crotty, the gilded North
Lyell man, provides for One hundred a year to the widow
—the R.C. Church being the residuary legatee. Crotty’s
wife struggled with him through the bitter bad times.
When the boom came she went with him to London and
enjoyed a very short period of magnificence, and now that
Crotty is dead she is forced to go back to the good old
struggle on 40s. a week. Provided always though that if
she will only enter a convent she is to get £6OO a year—a
very left-handed sort of bequest. If this information
be correct then Mr. Crotty was the sort of person who
should have died much earlier, and I would like to ask
Archbishop Carr if he intends taking all the Crotty thou-
sands and leaving the widow—who is morally entitled to
be regarded as a partner in the huge estate—her miser-
able two sovs. a week ? I hope also, in the interests of
people who are ballasted with tons of rotten Lyell-district
scrip, that the Archbishop won’t unload Crotty’s over-
boomed paper with any rash haste. For if (and this is
where I come in) the executors attempt any quick realisa-
tion a great deal of the Crotty £150,000 will vanish into
thin air, and St. Patrick’s will continue without its tower,
steeple, or something.

B. : An unpublished incident of the Elliott-
Lee atmospheric gas swindle. Before the flotation of the
syndicate I was introduced to Elliott and Lee, and only
refused to take shares because I didn’t understand gas—
that kind of gas, anyway. A Westralian friend of mine
was with me at the tests. He is an enquiring sort of man
and takes a lot of trouble to find out things that he will
never have any interest in—sort of man who would put
his head in a lion’s mouth to see if the lion had tonsilitis.
Well, he made a second trip—alone this time —to the
swindle-factory in Elizabeth-street, and he took with him
a small phial, close-stoppered and filled with cotton-wool.
When he thought no one was looking he turned a tap on
and held the phial over the escaping gas, re-stoppered the
phial, turned the tap off, and started for an analyst’s.
Elliott captured him and detained him till two large
policemen arrived, and my enquiring friend was forced to
hand over the phial and express his gratitude at escaping
arrest on a charge of stealing gas. That was sufficient to
cover the whole scheme with suspicion, hut I did nothing
when he told me of his adventure. Had I published it
Jake Garrard might have escaped his explosion, and Jaw-
bone Neild would have been a few hundreds poorer.

S-A- Insolvency Commissioner Russell has been
speaking out re the present state of the Insolvency law
and the facilities afforded lawyers to fatten on the carcases
of big estates. In the Harrold estate, which is still in the
hands of the lawyers, the legal costs alone amounted to
over £lO,OOO some weeks ago.
• * * •
The importance of the banana industry to N.Q.
can easily he gauged by folks if they recollect that in the
Cairns district alone 2400 Chinese make a living, and that
two-thirds of these subsist by raising bananas. Just now,
in London, there’s a perfect rage for banana-biscuits and
banana-flour, and Queensland hasn’t the nous to derive
one penny of benefit therefrom. Waiting for the Yanks
to step in.
* * * *
The flotation of the business of Lipton, of tea
fame, was most sensational. The capital was fixed at
£2,500,000, and twenty times that amount was subscribed
when the lists were opened, Glasgow alone offering to
supply the capital twice over. Extraordinary scenes
were witnessed at the Bank of Scotland, the police having
to regulate the crowd. The “ tip ” was thought a good
one, and the craze extended to the West End, where titled
ladies, officers, and public men besieged Lipton, implor-
ing him to take their money. The scrip, at latest, was
quoted at a big premium.
* . • *
The only notable recent market movements in
N.S.W. gold-mining are the heavy fall in Overflows con-
sequent on the failure up-to-date of cyanide operations
(slime difficulties having proved too tough), and the spurt
and subsequent drop in Mt. Drysdales. The latter co.
struck what it at first thought to be a replica of the rich
vein which, yearsago,paid -40,000 in dividends, butitgave
out and scrip went back to about the old figures. And how
quiet the find was kept till all the scrip available in
Sydney had been snapped up! As to the Overflow, it is
approaching the end of its available capital, and those
concerned now probably see how grave a mistake it was
not to open up the mine extensively in the first place and
see what it was stuffed with, instead of making all sorts of
costly experiments in plant before it was known what the
ores were like.

Randolph Bedford (no more, he insists, Ran-
dolph the Reckless) is painting his name on the map of
the Chillagoe district—that North Queensland copper,
silver, and lead mining country upon which General-
manager Stewart, of Bill., has just reported. Already
there is a Bedford district, a Randolph claim, and a
Chillagoe-Bedford mining and railway syndicate.
• * * »
The Bulletin is told that the biggest mine of
the 30 or more owned by the Chillagoe proprietary is the
Ruddigore, situated on a succession of low hills about
four miles north-west of Calcifer, the easterly township
of Chillagoe. Three cuttings are down—one 50ft. deep
by about 90ft. dia., and from the bottom of the cutting a
shaft is down 40ft., cutting the sulphides sft. above the
bottom. 10,000 tons of allegedly 9 per cent, ore are pad-
docked, and a costeen of a maximum depth of 30ft., put
in to lead the hill rains away from the big open cut, shows
the deposit to be over two chains wide. It is proved for
a length of nearly 10 chains. Also, it stops as abruptly as
it started, and the position-blocks pegged on to it have as
big a chance of striking the Suez Canal as ore.
Chillagoe (The Bulletin’s correspondent goes
on to say) will be no 4 per cent. Mt. Lyell scheme, and
there is not a Lake George sort of mine in any of
the proprietary’s holding. The North Australian at
Watsonville, a privately-owned mine, has been for years
sending copper ore to London at continuous profit. Pack-
ing has on occasions run into ±'9 a ton, besides freight to
the old country, smelting and realisation charges, and
yet the profits have averaged £6 a ton. The North
Queensland copper country is big and rich.
It is probable (continues the writer) that John
Moffatt’s Mt. Garnet mine, south of Chillagoe, will be
offered for flotation shortly. The Mt. Garnet is a very
rich thing, and as big as it is rich. A tunnel put in
at a point giving 82ft. of backs struck the lode on the
footwall side at 111 ft., and is now into the lode 60ft. with-
out any sign of wall. For all this distance the stuff is
highly payable and not a blank in it. A winze is down
54ft. from the tunnel floor and has just cut the line of the
sulphides. s.

Ed. Bulletin. — Re a recent paragraph. The
Chillagoe Proprietary Ltd. mines are now in a much
higher stage of development than Broken Hill was when
the original syndicate was selling _ a 14th at £lOOO.
Broken Hill had three pups—the British, Block 10 and
Block 14 —and, with bonuses, &c., the 14th reached
£‘600,000 apiece. Chillagoe Proprietary has 2000 a. in 35
properties—every one a mine, many with large develop-
ments—and its 480th is selling to-day at £l7OO or so.
There will be a separate co. for the railway scheme, one
for the smelters, and, for the purposes of easier manage-
ment, the 2000 a. is likely to be split into 10 pups. Now,
I tip those 480th shares to touch £40,000 each. Tall talk
certainly,* but the tongue was put in the cheek when
Broken Hill 14ths were tipped—on less evidence than the
Chillagoe Proprietary has — to see £5OOO. — Yours, Ran-
dolph Bedford. [R.B. has apparently got ’em bad.]

El. Bulletin:— Your Mount Morgan figures
(23'4/98) need explanation. For the years mentioned (’B7,
’BB, ’B9), the gold won was comparatively free. Since that
time, up till and including last half-year, the rich ore has
needed special treatment, and much slower treatment at
that. For last year the mundic ore, over 17,000 tons,
averaged a little over 4'oz. to the ton. During same term
low grades of comparatively easy treatment (over 70,000
tons) averaged slightly under loz. If the total cost of
treatment is only 4dwt. per ton (as is stated that the
management boasts) then the co. produces some 14,5000 z.
gold, or, say, £60,000 worth per month, at a cost of £lO,OOO.
Now the monthly div. accounts for £25,000, which leaves
£25,000 unaccounted for. The actual case is that the cost
of treating low-grade ores at the West Works is under
4dwt. per ton, including everything. These West Works
are now in full swing and are treating about 3600 tons
per week, or at least 14,000 tons per month of low-grade
ores. In the lower levels extremely rich mundic ores are
occasionally met with —one such dab was met with during
the last fortnight.—D.

* • * »
The reconstructed (English) Kalgoorlie Consols
co. is a fine, comprehensive affair. According to its
articles of incorporation the new co. has power to trans-
act mining business of every description in any part of the
world, and power also to carry on the business of a min-
ing, smelting, and trading co. in all its branches; to lay
out towns and villages, and to construct, maintain, and
work rail and tram roads, wharves, docks, piers, &c. ;
a'so, to act as hotel and tavern-keepers, ice merchants,
provision dealers, livery-stable proprietors, refreshment
contractors, licensed victuallers, brewers, manufacturers
of and dealers in aerated and mineral waters ; as tobacco
and cigar manufacturers and dealers, farmers, &c. ; also,
as hairdressers, perfumers, proprietors of clubs, baths,
&c. And the &c., is so comprehensive that almost the
only businesses the Kalgoorlie Consols Co. is not author-
ised to undertake are those of corn-and-wart extractor,
itinerant prawn-merchant, and chucker-out.

Average ore-yields last year :— Westralia, loz.
lOdwt. 14gr. ; Queensland, loz. 3dwt. lOgr. : N.S.W.,
17dwt. 20gr. ; Victoria, 9dwt. 16gr. “ The comparison is
vastly advantageous to West Australia.” Nothing of the
sort. N.S.W. is not in the same street with Victoria in
output or divs., yet its average is nearly double, and the
average yield of the biggest gold-camp the world has ever
seen is even lower than Vic.’s.

Foster, Vie. Minister of Mines, returned from
his Mt. Peddiok explorations, last week, with vague
tidings. He was pleased with what he saw, “ considering
the amount of work that had been done,” but would re-
frain from giving an opinion on the precious little work
visible until he gets the Govt. Geologist’s report in about
three weeks time. “In a number of places,” said Mr. F.,
“ tunnels had been driven into the hills, and some shafts
had a’so been sunk disclosing several lodes of galena. The
Mt. Deddick United Co. has displayed more energy in
opening up the lodes on its property than any of the
other mines.” Looks rather like a puffing of the particu-
lar spec, that has Foster’s colleague, Peacock, for its legal
manager. Anyway, it would he interesting to have
Foster’s opinion as to why those other mines, which were
boomed on the Melb. market previous to the birth of the
M.l>. United Co., have been worked with so little vigor.
Also, he might advance a reason why the energetic open-
ing up of the M.D. United “lodes” since February has
produced nothing better than a decline in the price of
shares from £3 to 30s.
The official pilgrimage to Mt. Deddick seems
to have finally disposed of the persistent Govt, railway
gag, anyhow. Even a narrow-gauge railway from the
mining field to the seaboard is voted impracticable, but
somewhere in the rugged country between Deddick and
Orbost a line may be constructed at some time, if some-
body should report that it can be done “ without incur-
ring a very large outlay.” The Govt., however, will
certainly cut one or two tracks through the awful bush
thereabouts, and auriferous discoveries may follow.

The now “Unique Colossal Melbourne Enter-
prise ” purposes to exploit the auriferous areas of
Northern and Western Borneo. It is pointed out
that, although the British West and British North Char-
tered Companies have at their disposal between them an
area of 80,130 square miles to explore and mine, there is
not a single mining plant at work on the island.

“S.S.”: Melbourne papers try to send loose
miners to Wood’s Point in search of fortune; and, as miners
still have faith in the papers, they go there, and strike
what is perhaps the most riddled district in Australia.
Wood’s Point, as a shop for the miner “ on his own,” has
been worked out about nine times, and a stranger would
not be able to find the diggings for old shafts and tunnels.
The co’s on rock seem to consider themselves lucky with
sdwt. stuff. The faith the miner has in a newspaper view
is most astounding ; and an old digger will often accept
the say-so of a city scribe in the teeth of his own experi-
ence, not knowing, of course, that the aforesaid scribe is
most probably only a street-bred expert, educated merely
in the cant phrases of the Exchange.
* * * •
Dear Bulletin. —A recent correspondent im-
plies that Bischoff-manager Keyser will have a better
claim on posterity than “ Philosopher ” Smith, the mine’s
discoverer. Let me point out that Smith spent nearly all
his spare time, for years, prospecting in about the worst
country and climate in Australasia, whereas Keyser only
came in after Bischoff was discovered, and managed the
claim for a good salary. Smith had to depend most of
his time on shanks’ pony ; the no doubt skilful and ener-
getic Keyser has been able to ride to and from the coast
by rail. K. has probably managed the claim well, but
there are others who could have done as well. Smith
voluntarily exposed himself to great risk and hardship for
a long time, without return ; Keyser managed about the
best tin-mine in the world, and paid big dividends —he
could hardly help paying them, for, anyhow, it would take
unimaginable mismanagement do seriously damage sucb a
phenomenal show. Easy to guess also who was the cutest,
and made most money.—Right Horse.

A floating business block to be moored at
Skaguay and Dawson City next season is the latest
Yankee idea ; it is to be steam-heated and have a perfect
village trading settlement aboard. The projector calcu-
lates that if the trade moves so can he.

The Cubans seem to be a lired race,
judging by their proverbs. “Hasta Manana ”
(There is always to-morrow); “ Only dogs and
Englishmen walk in the sun.” Sancho Panza-like
epigram is still natural to Spaniards. A witty
Spanish journalist recently defined an island as
“a piece of land entirely surrounded by English-
men.” “ How’s that for the Philippines?” asks
J. Bull.
She was a winsome damsel of 19, and, when
she had selected a suitable leg of pork at the
butcher’s, she ordered that it should be salted for
boiling and sent round to her cottage in Suburbia.
After settling accounts, she deemed it wise to jog
the buteherman’s faulty memory, and remarked,
“Now, don’t you forget to put my leg in pickle,
will you?” Then she blushed, and fled preci-
pitately.
S.A. Parliamentary Labor-men, bound to
anti-Billiousness, are thrown back upon the com-
plaint that Kingston and Glynn helped to sacrifice
the interests of the smaller States by voting to
limit the powers of the Senate in money matters.
Whereupon Kingston replies crushingly to Price,
who made the complaint, that, led by the late
J. A. McPherson, lie (Price) and other of the
Labor members voted in the Assembly against
suggesting an amendment of this provision, on
the ground that it was “ the only possible ” way
to Federation.
Stolid Premier Turner, the other day,
dropped a lovely “oner” on to anti-Federal Hig-
gins’s eye. Higgins stated in one of his speeches
against the Commonwealth Bill that Turner
would in his heart be glad to see the Bill rejected
“ because he saw he had made a mistake in re the
verbiage of the railway clauses.” Turner replies
that the one thing in the Bill which he most fears
from the Victorian point of view is the railway
provision that was carried in the Convention by
only one vote ; and the one Victorian who voted
for it was Higgins. Higgins is holding his eye up
against a cold lamp-post.

last cropped up in the Federal discussion. An
antiquated crank, writing to S.M. Hkrai.D, sug-
gests than ten of the most prominent Federalists
and ten of the Anti-Bill people should be chosen,
and their character, ability, s’ncerity and moral
weight duly considered, and the set in which the
predominance lies may be safely followed. The
“•Good ” man again ! But then, alas, who is to
define good or bad character, and who is to make
sure about the character of the person who does
the definition, and who is going to pay the cost of
the necessary delegation to Japan and elsewhere
to find out what the characters of our politicians
really are ?
Cosmos: “Re the ‘ blood-thicker-than-
water ’ gag. My experience of the Eastern and
Southern States —’B3 to ’B7—was that anyone stood
a better show in the scramble for work than a
Briton. In New York, there are whole colonies
of Italians, Germans, and Irish, very few members
of whom can speak decent English. Of pure
Americans, I met about half-a-dozen, and, any-
way, Americans don’t count for much in the U.S.
The * mob ’ are open for sale to the highest bidder.
Such a conglomeration has no patriotism, but
sticks to its racial and religious antipathies, which
are kept well sharpened. The Union is the most
illiterate, corrupt, and clique-ridden nation of the
Anglo-Saxon group. English is merely the official
language. As you say, ‘ They speak English, bub
think American.’ ”

Owing to that tired feeling which renders
most Tas. legislative acts and functions even more
slipshod than those of other provinces, the Isle of
ad .Dreams has to rub along without a fully-
fledged Chief Justice for yet a while. The
oupreme Court Act, 1895, bars the Governor-in-
Oouncil from filling the first vacancy that may
occur in the Supreme Court Bench without the
express consent of Parliament. The “first”
vacancy was caused by the death of Chief Justice
Dobson, but, having regard to the fact that the
Government only holds office by the narrowest of
narrow majorities, Braddon-Fysh discreetly de-
cided that ; the moment was inopportune to ask
parliament s approval of any appointment. The
ffifficulty is being wriggled out of by the creation
and filling of a second vacancy, against which the
9o Act says nothing. Thus Justice Adams is
T „‘, r ® d °‘ 1 11 h ' s Pension and his place as puisne
Judge is filled by the Ministerial appointment of
Opposition leader Inglis Clark. On May 14
Mr. Clark climbs on to the Bench and Mr.
Adams climbs off. Justice Dodds continues as
Acting Chief until that date, when he will
probably, be regularly installed as Chief Justice of
lasmania and its dependencies.
* * * *
Chief Justice and vice-viee-regal repre-
sentative John Madden was asked before Vic.
Law Commission the other day: “ Could you
suggest some means whereby a poor man who has
only got his brains could enter the profession ? ”
“No,” said his Waxcellency, chippily ; “there is
no royal road to the profession for such men.”
“ Royal road ” is good, implying an easy and
luxurious jaunt. The ambitious poor man asks
for no royal road, but only for the abolition of the
extortionate tolls, and the poll-tax, and the
customs and precedent duties, and the charges
and legal brigandage he encounters on the present
hard track. There is no royal road—the only
road is a royalty road ; and it is easier for a rich
man to enter the kingdom of the prophets than
for a poor man to enter the province of the law.
* * * *

The English Naicest’s growing inclination
towards the raking-in of base commercial shekels
finds a curious recent development in the adoption
by Lord Hopetoun’s two maiden sisters of the
horse-dealing trade. In addition to personally
driving home their purchases, they act, it is stated,
as their own “vets.”—a practice the incidentals
to which would involve some very peculiar pre-
dicaments for “ maiden,” or any other, “ladies.”
One “proud daughter of a hundred earls” is running
a dairy, in which, it is alleged, she works herself;
keeping a keen eye on the bawbees, and “ vending
penn’orths of milk to the poor villagers at the
back-door.” The Marquis of Salisbury, it is also
asserted, will not abate, even to his lOs.-a-week
serfs, one penny of the Is. each at which are sold
the rabbits taken off the estate under their noses.
How Napoleon’s “nation of shopkeepers” keeps
coming to the front!

At Melb. County Court, last week, a poor
fraud-summoned civil-servant pleaded that he had
nine grown-up daughters gnawing at his income.
Judge Gaunt asked the gentleman whether he
didn’t think it was time that some of the girls
turned their attention to marriage or other busi-
ness. Up to now, seemingly, the nine adult
females have been attacking the duties of the
household in battalions, and sweeping up the
“ dust ” as fast as poor pa can put it down.

The pangs of parental affection.
A cab dashed up to the hospital casualty-
room, and an elderly man sprang out and
dragged forth a half - fainting 6ft. son,
whose hand had been shattered by a gun
accident. Whilst the injury was being
attended to, and sundry fingers ampu-
tated, the old man danced up and down
out-side, tearing out his hair, and ex-
claiming : “My God ! my God ! what
have I done that I should have to suffer
all this ! ”
Talking of the recent development of com-
mercial instincts among the superiah classes in
England. A while ago, one Colonel Daniel, of
Pelham Place, S. W., was sued by his laundress
for an unpaid bill. The gallant warrior replied
with a counter claim of 255. on account of a pair
of woollen combinations belonging to his wife
which had been ruined in the wash. The Colonel
proved his counter claim and routed the plaintiff
He gave evidence to the effect that the legs of the
garment aforesaid had shrunk out of all knowledge
and wearing, in consequence of the undue hotness
of the water, or because they were allowed to
remain too long in soak before being rinsed.
Under ordinary conditions a military man would
not carry much weight as an expert in wash-tub
tactics, but the colonel was a crusher. He twirled
his moustache-ends and stated that he had been a
student of shrinkage in combinations and things
when he and a fellow officah conducted a laundry
at Wandsworth.” Female belief in the military-
can t stand many such revelations. The thought
that a majah-general on parade may be longing to
g’ot on duty and assist in turning the mangle is
calculated to dim the glory of his epaulettes. The
time is coming, perhaps, when the co-respondent
captain in a fashionable divorce-suit will be ac-
cused of luring the giddy matron to the back
parlor of his own ham-and-beef shop.

F-C. . Met a charming old lady the other
day who had never heard of the English

it is nearly twenty years since she visited the
big smoke, and she knows nothing of life as we
know it.

Thus a Barrier
school - teacher : “ The
ignorance of children
born and bred in this
district is astonishing.
If I ask a class of, say,
20 how many have seen
a river, possibly five will
hold up their hands.

a much larger percent-
age haven’t the slightest idea of a sea or ocean.
It is heart-breaking to attempt to teach some of
them.”

More than half the divorce cases in Aus-
tralia are based on stories about how one of the
parties to the union “just stepped out” casually
one day and never returned. Last week three
cases were heard in Melbourne on the same day :
(Case 1) “Less than a month after their marriage,
Faulkner left home, saying he was going to look
for work, and petitioner had never seen him since.”
(Case 2) “ On 10th June, 1891, respondent left his
home as usual to attend to his business. He did
not return.” (Case 3) “Evidence was given by
petitioner that he was a sanitary contractor. . .
Respondent kissed him (the sanitary contractor)
and said she was going to Collingwood to see about
the baby’s vaccination-papers and would be home
before evening. She did not come back.” (Case 4)
Respondent just stepped out, and died. Case con-
sequently withdrawn. Probably, it is the great
size and the grea,t emptiness of Australia that
makes the Australian the most disappearing man
on earth. The man who just steps out in Aus-
tralia is about as completely lost as if he had just
stepped off Jupiter into the universe.

dynamite explosion in Brilliant Deep Levels.

to pieces in shed near his residence. Richard
Davis (aged 72), Mt. Morgan, found in an out-
house with his head blown off by a plug of dyna-
mite —an open, sharpened clasp knife beside him.
Fancy waiting till you’re 72 to blow your head off!

P.Lufting
A poor man who has only got his brains
Must never hope to practise at the Bar,
The Brotherhood his company disdains—
Sing hey ! the rank outsider that he are*
Sing hey ! the rank outsider,
The quite unqualifieder,
The lowly rank outsider that he are.
A puppy with some “ pieces ” in his paw
May one day put a case before a Judge,
Provided that he studies for the law,
And purchases assistance from a drudge.
The Law is meant, say sich men,
For poor as well as rich men,
O, that’s the yarn they pitch men —but it’s fudge.
John Madden doesn’t want to find a means
To open his profession to the poor,
Unless one has a pocket-full of beans
’Tis vain to seek admission at the door.
The door is well defended,
The matter can’t be mended :
The Law was not intended for the poor.
SOUR GRAPES.
The distant lights within your liquid eyes,
Like dim stars glowing in a mist of tears,
Mock the dull anguish of my soul’s surprise—
False lights which lured me through the niopths and
years
To taste at last the venom of your sneers !
I thought your lofty brow supremely fair ;
I thought your tongue more eloquent than song;
The curling glory of your sunny hair
A radiant shawl to shield me from the throng:
’Tis but a net cast from the shores of wrong.
So if I am a fox who pines for sweets
Which me denied I curse from dusk to day,
I’ll still remember beauty is so fleet,
And wait until the season has its sway—
Then, when grapes fall, the fox will have his way !
N.S.W.
WILL. BRUTON.

One of the most awful things that
can happen to a miner is to have his spine
injured—and it pretty often occurs, through
a fall of ground while the workman is
shovelling the ore back from the working
face. Occasionally the victim lives for
years, hopelessly crippled, often utterly
incapable of moving his limbs and filled
with dread of possible brain - affection.
There has been such a patient lying in the
Bendigo Hospital for the past six months ;
and Broken Hill has another who reckons
his sufferings by as many years. A na-
tional mine-disaster fund that overlooks
such cases, while taking in those in which
merciful death has come promptly, hardly
fills the bill.

A Melb. daily mentions, as a fairly artistic
idea, a new fashion of floral advertising lately in-
troduced in I'ranee. “The advertisers,” it says,
“instead of making the landscape hideous with
hoardings, that appear at intervals all along the
different railway-lines, have devised the idea of
planting the slopes of the line with flowers of
every hue, and making by this means the lettering
of certain advertisements.” The Bulletin re-
lieves its prospective outraged feelings in ad-
vance :
Beak, solemnly, at conclusion of case in
N.Q. police-court, thus : “ This is one of those
cases, gentlemen, in which, after giving my deci-
sion, I don’t know whether I’m right or whether
I’m wrong.” And then he gave it. Same genius,
in case where white man was charged with having
stolen parrot from Chinaman: “lam not satis-
fied with this case. I shall dismiss it; but, if the
defendant is an honest man, he will restore the
bird ” —long pause—“ that is, if he has it.”

Specimen written answer given by pupil at
recent Adelaide University examination: “ Primi-
tive means very holy ; for example, the picnic was
a very primitive affair.” The boy who wrote this
may have been shady in his etymology, but he
was a keen observer and a fine, though unconscious,
humorist.
MA’S DILEMMA.
TERRIBLE INGANT: “ Ma, dear, I saw Uncle George kissing the governess at the
door of her room when you were in the bath.”
MA : “ Nonsense, my dear. It couldn’t have been Uncle George.”
TERRIBLE INFANT : “ Well, then, ma—if it wasn’t Uncle George, it must have
been papa!”

At Newington Asylum, Sydney, over 700
poor women, mostly old, are turned out of doors
daily at 7 a.m. One of these recently begged a
visitor to procure her a shawl, as she could not
move about to get warm like her younger com-
panions. Cold Charity !
The use, or abuse, of the
word “ Nice ” seems to be be-
coming an Australian'habit. In
a batch of advts. from a recent
Saturday’s D.T. and Herald,
19 advertisers had “ nice ”
rooms suitable for “nice”—
private, steady, quiet—families,
.and the twentieth just cut it
down to “apartments for nice
family.” Eleven wanted a
“nice” home, and three de-
mand “nice board, cheap.”
One|’tiser capped all not long
ago with “ young man, not
strong, requires nice employ-
ment, country preferred.”

Ponte: “Travelling in-
spectors of various kinds, whose
duties take them to strange
places, have fallen beneath the
wiles of an ‘ unaccountable fe-
male ’ at a certain wayside ac-
commodation house. She is
smart and buxom, with a per-
petual challenge in her eyes.
As most of the officials are
middle-aged, susceptible, and
grass widowers, her little free-
doms usually culminate in a laughing query
as to the whereabouts of her room. Singu-
larly enough, it adjoins his and through
the night the visitor mistakes the room. The
girl’s scream bring her brother on the scene with
theatrical quickness, and the victim gets out of it
by paying according to his wealth or status. A
thriving industry.”

Alexa winder Collins, unctuous dealer in oils,
and co-respondent in Melbourne’s most entertain-
ing divorce-suit, when appealed to —as he be-
lieved —by the wife of the other fellow —the lady
\\ ho had loved not wisely but too well, and was
cast out —sent, to relieve her mind and her bodily
distresses, pious precept and six twopenny stamps.
Alexawnder is Scotch, and he has wrecked one of
the best Scotch jokes of the century—that which
describes the wrath of the gude wife when, for
the second time, “ Rawbert ” came home saxpence
short in his wages. “ I ken wha’ it is,” wailed the
heartbroken wife, “ye’re keepin’anither woman!”
Alexawnder demonstrates that this is no mere flight
of fancy—probably the gude wife’s suspicions were
correct. Extravagance is a sin, but it does not
necessarily follow that a sin is extravagance, ye
ken.
* » * *
All the parties concerned in the case of
Collins, the seductive eucalyptus-oil man, be-
longed to a sect in which the members called
each other “brothers” and “sisters.” And when
Brother Horace wrote indignantly to Brother
Alexawnder about the leading astray of Brother
Horace’s wife, he received a beautiful reply, in
which Brother Alexawnder told him that he still
hung on to the “clear, shining Truth.” Then
Brother Horace, thirsting for vengeance, tele-
graphed in his wife’s name as follows : “ Wire
next address. Write. Send stamps. Great dis-
tress.” The telegram cost ninepence, and, as it
drew Is. worth of stamps from Brother Alexawn-
der, Brother Horace had threepenn’orth of fiend-
ish vengeance on his enemy, and proudly men-
tioned the fact in court. Strange how persistently
religious sects run to threepennies !

Macleay (N.S.W.) Akgus, 14/5/98 :
Apropos of the fair occupant of the Bench in company
with Judge Docker on Tuesday afternoon, it is the first
time in the history of the court—in this district, at all
events—that a District Court Judge has been accompanied
by an Associate. That is solely the privilege of Supreme
Court Judges, and even then their Associates are of the
masculine gender.
However, while a personage like Mr. Docker is per-
mitted to remain on the bench, it may be just as
well that he should submit his offensive manners
to the restraining influence of a lady’s presence.

A curious old Melbourne capitalist named
Houston died the other day. He was a lone
patriarch 6|ft. high and aged 73, and his gaunt
figure had been seen daily for 30 years sloping up
the back street to his little tin tenement. The
rent was 2s. a week, and for years the ancient
fought hard to have it reduced to Is. 6d. Then
the tin residence caved in through age and settled
the question, and the inhabitant removed the
stretcher and the old tin plate, which composed
his entire furniture, into lodgings. He never
smoked or drank, and he lived entirely on cold
victuals. When Houston first became known in
these parts he possessed a coat, hat, pair of pants,
two old boots, and a decayed shirt. He “couldn’t
see his way ” to buy socks but he acknowledged
several times in the last five years that he was
thinking of making a new shirt, as that process
was cheaper than buying one. But he never
carried out his purpose, and the old shirt-
fell away till all that remained of it
was a string round his neck, and, as he didn’t own
a waistcoat, a large area of his stomach became
public scenery. At last, one day, two small boys
made disrespectful remarks about the matter, and
the old man got angry, and seized a stick, and ran
after them in his unlaced boots till he fell down
dead. In his coat, which was an article of his own
manufacture, .£lOOO worth of securities were dis-
covered, and other wealth is supposed to be
planted. He was a fine example of Thrift, and he
died as a capitalist should, endeavoring to sup-
“A BIRD IN THE HAND IS
WORTH TWO IN THE BUSH."
press the democratic small boy and maintain
law’n’order.

A Sydney daily published a cable the other
day in which it talked about a line of steamers
between Paris and New York. A few days later
it sent a warship overland from Michigan.
* • » *
Donnybrook (W.A.) is said by those who
have inspected it to be the prize-stench town of
this continent. The short and simple annal of
Donnybrook is that all the refuse of the town
flows into the sluggish Preston river, and all the
drinking-water of the town comes out of that same
river. There are no drains or sanitary appliances
of any kind in Donnybrook ; everything goes into
the Preston. The local horse, who is a tolerably
seasoned animal, flatly refuses to drink the Pres-
ton water, but the placid Sandgroper doesn’t
mind. At latest advices, about half the fearful
town was ill with typhoid or diphtheria, and the
inhabitants set it down to the “ visitation of God.”
Pure water can be got a little way below the earth
surface, but a proposal to spend <£2o on digging a
town well was rejected with scorn. The ’Groper
doesn’t throw his money away in any such reckless
fashion as that.
* * * *
The Whifceworkers’ Board, now sitting in
Melb. to consider the possibility of fixing piece-
work rates, finds itself unable to grapple
with the multifariousness of women’s under-
wear. Some factories put more embroidery and
frills on the unmentionable articles than other
factories go in for, so a trade-log embracing every
variety of whitewerk seems out of the question,
Unless the Victorian consumers of ready-made
mysteries can meet together and arrive at an
understanding on the subject of decoration for
their most sacred garments, the present difficulty
must remain insurmountable. It is rather too
delicate a matter to be settled by a Parliamentary
committee.

Along the expanse of sands,
Where moon-rays fall
They lie, like leaves on autumn strands,
And, seeing them, one understands
Love levels all !

* * * * .
That shrewd and gallant veteran, Hosie
(Melb. restaurateur), the original Scotch-pie man,
has given his 3d.-course-catering-biz. best. Hosie
started this venture in his old age on the col-
lapse of the boom, and, at the buffet, ex-million-
aires (on paper) and bankrupt silver-kings stood
up and wolfed their modest threepennorth. Now,
so many restaurants give a full dinner of three
courses for sixpence that the old man’s craft was
blanketed.
* * * *
The idiotic and wanton injury to the
beautiful limestone formations in the Copper Mine
Ca.ve, Yarrangobilly, N.S.W. has induced the
Mines Department to publicly notify that the law
will be pushed to its extreme against any dis-
coverable mutilators of any limestone-cave-forma-
tions in the province. In the Copper Mine Cave,
axes, crowbars, and even gunpowder have been
used to effect a smash-up which, though apparently
directed in part to the procuration of calcite-
crystals, has evidently been, in the main, due to
sheer senseless delight in destruction.
Ah, sweet it was and pleasant,
In Youth’s enamoured hours,
To speak unto the Loved One
The Language of the Flowers 1
But soon another meaning
May rose and lily bear,
And violet sweet, and marguerite,
In garden and parterre.
For roses, ranged in order,
And violets may spell—
“ Buy none but Simpkins’ Sheep-Dip 1 ”
Or “ Ask for Hogg’s Hotel! ”
And in our public gardens
There yet may be descried
The legend writ in lilies —
“Try Smith’s Insecticide 1”
Tub Bulletin, in loathing,
Might wish that it were dead
When here that fashion reaches,
But that it has a dread
That o’er the silent dwelling
Where it would lie at rest
Some fiend would write in roses white—
“ Moidd’s Coffins are the Best.”

A WOMAN’S LETTER.
T 4 „ Sydney, May 17, 1808.
My Dear Myee—
“ The Middleman ” chased “ Moths ” out of the
Criterion last Saturday, with a force and fury that
has not been heard in the theatre this season. The
night was wet, as all nights were just about that
time, but no damp got into the house. Cartwright
dried the air up to red heat with fiery furnaces,
and baking tea-pots, and his still more burning
portrayal of an old man cursing. Any sort of a
curse goes down with a house this century. Statis-
tics prove that the poor old grey-haired damn still
tmkles the funny-bone of a tired audience more
effectively than any other effort. But Cyrus
BJenkarn s curse was a work of art that made the
top ceiling hang low for minutes, and even the
best-conducted feet in the dress-circle stamped
their approval all over the carpets.
Cartwright has had such a run of Arctic weather
all through this venture that his admirers have
been smitten with sympathetic neuralgia as they
sat out the rapid series of chilly first nights,
and watched the houses get thinner and thinner,
and heard the applause scare itself away in gho-tly
whispers that died if you looked at them. The
of a one-time star made things no
better, and one evening through the week of
«« ti° rn an hour before the curtain was to rise,
t‘ tutu der ” vvas in rehearsal. Fortunately
Moths ’ managed somehow to flutter through the
performance, but there was a lot of desperation in
the flutter. It was a glorious victory for the ad-
mirers to see their actor’s spark flash out once
more on Saturday, and strike the audience in the
eye with a dazzle that turned Wilson Barrett’s
light low by comparison.
The play of “The Middleman” leans more
heavily on its acting than its dressing. The frocks
are not remarkable. Miss Stewart, in a white wig
ftnd some rich black silks, makes a comely mamma
to pretty Hope Mayne. The wife’s-mother-made-
easy is one of Miss Stewart’s own patents,
and the man who wouldn't take Hope on
her own (if there is such a man), would have
had her by the dozen for the. sake of such a
mother-in-law. Miss Lambs one best frock is
snatched out of sight by the curtain. There is
only time to see a lot of green tucks disappearing
into Cartwright’s arms when the lights are up and
the play is over. I fancy that this is the briefest
glimpse on record of a leading lady’s best frock.
Thursby, as Captain Chandler, arranged his
moustache too close to his nose, and made hearts
beat by bis resemblance toa popular Aide. Only a
clever man like Arthur Styan could have worn one
tie so smoothly for two years. The front row of
society leaned over the red velvet dress-circle, and
demonstrated cuttingly what a little row that
front row is. From a side box lively Lady Holled
Smith sparkled in black and steel alongside of her
large commander, and on the other side of the
house a lady achieved a bathing-costume effect in
tight pink sleeves, as plain at the shoulders as a
long-sleeved camisole.
Speaking of Wilson Barrett a while ago reminds
me that Her Majesty’s stood on its head last
Wednesday,,and kicked itself into hysterics over
A.N.R.’s” tilt against his majesty’s Hamlet
Everybody knows that the tilter is Nugent Robert-
son, journalist, poet, anti-Federationist, and bar-
rister, as the company took the trouble to discover
before they launched a dart that could wound
nobody but a barrister. “ You are the clerk of
some fifth-rate, pettifogging attorney,” is a barb
altogether invented for the slaughter of the Bar.
Led on by Wilson Barrett, the whole troupe
threw themselves into the battle, and a large
theatrical advt. travelled free through the land on
the back of the Daily Telegraph. Barrett’s
oration was adorned with “ Dear-me’s,” and
“ Never-mind’s,” and “ Bless-mv-soul’s,” until
the authorship of the “Sign of the Cross” was
irrevocably established. “ A Southern Woman ”
was undiplomatic enough to first scream out an
invitation to “ A.N.R. ” to come to America to bo
cow-hided, and after that to ask him if he had a
wife. Surely an American dame would manage
better than that. Such a bleating and a bellow-
ing has not been heard since- well, since last
year’s cattle-drafting.
A calf also played a big part in the opening of
tn© Vaccinal Institute last week. Tli© Oovernor
and a mob of medicos waded through torrents to

a good white arm is ruined for ball-dresses by
great leprous-looking blotches half-way between
elbow and shoulder.
A University ball occupies Paddington town-
hall on Thursday, May 26th, and another intelli-
gent dance will be the fancy-dress ball the Society
of Artists is discussing for the opening of its
next exhibition.
The London criticisms on our Austral artists’
pictures have brought to light several sky-fanciers
whose names are not even names to the Australian
public. Several of these unknown painters figure
among the creators of works marked “sold,” and
many leading lights are not noticed at all. The
most successful lady is a Sydney artist, Alice
Norton (daughter of Hon. James Norton, the
veteran solicitor), whose work is deservedly
admired locally, and who has been favored to the
extent of four purchases. Mr. Lister Lister has
possibly made the biggest pot of coin out of the
sales, but there was not much elevation even in
the top prices. The Duke of York went to quiz
round, but no record of his purchases is to hand
yet. A critic in the Daily Graphic comments
that the nude is but “sparsely depicted,” and
history is almost entirely neglected. The remark
about the nude sounds as if the Graphic wanted
draperies, and, as for the moan about history, the
absence of Queens of Sheba, Elizabeths signing
Mary Stuart’s death-warrants, Cromwells leading
Ironsides to the fray, &c., won’t raise an echoing
moan this side of the globe.
The latest news of Edgar Straus tells of a little
mend in his health, lie is still recruiting in the
dry climate of Arinidale, and doctors’ orders for-
bid sea air for the present. The benefit concert
came off during Saturday’s loudest rain, but there
was a better booking than the house revealed. Eel-
low-musicians offered up a lengthy programme,
and every one who sings at Sydney concerts was
represented. The only sensation of the evening
was lent by Mine. Kellerman, who maimed her
finger in her sparkling Train du Diable, and was
unable to touch the notes again.
Songs without fees are correct at such a concert
as a benefit to Straus, but there are other occa-
sions when the correctness will not be fixed by an
etiquette-book. A moneyed feminine coterie
invited one of our tenors to sing for his afternoon
tea, the other day, and was knocked over by a
courteous memo, from the singer, that he sold his
warbles for three guineas. An icily-reproachful
reply withered him with the information that
those At Homes were conducted on principles that
applied even to the buns, and the pastry, and the
loaf-sugar, and the boiling water. It told him
that the afternoon-tea was acquired at the very
lowest rates, and left him to infer that there was
still a lower rate for songs, and that his callous-
ness was immense. But he boldly refuses to
wither, or sing, or do anything at 'all for that
coterie, without a “definite arrangement.’'
The St. Bernard's Home for Working Gentle-
women is not exactly a charity, as it supports
itself to the extent of meeting its own housekeep-
ing bills and incidental expenses. The rent is the
only difficulty, and to arrange its payment a com-
mittee exists that keeps an eye on the working of
the Home, and gets up entertainments when the
funds are low. The retreat is an ordinary, un-
branded house in William-street,carefully managed
within. Nine working gentlewomen are allowed
to live there at an inexpensive rate, and situations
are found for those who need them. The docu-
ment which sets this all forth does not say whether
the gentlewomen are turned out if unable to con-
tribute their inexpensive items, but the probability
is that the Home is especially good to them at that
time, and keeps them there unspotted from the
world until something turns up. Anyway, it is a
good deed that saves even nine elderly spinsters
from the steak and turmoil of a third-rate board-
ing house.
lo help the Home along there was a Musical
Afternoon, last week, in the Oddfellows’ Hall.
The gathering was entirely feminine - clergy,
pretty girls with red ribbons round their waists,
and the strained essenceof the cable-tram suburbs.
A pleasant little programme went on at intervals
under Miss Woolley’s care, and conviviality set
m with the frogs and monkeys of the Toy Sym-
phony. One tiny girl managed her violin with a
pretty promise, and jolly little owls and night-
ingales introduced an air of midnight revelry into
the otherwise peaceful atmosphere of breaking-up
day at a ladies’ school.
Miss Marcia Cox, fourth sister of the doctor’s
daughters whose beauty mortally wounded two
aides in the Carington days, is to be married in a
few weeks in Sydney. After her marriage she
settles down in the Big Smoke.
Two jaunts under a mackintosh to the Tennis
Tournament revealed no audience to speak of, and
a congress of players in the unhappy state of
never being dry for a week. The trip'was a dis-
appointment to the Victorians, who had heard big
stories of Sydney’s enthusiasm over tennis. But
in Sydney, as everywhere, even fine weather can’t
turn tennis off the road to death. It is galloping
along at a great pace to the croquet-cemetery,
where the racket stops from troubling, and the
emptiness of love loses its power to grieve. Miss
Peyton played a gritty game last week, but hurt
her knee just when she whs expected to win, and
was beaten on Monday by Miss Dransfield in the
final for the Ladies’ Championship.
Tennis players were the organisers of the bright
Neutral Bay dance last Thursday, and several
tournament folk were among the guests. The
wettest night of the week told on the attendance,
but, as no more than two people dance together, a
crowd is not the main element of a successful hop.
A pretty young play-actress toned down her re-
markable black hair with a blue frock, and her
fair sister assisted her fairness in the green that
works harder than any color for golden-haired
women.
No one would be surprised if our ex-Governor’s
wives forgot us in the shortest possible space of
time. Yet Lady Jersey writes regularly to the
University Women’s Society, and showed an in-
convenient memory, the other day, in alluding to
an engagement that began in her day and since
ended abruptly. In a letter that was read aloud
to the Society by the secretary, she directed some
prettily-worded sentiments to the erstwhile en-
gaged ones. It was awkward that the lady in the
case should have been present at the reading.
Not even vice regal interest in one’s doings can
atone for publicly-offered congratulations on an
engagement that has ceased to be.
An Australienne in London writes: “The
rumour—now gaining much credence in ’Ome
circles—that multi-millionaire Astor is to have a
peerage given to him with the object of taking
him out of the rank of a commoner so that he can
marry Wales’sole remaining and plainest (laugh ter,
is causing a great deal of nervous fluttering in the
dove cotes of the * smart ’ sassiety journals. No
official denial of the story has yet been given,
and mighty folk ‘in the know’ are strangely
reticent on the subject. So it needs the most diplo-
matic handling on the part of the scribes whose
aristocratic susceptibilities are outraged at the
idea of a man, whose; father or grandfather handed
round fried-fish in a Bowery saloon, mating with a
princess of The Blood. But these same scribes
know well enough The Blood’s strong reach-out
after coin, and Tummy’s democratic tendencies
wherever coin is concerned ; so they wisely neither
deny norcontirm the rumor, but make vague gene-
ral remarks about it. One gushful scribess, whose
column seems to shed inky tears at the idea of the
mesalliance, skilfully turns the subject up by say-
ing, ‘Still money, even in royal circles, is an un-
doubted power and the lack of it causes much dis-
tress, which has been recently proved by the
great economies that have been revealed in the
Tecks’ happy home and the sad break-up and loss
of income the Duke of Teck has had to grapple
with since his popular wife's death.’ This fat and
bizy royal fortune-hunter has been living on the
money granted to his huge wife by the great B.P.
for nearly 30 years past, and it seems high time
that the said B.P. should knock off paying several
thousands a year for the privilege and pleasure of
beholding Teek’s prodigious and celebrated yawn
at all public ceremonies.”
Surely the unacknowledged brave should in-
clude those people who lose false hair and teeth,
and advertise their deprivations in “Lost and
Pound.” To own up to a toupee is about the
ultima thule of feminine courage, but last week a
candid notice in a daily appealed for “a tail of
lady’s.hair, lost at the Haymarket. ’’ and promised
a reward “on return to the Wesleyan Book
Depot.” Shades of the holy John ! Those old
rnppings at the Wesleys’ parsonage will resuscitate
if female folly is to be cloaked thusly with the
mantle of this fierce revivalist. Or does the advt.
bear an inner meaning, and has the Wesleyan
Book Depot lent itself to the bereaved lady on
condition that she hands over her fragile soul to
be saved, by Wesley's saving clauses, from the
tails of other people's hair and the gnashing of
other people’s teeth ?
Someone with a cunning knowledge of human
nature is advertising that lie will remove tattoo
marks from Sydney’s brawny arms—money other-
wise returned. There was a time when a young
man who had not stamped himself with these
sincere symptoms of love’s fitful fever would not
be smiled upon by any self-respecting housemaid.
Even school-boys measured school-girls’ interest in
them by.the amount of arm the tattoos covered.
But, when all the rest passes and the boys are men
and the girls other men’s wives, it is a little
aggravating to have these marks revive old
memories and bear a silent witness to the follies
of the past. If the advertiser doesn’t make a
fortune ’twill be because the marks won’t yield,
and the money has to be returned.

“ The story of Mine. Risser, one of the victims
of the Balaclava double suicide, shows a strangely
persistent accumulation of misfortunes. Married
at 23 to a man of rank and wealth, she soon after
lost her husband and little son, and also her
money, which went to the husband’s family.
Came out to Australia to be married to a cousin,
but the match never came off. Went governessing,
met Risser, married him after two weeks’ acquaint-
ance, though much his sup rior socially. The few
Melb. people who knew Madame Risser say that
she was a perfect type of the graceful and fas-
cinating Frenchwoman. Her sorrows had so told
on her, however, that, though only 27, she was
white-haired.
‘ ‘ E very boom -burst Iyd y turns to tea-room i rig, but
one, the widow of a Melb. banker who is dead,
has taken to tea-rooming with a difference. She
cooks by wholesale for other tea-rooms, as her
husband cooked the public goose; and there is a
run on her pies and tarts just as there was on

has waltzed into business with a partner with
whom she used many times to waltz in ball-rooms,
for he was an aforetime haughty youth and one of
the best dancers in Melbourne. Now he has
caught hold of the right end of a gridiron and is
one of the Lest grillers going. At a chop or steak
he is considered Al. Good grit in both of them.
Talking of cats that come back, AVestralians
swear to the truth of the following puss-yarn. The
only son of the late Rev. J. Johnston, first Con-
gregational parson of Fremantle (see memorial
church in that town) was returning home to W A
in a sailing vessel. His ship, sighted off Rottnest
Island, disappeared in a moment. None of her
crew were seen again, so she must have gone down,
though the sea never gave up its dead. The
missing brig's carpenter had bis home on Fre-
mantle beach, separated from Rottnest by 12
miles of sea, dotted with rocks and an islet or two.
boniß nights after the ship’s disappearance, Chips’
wife, distressed but not despairing, sat worrying
over the vessel which came not. Suddenly she
heard a feeble scratching and opened the door to
adimt, not the missing carpenter, but dear old
labby, Ins trusty ship-mate. Poor puss, drenched,
wasted to a skeleton, her claws worn down to the
quick, was palpably near the end of her nine
lives. Welcomed with tears and kisses and
nursed tenderly as a baby for the absent one’s
sake, she drew her breath in pain all night and left
tor the happy hunting-grounds at dawn. The
carpenter s wife at once sent word of the night’s
experience to old Mrs. Johnston. That distracted
mother hurried to the longshore cottage and both
women gave up hn pc as they cried together over
the battered body of “ the messenger from the
dead, for it was in this light they chose to regard
poor puss. °
“ Nowadays, wanderers just back from ’Ome
hold a parade of the sartorial spoil annexed en
route, inviting their cronies to gnash their teeth
enviously at the splendor thereof. More often
than not the bulk of the finery has been locally
modelled on the lines of the few-and-far-between
genuine Parisian creations secured by the pilgrim.
A thickly-gilt recent bride got back from her
travels not long before her wedding, and at once
called upon her former modiste for new plumage.
‘ Whatever I wear will go down as “ imported,” ’
she said airily, ‘so I just saved all my shopping
for Melbourne—and pa’s account! ’
“ A quiet wedding was celebrated on Thursday
week between bridegroom Dicketison, of ‘John
Anderson My Jo’ time of life, and bride Wright,
40 years younger. For once, the sassiety reporters
could have got-off their stock-wedding announce-
ments with freshness. The ceremony was almost
in dumb-show in the Friends’ Meeting House, in
Russell-street. Only three couples have been
thus turned-off, according to Quaker rites, in 20
years. The quaintness of the business made it
pretty, and the ‘ stiffkit ’ was the size of a tray-
cloth, and was unrolled and signed before all
friends present. So the Quaker ceremony, which
is quiet, plain marrying, gets there just the same
as the fancy ceremonies which have much frill
and are cut bias, with plenty of train and veiling.

“Mr. F. M. Alexander, a slim young man with
a curly-headed smile and much pink on his cheek,
gave a dramatic and elocutionary recital at the
Athenaeum Hall last week. The tinted aspirant,
who claims to hold the secret of a perfect voice-
production, turned out to be a genteel entertainer
of the tea-party order, and his show had the
peculiar merit of closing at 10 o’clock sharp.
About the best item among his assortment of
sketches’ was ‘The Amateur Rider,’ by ‘The
Banjo,’ in which he expressed a ringing good
voice and an evident intention to achieve a suit-
able racecourse accent. He didn’t achieve
the accent, and it would have clashed
horribly with _ his curly hair and shop-
walker graces if he had succeeded—but the
humor of the yarn wasn’t lost in the struggle.
Mr. Alexander’s pupil, Miss Edith Tasca-Page
(the stage-struck wife of a Tasmanian official),
likewise spoke two pieces, previous to supporting
Mr. Janies Cathcart in the Quarrel Scene from
School for Scandal. ’ The elocution and acting
of the lady pupil left several things to be desired,
but a semi-sassiety audience felt very grateful for
veteran Cathcart, and the long pent-up applause
gushed forth so freely that his pretty colleague
probably didn’t notice her shortcomings.
“ Love laughs at lymph. A little idyll of the
Quarantine-station was completely nipped in the
bud a week or two back owing to a lady’s insus-
ceptibility to vaccine. Her husband went down
to Portsea to medically attend some spotty
patients in hospital, and his wife (only a short
time married they were) yearned to share his
exile, but an obdurate health-officer refused to
allow her unless she were vaccinated. So she
forthwith had her arm punctured, but it didn’t
ta,ke. A second time she underwent the ordeal
and still no result. And as the official was ada-
mant she had to be parted from hubby after all.
“ Collins-street Congregational Church gave a
choir-concert last week, which caused a slight
rumpus with the municipal authorities. Admission
was by sixpenny tickets, and as the good folk were
tripping up the steps with their pasteboards ready
to hand in, they were met with the cheering news
that they could go in gratis, as a veto had been
placed upon the sale of seats in a church, it not
being a 'licensed place of amusement.’ The diffi-
culty, however, was dodged by the concert-pro-
moter announcing that the plate would go round
at half time, which it did, and the sixpences
honestly rattled in.
“Elliott Page, who plays the rejected Berenis
and the slighted second Mrs. Nap and such-like.
women scorned,’ says, laughingly, that she is
tired c? being repulsed on the stage and yearns
for a change—for a part ‘where somebody will
accept her.’ In real life she doesn’t suffer'much
from neglect, as she is a brilliant talker and
usually the centre of an interested group of listen-
ing men. She is—on ‘mamma’s’ side- of the same
family as John Elliott, learned in Red Indian
dialects, who missioned the Red men and first
translated the Bible into one of their guttural
languages.

The Coming of the Cold
AND THE DANGERS THAT IT BRINGS
TO SO VERY MANY.
Winter is coming !
Indeed, some of our great scientific men assert
that the approaching winter will be one of the
severest on record.
The winter is always dangerous, although too
many people forget this important fact. The
mildest winter causes more deaths than the hottest
summer. It has almost become a proverb that
“ cold weather kills'.” And why does it kill ?
Because so many people are in no condition to
pass through a severe winter. They complain of
tired feelings, they suffer from billiousness, pains
near the heart, headaches, backaches, and seem to
lack their customary energy. Cold weather,
instead of bracing them up, only makes them feel
languid, irritable, nervous and depressed.
And that is just where the danger lies. People
who feel like this are running some very grave
risks. The chances are that possibly 50 per cent,
of the people who suffer from the effects of
approaching winter will never see its end unless
they exercise the greatest care.
But there a way to avoid these dangers. By
building up the system, restoring healthy action
to the liver and kidneys, freeing the blood from
impurities and regaining renewed strength, the
penis of winter can be safely encountered. To do
this a suitable remedy must be taken, and there
is nothing for the purpose that can be compared
with Warner’s Safe Cure, because its effects are
immediate and lasting. As a means of restoring
lost vitality and promoting the general health this
grand remedy has never been equalled. That is
why it is so universally prescribed by physicians
and chemists, who know from experience that it
can always be depended upon. If you are suffer-
ing from any of the symptoms that denote weak-
ness you need to regain health, and Warner’s Safa
Cure will speedily help you to regain it.
PERSONAL ITEMS.
A rumor somehow got abroad in Sydney
last week that B. R. Wise might find himself ap-
pointed Chief-Justice of Tasmania.'
The Victorian estate of the late George
Robertson, publisher, is valued at =£119,370, and
is left absolutely to testator’s widow and children.
One is almost inclined to think that poor
Duncan Gillies dislocated his shoulder the other
day for the sake of the advt. Gillies was so much
in need of an ad.
Nat. Kinsman, the marrying auctioneer,
late of Fitzroy, Vic., cut up inconsiderably, after
all, leaving less than a thousand pounds. Yet he
married for money.
Professor David, of Sydney University,
has lately had flattering offers of scientific posts in
England. His Funafuti explorations delight the
disciples of Darwin, and have given his name a
European prestige.
Major Ferguson, Gov. Hampden’s son-in-
law, has, accordingtoaremark allegedly dropped by
his spouse on the eve of her departure, rosy hopes
of early returning to Australia in the capacity of
full-blown Governor
Lawyer Jones, who won the case for the
alluvial-diggers against the leaseholder, in W.A.,
the other clay, was once a practical miner. He
was one of the first to peg out a “ show ” on old
Pleasant Creek (Vic.) diggings.
Boyd D. Morehead, the only political mag-
nate put on trial for Q. N. Bank affairs, was for
years a clerk in the Bank of N.S. Wales, in
Sydney. His life, till past middle-age, was a
blend of Sydney and the Queensland bush.
Richard Seddon completed his fifth year
as Maoriland Premier on May 1. The longest
Maoriland Premiership was the five years and 40
days of Edward Stafford in 1856-61, and if Seddon
lasts till 11th June he will hold the M. L. record.

Lalor, J. B. Patterson, R. Le Poer French, John
Woods, J. M. Grant, and Major Smith—the other
members of that Cabinet—have all “ gone over. ,>

G. H. Reid does not like to be addressed
as “Dr.” Reid. But, if he didn’t want to be on
the level of Dr. Garran, or Dr. Cullen, or Dr. Sly,
he could have begged off. When Thomas Carlyle
was offered an LL.D., he said “ No ” emphatically,
and there was an end of it. There is something
trickily coy and vainly feminine in accepting a
distinguishing title in one breath, and in the next
whispering around, “ Oh, don’t say Doctor, say
George ! ”

papers may say. Yet, to the slightest criticism of
his actions, Fitz. may always be depended on to
reply with columns of slush, if not even with one

politan Board, Fitz. has allotted a huge volume
to the recording of all newspaper-references to the
board. Even the faintest allusion to it or any
of its members in the remotest backblocks rag is
carefully pasted into the gigantic tome. Even
borak poked at Fitz. as a poet is solemnly cut out
and entered up. The whole time of one clerk is
occupied searching the newspaper-files of all Aus-
tralia, and, in due time, the present par. will itself
figure in the ponderous record.
Piripi : “Idly re-reading Lawson’s
fine poem ‘ The Days when the World was
Wide,’ and pondering on its keynote of
juvenescent impatience o f humdrum
modernity, I bethought myself of a Syd-
ney acquaintance, a retired sea-captain,
who has crowded into the last decade of
this century a sufficiency of uncommon ad-
venture to satisfy the most ardent longer
for thrilling experience. Since 1892 this
old sea-dog has commanded a vessel in
the Queensland labor-trade, has had many
hair-breadth escapes whilst illicitly running
cargoes of weapons to Cuba for the insur-
gents, and has taken a shipload of convicts
from Havana to Spain, this trip being
spiced with a good old mutiny and a satis-
fying amount of bloodshed. Tinally, he
commanded the ship which conveyed An-
dree and his balloon to the White North
as a preliminary to his hare-brained trip to
the Pole. A good and varied record for
the prosaic Nineties.”

N.S.W. American Consul Bell as a prophet
(on the declaration of war): “If our folks take
the business in hand they’ll just put it through,
slick, and get home to breakfast.” Report of the
Manila fight: “Hostilities ceased for about two
hours, during which the American crews had
breakfast.” Breakfast is obviously a very import-
ant circumstance in Yankee estimation.
Sergeant Nelson, one of the Cootamundra
bunch mixed up in a trouble which is the subject
of a criminal prosecution, died suddenly the other
day—a habit which John Norton’s adversaries or
victims appear generally to cultivate. Thirty-four
years ago Nelson’s father, also a policeman, was
shot at Collector by Dunn, the bushranger. Dunn
was hanged at Darlinghurst on Christmas Eve.
1865.
A pretty story goes round the papers that
General Kitchener, the commander-in-chief in the
Soudan, is the son of a poor farmer in Tasmania,
where his sister still keeps a village-school; and
young Australia is further invited to consider that
it was just hard work which opened the way for
the farmer’s boy to Sandhurst, and thence, step-
by-step, to his present billet. Just as a matter-of-
fact, Kitchener’s father was a lieut.-colonel, and
his training-place was Woolwich.
Clement Wragge writes to the Brisbane
Courier announcing his intention to henceforth
divorce poetry from meteorology, keep rhetorical
fireworks and cyclones strictly apart, and other-
wise conduct himself as a good, staid, respect-
able weather-prophet ought to. Clement naively
admits that press criticism and remonstrances of
friends have driven him to this desperate ex-
tremity. Henceforward Wragge will be plain,
scientific, dull, and uninteresting.

It is settled that Tom Mann is to come out
when Tillett gets back to England. A manly,
fearless, downright fellow is Mann, with all Til-
lett’s enthusiasm, and other qualities of which the
hard little nut cannot boast. His personal worth
was indicated by the choice of him by Lord Salis-
bury’s Government to serve on the British Labor
Commission. There was some talk of his becom-
ing a parson a few years ago, and he has always
been a bit soft on the religious side, but it didn’t
come off.

claiming, “Norton actually employing counsel
against Hagey-Caulfield’s application for a “show-

Not much ! The matter simply is that Norton—
