Since the subjoined was written by Thb
Bulletin’s London correspondent, the terrific
crash in Bottomley’s Westralian and Northern
Territory mining scrip has much altered that
doughty and well-advertised plunger’s financial
position
Horatio Bottomley, the latest reputed millionaire, told
me he is going to take the Derby with Hawfinch, and
very likely the Two Thousand Guineas also beforehand.
The “ West Australian Market Trust” man is a mysteri-
ous marvel. Originally a parliamentary shorthand re-
porter, he has seen extraordinary vicissitudes, and after
many reverses at last struck it so rich that he was able to
give as a gift a quarter of a million to the disappointed
shareholders of the collapsed “ Hansard Union.” They
had neither a legal nor even a moral claim on him, and it
seems a big sum to pay for an advt. Bottomley has a
strong facial resemblance to the late Charles Bradlaugh,
who was a near relative, though there is much difference
in the stature of the two. In manner he is easy and
keenly intelligent, with very broad views, and intends
standing for Parliament shortly in the Liberal interest.
His ambitions when put into words are appalling, though
given a continuance of wealth and health and he may
attain them all, and he is blessed with the attribute that
Bismarck loves so well —he is a magnificent hater. He is
now going for success on the turf, then for the House,
and up a ladder of illimitable possibilities.
Q. : “Have just re-read ‘The Ballad of
Reading Gaol ” for the fourth time. What a
blood-curdling piece of realism ! and how forceful
compared with the ballads of the library, or those
not so intimately inspired—Kipling’s ‘ Danny
Deever,’ for instance ! Only men who have come
in close contact with the condemned can com-
pletely feel all it contains. Oscar Wilde's sen-
tence has been a crucifixion and a resurrection.
Petty pietists will never understand the true
meaning of the process as experienced so tragi-
cally by him —
How else but through a broken heart
May Lord Christ enter in ?
He will give us no more epigrammatic plays, no
mere cynicism, decadent philosophy, and aesthetic-
ism. Contact with human degradation, suffering
and sorrow have made the scoffing poet human.
Every sane intellectual man who undergoes im-
prisonment is similarly affected in due degree.
The old Oscar is dead, dead, dead ; and a new,
clean, purified and sane Oscar lives in his stead.
He is the Penitent Thief of the Decadents.

LAGER B€€R.
Aboriginalities.
The recent appointment in Queensland of
an Aborigines’ Protector has caused quite a boom
in the mixed matrimonial market in the lar
North and the Never-Never. It must be men-
tioned, for the benefit of the uninitiated, that the
abovementioned Protector has power to take
the lonely or persecuted aboriginal female under
his wing and trot her off to a reservation. Now,
numbers of Queensland station-hands live with
the native black women, and are often fairly
happy with their ebony brides and their saffron
offspring. Consequently the new law has stirred
them up to take measures for the securing of
their domestic treasures against these ravening
wolves of Aborigines’ Protectors, and _ several
dusky Kitties have lately donned a wedding-ring
“allee same white Mary.”
• * * *
During the recent dry season an ‘ ‘ Out-
Back ” blackfellow, the soles of whose feet had
begun to crack with the heat, bought a packet of
boot-protectors and hammered eight or nine into
the sole of each foot. They stopped the wear-
and-tear admirably, and he now declares that no
nig. should be without a packet.
* * * •
At a woolshed near Condobolin, last
season, there arrived a traveller who “ chucked a
scranny ” (pretended to ha,ve paralysed arms),
and “ had ” the sympathising shearers to the
extent of £B. As his arms were useless he got
them tied into a belt round his waist, and after
thanking the shed-hands with tears in his eyes,
he set out for the nearest township to take train
to his poor anxious wife and family in Sydney. A
few days later one of the shearers visited the
township and found the whole of the inhabitants
gathered outside a pub. in the main street col-
lected round the “scranny” gentleman afore-
said, who was trying to climb a lamp-post feet-
first, with his swag in his teeth and a billy hung
on each ear, for drinks all round. The shearer at
once rolled into him on behalf of his absent mates,
but “Scranny” used his paralysed “dooks”
with deadly effect, made a most unholy wreck of
the avenger, and proceeded with his climbing
amidst the cheers of the townsfolk.

About Taunton’s dog. Taunton’s dog was
a bitch, a retriever, large and black, and Taunton
declared that for years he had been looking for a
man who knew as much as Elo and was half as
honest. Flo was intelligent (if you really want an
intelligent dog, catch a black retriever young, and
make what you like of it), but her honesty was
nothing to brag about. She was honest to Taun-
ton, however, and that is how we understand
honesty as a general thing. It was Flo’s ability
as a thief that made her most useful to her owner.
Taunton was working on the roads near Hamilton,
and living under canvas with a party of three,
and it was Flo’s duty to keep that party pretty
well supplied with fresh meat. _ There were two
butchers’ shops within half-a-mile, and Flo drew
on both for supplies with the greatest impartiality.
Taunton gave his dog the office, and she trotted
off, and returned with meat in five minutes, or an
hour, or two hours. The dog knew enough to
watch her opportunity, and sometimes the oppor-
tunity was a long time coming. One day 110
went off for meat, and did not return. Next day
Taunton went hunting, but nobody had seen 110,
nobody had heard of her. Later, however, T. got
a clue, and v/hen next Dwyer, the butcher, called
there were bitter accusations and bad language.
Stung by profane abuse, Dwyer spoke up at last:
“ Know them sausages you had Thursday ’ Liked
them, didn’t you? You had more on Friday.
You’ve just paid for ’em. Damn you ! them
sausages was Flo. Now I’m even.' 1 Taunton
called for a ring and buckets for two, and when he
was knocked chin-up in the fifth round it was felt
that butcher Dwyer had just about squared the
ledger. ,

C. : “Can’t- say about snake-killing, but
the jackass is a most expert monser. In the
passion-vine round my verandah there is a colony
of mice, and every day Jack sits on the top of an
adjacent tree with his dull, deceptive eye fixed
upon the creeper. From time to time he makes a
lightning dart, drives his beak into a mass of
leaves and brings forth a shrieking mouse. A tap
or two on a handy log quietens it before it dis-
appears down Jack’s throat. Then his
tail and he is ready for the next victim.”
* * • *
Ellendale : “If I hadn’t had a large ex-
perience of gohanna hunting I should be writing
to say that all of them turn back-somersaults
when overtaken by a dog. My sheep-slut (just
deceased through taking a ‘ bait ’) was death on
all gohannas, and killed hundreds of all sizes
without getting a scratch, until she met her
Waterloo in a big yellow fellow who, just as she
was about to snatch him, turned a back somer-
sault and ran the other way. She coursed him
backwards and forwards, with the same somer-
sault at each turn, until I rode on and left her at
it. When she rejoined me her was cocked
sideways and covered with blood. The acrobatic
gohanna is a new thing to me and evidently
puzzled the slut. Can anyone illuminate the
subject? ”

Bronze wing:
White ants—
numerous in
Illawarra dis-
trict — used to
confine them-
selves strictly
to timber as an
article of diet,
but since the
coal-mines star-
ted so much
timber has been
used in them
that the supply
isn’t enough for
the white - ant
population.
Last week the
fettlers on a col-
liery branch-
line, while ex-
amining a rail,
noticed that
white powder
dropped from it
when struck
with a hammer,
and, on investi-
gating further,
found that a
colony of white-
ants had made
a nest in what
had originally
been a flaw in
the rail. It had
become so
weakened by
the insects that
it had to be
replaced. A
flake of metal,
about a foot
long, came
away from the
web of the rail
when pulled.
Looks from this
as if the ants
are dropping to
the fact that
timber will soon
be unavailable
for their nests,
and are sending
colonies to
build nests in
the materia
that is super-1
seding wood, so that coming generations may
possess jaws fit to nibble iron, and digestive organs
that will find nutritive properties in the metal.
* • * *
A great race happened once between the
Exton-Deloraine (Tas.) mail-train and an old grey
horse. The moke got the start and kept it to the
finish. For the first half-mile the driver pitied
the gallant steed and steadied down to allow him
to retire with safety and honor ; but each time the
engine slowed the ancient would look back con-
temptuously and kind of beckon with his old
plume of a tail in a way
that made the driver mad.
Then the engineer would
blow the whistle and shove
up the lever hard and rattle
down the line after the
gee-gee at express speed ;
and the way that beast
would scratch along with
his tail up and his nose
down, and the gravel
squirting off his toes like
hailstones, would have
made old Carbine burst
with envy. At the third
mile he was still going-
strong, and in the final
straight the driver opened
the front steam-pipes and
raised an unearthly blast
on the whistle. Then the
old grey laid himself out
and they couldn’t see him
for gravel and lather.
The Deloraine people woke
up when they saw a streak
of grey horse skim past
with his eye sticking nine
inches out of his head and
the hair all blown off his
tail. He went up to the
nearest stable, followed by
the wholepopulation. Here
he stopd one moment exul-
tant ; then gradually sank
down upon his poor old
knees, breathed a prayer
or two, and, with a sweet
smile of contentment on
his face, rolled over and
expired. Now, in Delor-
aine, when you recount any
great racing feat, some old
weather-beaten battler will
turn over his quid and
remark: “Yes, but yer
should have seen the old
grey hoss bate the injine.”

Dear Bulletin. —A
bush “ matchbox trick.”
Lender shakes box when
lending; borrower gets
light, then drops full box
into the palm of hand, holding an empty one
between htumb and finger, knuckles upward.
Shakes his hand ; matches in palmed box rattle,
but empty box is handed to lender and pocketed
unsuspiciously.— Malua.
* * * *
Werrimee : “J.G.’s” argument re dingo
amounts merely to a contention that as he has
seen the hungry one disdainfully pass newly
killed flesh, no one else can have ever seen him do
otherwise ; and the accounts of thousands being
poisoned are simply all lies. The one that
“wrestled” with the maggots was observed by
two others as well as myself, on Knapman’s selec-
tion, near Casino. In that part, teeming with
game, a dingo has often been known to pick up
rotten meat when fresh was easy to get. Not long
ago, also, I saw two dingoes quietly shepherd in;/ a
flock of sheep, whilst a third dodged about waiting
TWO FEET ONE YARD .
SHE (after forty minutes' walk): “ I thought the policeman said it was only
ten minutes' walk.”
HE: “ My dear, you forget the size of the policeman's feet!"
to get a lamb on the outside. This shepherding
propensity is well-known about here, and man 5
squatters try to cross with the dingo on that ac-
count. Again, twice last week, I caught dingoes
rounding up sheep at noon; and I can show the
sceptical the trodden places where mobs were
rung. I was born among dingoes, have been
among ’em ever since, and have never yet known
one to go straight at anything. He’s naturally a
prowler, and his bent is to hang around for
chances. The calf-snatching strategy was wit-
nessed by myself and three drovers on the Mara-
noa, where the occurrence is common. Another
instance. J. Toohey (ex-manager of Taroma
Station, Q.) and a stockman came up to two
dingoes harassing a cow with a young calf on
Paradise Creek, Taroma. They cracked then-
whips to drive them off, when 20 others sprang
out of the long grass, forming almost a complete
circle. They hadn’t their eyes on Toohey, for a
rise hid him from view till within 20 yards.

C.C. : “ Re dingoes, ‘ J.G.’ seems to dog-
matise on the strength of a single experience.
Unfortunately, I have too many opportunities for
acquiring dingo knowledge. I can confirm Wei-
rimee’s ’ statement that they ‘ round-up sheep,
and even drive them some distance before attack-
ing. I have seen one dingo go ahead of a mob of
sheep and drive them back upon another follow-
ing up behind. ‘ J.G.’ says they k never perform
in the evening, 5 but I have known them often to
kill at sundown. They certainly do not mvari-
ably w.ait till night ’; one may hear them howling
to attract their mates at night, but the favorite
time for killing is daybreak, when the sheep are
moving off camp. I notice that on misty, drizzl-
ing mornings local dingoes are always on the job.
I may mention that in spite of all the dodges to
disguise the ‘human aroma,’ I have never suc-
ceeded in poisoning a true dingo, and I’ve tried
everything, from fat run on to a pegged
skin to sardine-oil. I’ve caught tcune dogs in art-
fully-concealed traps with a trail of valerian ovei
them, but never a dingo. I find lead the only
sure remedy.”

“ R.” ( r L\as.) : “ Old Man’s ” remarks in a
recent Bulletin re kangaroos are correct. They
do not touch the ground with their tails when
going at full speed. Should they scent danger,
they give two or three jumps, with a pause be-
tween each, alighting on the feet and tail simul-
taneously, thus causing the heavy “ thud,” which
is never heard otherwise. They use the tail as a
balance-pole (vide “ Old Man ”), and as a rudder
when swimming; likewise to brush the flies
off. (Also it makes good soup.) A kangaroo
will creep into a grain-paddock as noiselessly as a
bandicoot, but, when satisfied, clears off in a
jaunty manner with an insolent flourish of his
tail. You can’t beat an old brush-buck much for
cunning. He will allow his mate to crawl first
into a feeding-patch to test the safety of the locale,
whilst he gammons to keep watch just inside the
scrub. The way to nab the old stager is to let the
doe feed quietly and pot him when he follows her,
which he will soon do unless he smells trouble,
when he will utter a note like the roar of a stag ;
then pot the doe, or you will lose both. A rut-
ting doe will attract the bucks for miles.
Then a shindy happens, and the best
man gets her. If you shoot him , the doe
returns next night to make enquiries, and
if unsuccessful leans up against a stump and
weeps. If you shoot her the buck doesn t happen
around again but seeks a new spouse. He is a
philosopher. A good plan to shoot a kangaroo is
to get on top of a stump not less than 10ft from
the ground. He never looks and cannot scent
you at that height. I once got into a beautifully-
placed stump and was preparing to take a sight at
an old buck when I suddenly got up and left. I
was calmly sitting on a nest of bull-ants. I saw,
one moonlight night, a party of eight kangaroos
dancing a set of Caledonians. I hoy had chosen a

H.F. : “Amen to ‘Compton’ on pollard
as diet; it is all he claims for it, and its general
use would ruin the pill-trade. A mixture of one
part pollard to two of flour makes a grand loal.
My own invention is the addition of sugar and
making the dough into buns ; they fill a void
under the belt like a charm. Also, pollard bread,
like whole meal, keeps well. pollard is best.
• * * *
In che desert country ('W.A.) where
often no water is to be found three hours after
rain, the blacks scoop out a little crescent-shaped
tree-limb, and sink it in a clay-pan till the ends
are level with the surface, then cover it carefully
TE KEEPA (Major Kemp), a lately-deceased
Maori warrior.
Photo., Kinsey & Co., Wellington (M.L.).
to prevent evaporation. This is done at about ten-
mile intervals, and the limb is dug up when
wanted and gently tilted to run the water out.
Diggers on many western fields serve the same
purpose by sinking hollow logs, blocked at the
bottom, perpendicularly in the ground.

Plover •. ‘ Saw an ox-conductor lately
driving his team across a flooded creek. Bullocks
all under water ; but bullocky didn’t seem to mind
much. He cut with his whip at an extra large
bubble and yelled, ‘ Smoker, you ! going to
sleep there, are you ? You’d loaf on your (blanky)
road down a precipice, you would.’ ”
• * ♦ •
He was one of the liveliest residents of a
town which was very lively in the old days, but
Cupid “ pinked ” him, and he entered on a course
of reformation much to the disgust of his
quondam boon companions. One evening, for no
particular reason, the township started on a howl-
ing drunk, and while the night was yet young the
reformed one was missed, and was sought for high
and low without success. By and by “ the boys ”
learnt that he had been seen making for the court
house (a very primitive building) with what ap-
peared to be a bundle of bedding under his arm.
A secret meeting of the “ Royal Silvertail Push ”
was at once called, and plans laid “to nip this
sort of thing in the bud.” Within ten minutes two
men in a buggy left the “ swell ” hotel yard. At
the same moment four men entered the hall of
justice (per window) and pounced upon the sleep-
ing escapee. It was a hot summer’s night and
his toilet scanty in consequence. A shawl was
tied round his head —a sheet covered the rest and
a couple of saddle-straps held the parcel together.
He was carried out to the aforementioned buggy
and away it went. The four “sc.rufflers” then
separated, and soon after. “ the whisper ’’ went
round that the captive “ mid nodings on ” might
be seen “for one night only,” whereupon the
whole male population turned out to enjoy the
“wanderer’s return.” He came—fleeing like, a
hunted hare and sore distressed by his 3-mile
canter from the “dumping” spot. The town-
ship’s ecstatic reminiscences of that spectacle
finally drove the “ exhibit ” out of the district.

Robbery, etc-
Dear Bulletin. —Why are postal rates so com-
plicated? What is really gained? I only know the
Maoriland scale, but just consider it. Letters, 4d. the
ounce ; books, 4d. the lb. That is plain, and not unfair.
But then go on to the complication of parcel post—6d. the
first lb., 3d. each succeeding lb. ; book post, jd-the first
2oz. ; commercial papers, Id. the first 4oz. And outside
Maoriland a printed paper under 2oz. is id. anywhere,
while the same matter type-written is 2id. Could we not
simplify the whole, without much loss of revenue, into
two classes, “closed” and “open?” The “closed’ matter
would include what we now call “letters, which may
only be opened by special warrant, and may not be de-
layed ; 2d. the ioz.' should carry them anywhere “ Open
matter would include every kind of parcel, book, and
non-private manuscript, liable to be opened and, in case
of pressure of business, delayed. This could g° every-
where at id. the 20z., as “ books ”do now. The limit of
weight is also very complicated, differing m different
countries for books, letters, parcels, samples, &c. Surely
one limit would do for all ?—lgnotus.
* * * *
Maoriland talks of putting an export duty
on kauri-gum, which, it is believed, is now the only
much-used substitute for copal in the manufacture of
varnish. Kauri-gum is only found in the Auckland pro-
vincial district, its habitat being from the latitude of
T ake Taupo northwards. Thirty years ago gum was
largely used by the settlers as a fire-kindler, and could be
bought from the Maoris at about a couple of shillings a.
sack. Now the gumdiggers receive from £4O to £5O a ton
for it at the bush-stores, and it is believed it costs the
varnish-makers as much again.

Out at Bargowlahbudgeree (Q.) the Q.N. Bank
has a branch. It used to be a hot shop once, and the
manager, a timid kind of chappie, was often bullied into
bad business. So they sent out a rough-and-tumble
manager, who ran things better. When the inspector
came to visit him, he found the pug manager’s safe full of
various tins containing coin. He was short £l6. Let 8
see,” said the p.m., “ there must be another sardine-tm-
full somewhere.” And there was.
• * * *
Sydney G.P.O. now sells British penny stamps
for the convenience of people remitting small sums. You
can buy 2s. worth of British stamps for 2s. Bd., or Is.
worth for Is. 2d.—at least that.was the experience of one
recent purchaser. So he naturally bought two lots of Is.
worth each, and left the flabbergasted clerk struggling to
decide whether 6d. worth of stamps would be 6|d. or 10|d.
* * * *
In the early days of Coolgardie a noted expert
was asked to report on an alleged gold reef. On arrival
at the property he was shown by the man in charge over
a Godforsaken-looking piece of ground entirely devoid of
any promising features. “ But where is your blessed
reef ? ” he asked at last, and the reply was a comprehen-
sive arm-sweep that took in apparently the whole lease.
“Oh, I see!” was the rejoinder, “and the sky’s your
hanging-wall.” * * *

The export of Victorian butter alone this season
ehows £409,000 decrease as compared with last season’s
shipments of the same commodity. Also, very large
sums indeed have this season been dropped by every
shipper to England of tinned rabbits.
It’s not so easy to get safely away with bullion
stolen from the closely-guarded strong-rooms of ocean-
going steamers. A person under suspicion in connection
with a mysterious robbery which occurred some time
ago is constantly shadowed by detectives. An itching
to handle the spoil is certain to put him away sooner
or later, if guilty, and-the co. means to have revenge,
even at the cost of thousands in cash and years of strict
surveillance.

• * • *
R. L. Nash, financial editor of the Sydney
Daily Telegraph, has issued the Australasian Joint Stock
Companies Year Book. It gives a complete official record
of all investments, furnishes a list of all the mining cos.
with data as to capital, production, divs., &c. It is by
far the best book of the kind published, and should be in
the hands of every broker and speculator.
* * • *
To the many hundreds who have gone bung
got out of their liabilities either by compromise, insol-
vency or fraudulent “ statutory declarations—the last
banking crisis is forgotten, but the remorselessness with
which poor wretches, who have hitherto met all calls, are
pursued for the balance of their liabilities, both to banks
and trading concerns, is appalling. The conscientious
man is being made the victim of those who were rogues
enough to “get out” at any cost—perjury or fraudulent
compromises were the most favored methods. So long as
an honest man has a cent, he has to pay to the uttermost
farthing. “ Honesty is the best policy ! ” Not much !

charged a jury in a libel-case in this fashion :“ My own
opinion is that the man who honestly criticises pro-
spectuses does a great public service. When you remem-
her the large number of wrecks enquired into by public
officials you will see how enormously the public have been
imposed upon by what are called ‘ wild-cat schemes, ana
you are entitled to afford every protection to the
journalist who honestly, in the discharge of his duty to
the public, exposes such ventures.” Phew ! When will
an Australian Judge talk in this strain .

Jarrahdale Timber co. (writes a Bulletin cor-
respondent) is one of the oldest W. A. commercial institu-
tions, and at present one of the most prosperous. Has
absolute possession of a tremendous tract of country,
paying a paltry annual £5O rental. Owns about 40 miles
of railway with rolling stock and wharves, &c., at Rock-
ingham Bay—a harbor only second to that of Albany.
Employs over 300 men, and pays, say, £5OOO a month in
■wages. In the early part of its history the co. failed to
make ends meet, but of late years it has progressed
wonderfully, the Perth building boom giving it a big
lift. It must have been a splendid investment for the
shareholders—George and John Whittingham (Melb.),
Neil M‘Neil (Perth), Reid (R. Reid and Co.), and M Lean
<M‘Lean Bros, and Rigg).
All the big W. A. timber co.’s have been floated
in London. Miller’s, of Torbay, got a big lead of the
rest, and through plentiful advertising and a judicious
selection of imposing names for the directorate scored
heavily. The Jarrahdale co., probably Australasia’s best-
equipped timber station, was next, and several others
have followed suit. Jarrah is a shade better wood than
Karri, but the Torbay people more than equalise matters
by lower quotes and smart “ pushing” in foreign markets.
This timber-trade means a big thing for the land of W ait-
a-while. Latest shipments of Jarrah —90,000 sleepers for
Port Elizabeth and 50,000 for East London, South Africa.
Orders on hand—loo,ooo sleepers for Calcutta, and 250,000
blocks for Colombo.
In Campbelltown (N.S.W.j church-yard a
monument bears testimony to the “ many virtues
of Thomas Reddall, J.P., as a minister of Our

Judging from the accounts now available,
the great sea-fight at Manila was just the sort of
row with just the sort of ending that might have
been expected, when the most wide-awake nation
on earth meets the nation that is fast asleep and
dreaming of Cortez and the Cid and the glories
of the old Conquistadores. The Spaniards forgot
to lay mines in the channel. They forgot to keep
a look-out. It was discovered at the last moment
that their torpedoes were useless for want of cer-
tain fixings which couldn’t be obtained nearer
than Europe. Their guns were old and obsolete.
Their magazines were planted conspicuously on a
hill where the first shell could blow their ammu-
nition to Tophet. Their ships were old and feeble
—some of them wooden, and the fires were out,
and there was no steam in the boilers, so that even
the ancient craft that they had couldn’t move to
meet the agile Americans. The only thing that
was ready was the ever-reliable grit of the men,
who apparently fought their utterly hopeless fight
to the bitter end. But the 15th century has no
show when it knocks up against the 19th nowa-
days.
In the waiting-rooms at manyN.S.W. rail-
way-stations the fenders are made of portions of
disused steel-rails, liberally covered with Bruns-
wick black. Economical—and few people recog-
nise the deception.
In a Cape Colony court a defendant re-
cently pleaded that he was not liable for his wife's
board-bill of <£2l odd, as they were living
separately. Said the beak: “But plaintiff says
you live in the same house, eat at the same table,
and sleep in the same bed with the lady.” “That
is true,” admitted hubby; “but then, you see,
she finds all the cash. I’m out of work, and you
can’t say my wife is legally living with me when
she is earning her own living. If I’m without a
cent of my own I can’t possibly be ‘ keeping
her ! ” And the flabbergasted beak gave it up.

Demos: “‘Eureka Stockade,’ again. A
vivid account is given, at length, in Marcus
Clarke’s ‘ For the Term of His Natural Life.
‘ How ? ’ says the reader, ‘ I cannot find it in the
book.’ Which leads me to prophesy that the
work must be published complete some day, as
Clarke wrote it. Only two-thirds appears in the
book. The introductory portion was slashed off,
and the remainder truncated, in deference to an
omniscient and omnipotent Mr. Bentley, of Lon-
don. Hence whatever there is of rag-jag and
cobble. Yet the whole was twice published in
the Australian Journal during Clarke’s life, and
for the third time after his death. Conclusive
evidence was thus afforded that Clarke’s work as
a whole was worthy of its author.”
The manager of Spiers and Ponds’ central
depCt, London, states that whisky-and-soda is far
and away the most popular restaurant drink
throughout Great Britain. Also, Scotch whiskies
are knocking the Irish brands into oblivion. The
various gins—Old Tom, unsweetened (otherwise
Dry) and Hollands —maintain a steady circulation,
but the flow of rum and sherry diminishes
rapidly. The history of bar drinking, however,
gives many instances of startling revivals in cer-
tain liquor lines. Five-and-twenty years ago
“three of Irish ” came to be a popular cant order
at every London pub. “ Threeofirish.” Then, in
a moment of inspiration, perhaps, a representative
of the opposition whisky industries started the
cry of “ Seotchandsoda,” which has been increas-
ing in volume ever since.
Hardly anybody would, at a glance, take
the portrait in this issue of Tim Bulletin for that
of a full-blooded Maori. Major Kemp, known as
Taitako, up Wanganui way, and deriving his
Anglicised name from “Te Keepa,” did a lot of
fighting for the pakeha against the Maori warriors
of the early days, the medals for which are seen
on his breast. The sword by his side, a present
from Queen Victoria, is inscribed “For unfailing
loyalty and valor.” “Loyalty ”is a pretty elastic
word, but it seems a good bit overstretched in the
case of a man who fights tooth-and-nail for the
invader against his own kith and kin. It says a
lot for Maori apathy that Kemp was, after all,
allowed to die in his bed. Te Keepa was the son
of Tanguru and Reremaki, with a mana extending
throughout Wanganui, down to the much-con-
tested Horowhenua arid Papaitonga Lakes. Only
the day before Te Keepa died the Maori Appellate
Court justified his conduct in connection with the
Horowhenua Block transaction, a result which is
said to have killed the old man (aged 75) with joy
or astonishment. So useful was Te Keepa to the
Eakehas in the war that General Whitmore said
e was worth 200 men in the field, and he was
repeatedly mentioned for feats of valor. (Curious
fora Maori chief to get “ mention ” in British
despatches about engagements with his country-
men.) When the country between Wellington
and Wanganui was overrun by hostile war parties,
Te Keepa carried the mails through single-handed.
Major Kemp declined a seat in the Leg. Council,
saying he would rather win a seat in the Lower
House by contest. His death-bed advice to his
tribe was--“ Sell no more land. Keep what you
have for our own people.”
Mr. “Jack” Russell, of Newcastle,
N.S.W., the hero who carried the life-line
ashore at the Maitland wreck, is a burlj 7
man of the type universally associated
with ideas of bravery. Quiet, unassuming
and self-consoious, he resents the publicity
given to his name over what he appears to
consider a mere matter of course, and, at
present, his time is fully occupied in dodg-
ing interviewei's. Mr. Russell was a pas-
senger by the Maitland, and, after seeing
two men drowned in the attempt, he suc-
ceeded in reaching the shore with a line.
After almost miraculously escaping death
in the first instance, he repeatedly risked
life and limb while successfully helping
many people to shore. It is intended to
present him with a testimonial, and to urge
his claim to the Albert medal. Those
who know him expect to see him disappear
for a time to escape these well-meant
honors.
Dear Bulletin . Kioto has been
tin? capital of Japan for 11 centuries. The
former capital was N.igaoka, and Kioto
was made the capital by the Emperor
Kwaimtm. The Imperial Palace is there;
the last national exhibition was held there,
three years ago; and, although Tokio is
now politically and commercially the capi-
tal, Kioto is still inspected by the older
class of Japanese as the representative
Japanese city. As the hydraulic and elec-
trical engineering works of the city beat
anything in Sydney, I fail to see how it can
be considered an “ unknown town.” Also,
the name has not been changed to “Sai-
kio,” as you state, but an eastern quarter
of the city is now called “Okasaki.” —H.X.
[“ H.X.” says that Kioto “has been the
capital for 11 centuries.” Then he admits
that Tokio (which is four times the size
of Kioto) “is now” the capital. Well,
The Bulletin said it was.—Ed. B.
E.J.B. : “Interesting to watch banana-
shipping in Suva, Fiji. The steamer anchors out
in the harbor, and is quickly surrounded by a
flotilla of small craft of all shapes, two and three
deep. All the hatches are worked at once, and
the bunches come up one at a time, handed on by
natives perched on stages up the ship’s side and
down the hatch. The shipper’s agent, standing
over the hatches, keeps his eye open for bad
bunches, which he rejects with an angry “ Beu
Ongo ” (throw out that one !), at the same time
counting the accepted bunches with a sheep-tally
machine. A bunch often passes through 20 pairs
of hands from the small craft to the hold. The
A.U.S.N. and Union Co.’s steamers take away
every fortnight some 15 to 20 thousand bunches.
Fiji is one of the few countries in the world that
produce bananas all the year round, and in this
respect has the pull over Queensland, which has
“ seasons.”

Scot: “When Bessemer’s steel first was
made, I was present at a seance of shipbuilders
and engineers in Glasgow'. Everyone except old
Randolph, of Randolph and Elder, and John
Fulton, of the Lancefield Forge, pronounced it
«Crude, very crude, and unworkable for ship-
building.’ James Rodger, of Smith and Rodger,
said it would improve and eventually make rolled
plates. These three were right. One retired,
took to fly-fishing and hanged the ‘ tred ’ (trade);
another died mad on steam-hammers; the third
sold his yard, also took to fishing and a kayrich
(carriage); but Bessemer’s steel lives in many
improved forms.”
The Fatherhood of the China-
man.
Dear Bulletin,—A few months ago I pointed
out in Sydney D.T. that much of the prejudice
against the Chinese rested on illogical bases —
that they were law-abiding and thrifty ; that they
were producers of wealth from the soil which
would not be produced but for their industry and
Chinese half-caste girl, aged IT. Musician and
singer.
Photo., Osborne Bros., Sydney.
skill, and that their standard of living was high,
and their consumption of European food and
clothing much greater than was generally sup-
posed. Not long afterwards the S.M. Herald
contended that, though this might be true, the
racial deterioration which followed the mixture of
Mongolian and Caucasian blood was sufficient
argument against any mitigation of the policy of
restriction of Chinese immigration. Now, having
numerous friends and acquaintances amongst both
Chinese and Chinese half-castes, and knowing that
the European mothers of many of these half-castes
sprang from a very debased class, it had often been
a matter of surprise to me that the social condition
and educational status of their mixed offspiring
was so high ; and I had arrived at the conclusion
that this apparent anomaly could be only due to
the care and self-sacrifice of their Chinese fathers.
The majority of the girls are educated at
expensive schools, and a half-caste young
woman who does not possess the usual accom-
plishments of the daughters of well-to-do
Australians is rare. The piano and the
American organ are common articles of furniture
in the homes of the half-castes, and the frequency
of illuminated scriptural texts as adornments of
their dwellings marks a “ serious-mindedness ”
somewhat remarkable to the uninitiated. Know-
ing, however, that my ipse dixit would not be
accepted unreservedly by a public strongly pre-
judiced against aliens of any kind, I determined
to see what independent evidence was collectable
upon the subject. The statistical publications of
N.S. Wales throw no special light upon the
matter, but the census returns show that at last
enumeration there were 867 of these hybrids in
N.S. Wales, and that of the white wives of China-
men, 61 per cent, were Australians. There were
other official sources of information available,
however. Further, in reply to an inquiry on the
point, Police-Inspector-General Fosbery stated
that there were no half-caste prostitutes in the
metropolitan district, and I happen to know from
Chinese sources that Mr. Fosbery made careful
investigation before answering my query. And,
when it is remembered that there are nearly 300
half-caste women in the metropolitan district the
antecedents of whose mothers would, in the
majority of cases, be extremely dubious,
this is valuable evidence in favor of these
people. Also, putting actual prostitution aside,
the Deputy Registrar-General states, in reply! to
an inquiry as to the prevalence of illegitimacy
amongst Chinese half-castes, that, though prob-
ably some Chinese half-caste girls have English
names, “no women with Chinese names have been
registered as the mothers of illegitimate children
in Sydney from Ist January last (1897) to date.
And, as half-castes almost invariably .adopt the
paternal (Chinese) surname, it may be taken for
granted that illegitimacy is almost unknown
amongst them. Mr. Sidney, secretary to the
Charity Organisation Society, also informs me
that, in 19 years, his society has not had
a single application for admission to any lying-
in-home on behalf of any Chinese half-caste, or for
charitable relief of any kind by a Chinese or
Chinese half-caste.
The standing figure is a Chinese half-caste. Has
won prizes shearing, and last season “ rung”
several sheds in the western district.
Mr. F. H. Galbraith, police-magistrate at Hay,
who was for some years resident at Shoal-
haven, sent me word that the half-castes in that
district were “ all very respectable and well con-
ducted.” Mr. W. Seymour Wells, formerly road-
superintendent in the Shoalhaven district, informs
me further that young half-caste women were m
great demand there as general servants, and com-
manded a higher wage than their European sisters
of the cap-and-apron.
So conclusive, indeed, is the evidence of the
half-caste women’s general morality that I thought
their freedom from vice might possibly be due to
some congenital lack of sexual vitality, aim I
communicated this doubt to a medical man hold-
ing a high position in the Sydney University.
He replied with characteristic caution as follows:
“The freedom from vice of Chinese half-caste
women compares favorably with what is com
monly believed of half-caste women of other races,
and it would not be safe in the present state of
knowledge to attribute it to a lack of sexual
vitality. The questions involved are very compli-
cated, and an enquiry into the fecundity, fertility,
and sterility of Chinese half-caste women both
here and in China would well repay the trouble it
would entail.”
Aspiring physiologists have here a new field of
research ; but, as for myself, I had, perforce, to be
satisfied with the appended “fertility” statistics
of ten families the mothers of which are half-castes.
No of „ •, No. of
Family. Children. Family. Children.

Had statistics dealing with women of maturer
nge be ;n available, it would probably be found
that the fertility of the half-caste mother
exceeds that of the average white mother; and,
consequently, a high standard of morality may be
legitimately assumed for the Chinese half-caste
women.
N.S. Wales.
J. A. PHILP.
Land of the Crocodile.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
In a retired spot, a few miles out of Bris-
bane, lives an old fellow who was a lieutenant in
the 55th lowa Regiment, at the battle of Vicks-
burg. The men of the 55th lowa were known as
the 41 Fighting Methodists,” and, after pitohing-
in like devils at Vicksburg, they retired
Family Group.—Father Chinese, mother Chinese
half-caste.
Photo., Lemaire, Sydney.
and held a thanksgiving service ! Says the old
man referred to : “We smote the enemy with
the sword of Gideon, and then sang a hymn of
praise to the Lord ! ” Hallelujah ! That was one
occasion, at all events, when Methodism was not,
as Carlyle sarcastically puts it, “looking inward,
contemplating its own navel.”
* * * *
If MTiwraitli returns to Queensland in
good fighting form he will flutter the pigeons in
more than one dovecote. I remember asking him
one hospitable night at “ Auchenflower ” (his
private residence): “ What is your predominating
ambition?” “My ambition,” he replied, with
fiery emphasis, “is to make Queensland ( ',e
wealthiest, happiest, and greatest of the A.i.s-
tralias ! ” And, doubtless, he was perfectly sin-
cere ; but, alas! the keystone of his Arch of
Triumph gave way under hostile pressure, and the
rest fell to pieces like a Chinese puzzle. Alone
and free, he could have done it—a veritable Corio-
lanus in politics—but the Fates were against him,
as they doubtless would have been against
Frederick the Great, and Peter the Great, and
Alexander if they had been compelled to consider
the majority in a Parliament elected by “loud
cheers ” and “ tumultuous applause.”
* * * *
An old white-headed Richmond River
aboriginal recently told me that he was at Runny-
mede station, on that river, when it belonged to
“ Mitha Teefen ” —meaning Ward Stephen, once
of the Sydney Hkrald —and that he was close to
Stephen’s son when the latter accidentally shot
himself. The young fellow was buried in the
station-garden.
* * * •
Had a recent chat, at Jimbour station,
with Charlie Samuels, the once famous sprint-
runner. His chief regret was the wasting of all
his money among Sydney thieves and spielers.
Charlie’s father, a fine old fellow of 70, is a Namoi
native, and came over to the Logan River in
South Queensland via New England in 1846 with
the late C. R. Haley. The old chap still speaks
his own language, the Kamilroi dialect of the
Namoi, in addition to the “ Wacca-Wacca ” of
the Darling Downs.
* * * *
The word “ bushranger ” in the early days
of Australia appears to have had the significance
of “ bush man ”at the present time. An ad. in
Sydney Gazette of 1805 is for “a steady, honest
white man, who is also a good bushranger 1 ”
Chinese half-caste youth. Won gold medal at
Scots College, Woollahra, on the votes of his
schoolmates, as being the most popular boy in the
school.
Photo., Crown Studios.
One of Thomas Mitchell’s exploring-
party, in 1846, was an Italian, named Felix
Maguire, who was invaluable in dry country.
Mitchell records that Maguire could go to sleep,
dream of the locality of water, wake up, and go
straight to the spot. He did this at least twice.
I have known a woman who possessed the same
curious faculty.
* * * *
Let me commend to title-craving Aus-
tralian politicians the system of the Brazilian
statesman, Pereira. All Brazilian titles were sold
at fixed ad valorem rates, and the resultant money
was devoted to the erection and endowment of the
splendid lunatic asylum on the shores of the Bay
of Botafogo ! Cold historical Brazilian fact! The
asylum has been - described as “ less a monument
of humanity and love of our afflicted neighbors
than of the vanity and frailty of poor human
nature, the tributes to which erected it.” Much
coin for similar purposes could doubtless be raised
in Australia from the foolish human fowls who
desire to strut about with a solitary bird-of-para-
dise feather stuck in their own commonplace tails.
• * * *
In South Brisbane lives an ex-U.S. Texan
Ranger, who tells me he was at the public-house
near the Sandridge rifle range when Lindsay
Gordon shot himself, Gordon was passing with a
rifle over his shoulder, and someone hailed him.
He turned back, had a glass of brandy, and
resumed his walk, saying he was going to the rirb--
range to “do some shooting.” Soon afterwards
the pub. party heard a rifle-shot, and news cann-
in that Gordon had shot himself. Just as my
informant arrived on the spot Lieut. Scratchier
rode up, jumped off his horse, lifted Gordons
head, and observed :“ A bag o’ bones —a bag o
bones ! ” Scratchley afterwards became Sir Peter
Scratchley, and met a somewhat tragic end on the
North Queensland coast.
* * » *
Alexander Forbes, brother of the famous
war correspondent, was a middy on the Omar
Pasha about 1860. Afterwards shepherd on
Hooper’s Hawltwood station, on the Burnett River
(Q.), where he wrote many poems and ballads, and
covered the walls of his hut with these poetic pro-
ductions. Died in Toowoomba. When Archibald
Forbes was in Australia he went to visit the
grave.
MAROOGALINE.
In Hobart Mercury 44 A Christian Woman
shrieks wildly to her “brother-ministers ” to put
more “ sand ” into their rain-supplications. “Cer-
tainly, we did have an answer, but only in a
small way.” The ghastly unhumorousness of the
pious person is proof even against the grotesque
conception of a pro rata Omnipotent, running a
universe on the strictest business principles.
“ Absolutely no Credit! ”
According to a decision of Melb. District
Court, the cyclist can disport himself on the paths
of local reserves without bivach of the law. It
was held that as the paths had not been surveyed
and laid out, the cyclist is not punishable iinder
the Police Offences Act. So the silent _ bike < n
these early setting eves will be as deadly in M« I ■-
parks as Bill Sikes’ sand-bag.

Public Notices.
Public Service Board,
50, Young-street,
Sydney, 30th April, 1898.
ri HE Public Service Board hereby notify that a Com-
-1 petitive Examination of Candidates for admission to
the Professional Division of the Public Service of the
Colony will be held in Sydney, and at various Country
Centres which will be fixed as far as possible to suit the
convenience of candidates, on the 27th June and four
following days. The estimated number of appointments
to be made is 20. Candidates must not be less than lb or
more than 21 years of age on the first day of examination,
3Bd moral character, and in sound health Persons
already employed in the Clerical and General Divisions
of the Public Service, or who may have previously been
employed therein and retired for reasons other than an
offence, wi 1 be admitted to the examination provided
their does no exceed 30 years. Candidates may elect
to be examined for either the Engineering, the Survey,
or Drafting Branches, but must state in their application
the Branch which they desire to enter. Candidates for the
Engineering Branch will he examined in the following sub-
lets'-(1) Handwriting, (2) English (Dictation. Com-
position, Prck'is writing, raising. Analysis, and Derivation
of Words), (3) Arithmetic (full course), (4) Geometry
(Euclid, First Book), (5) Geography (outlines of physical
and political geography, and the geography of the Aus-
tralasian Colonies in moderate detail, (b) English History
from the middle of the Eighteenth Century to the present
time, especially with reference to the Colonies and
Dependencies, (7) Mathematics (Euclid, Books 2 to 4 and
6 including easy exercises in these hooks, Algebra up to
and including quadratic equations), (8) Elementary
Drawing, and (9) Elementary Geology. Candidates for
the Survey or Drafting Branches will be examined in the
first eight subjects referred to above. Candidates tor all
branches will also be allowed to take any two, but not
more than two, of the following subjects (ft) English, a
prescribed author, viz., Tennyson's “Gareth .nd
Lynette ’’ (MacMillan). (?>) Latin, (<■) French, (-1) German.
(e) Elementary Chemistry. Any candidate for admission
to the Professional Division, who has previously passed
the examination for the Clerical Division, may, on appli-
cation being made by him, be credited on examination
with the number of marks obtained by him at the
Clerical Examination in the subjects that are common to
the two examinations. Application to attend the ex-
amination must he made on a form for the purposjp,
which can he obtained at the office of the Board, and
must reach the undersigned not later than the Ist June.
Candidates will he required to pay an examination fee of
I<K, which must be lodged with a Clerk of Petty Sessions
or the Treasury, and the receipt therefore forwarded
to the Secretary of the Board with t lie form of applica-
tion. A list of registered candidates, with centres of
examination, will he published in the OovKiiN.vti.Nr
Ga/.kttk, after the time for the receipt of applications has
expired, and candidates will also be advised direct of the
place at which they will he examined. _ ...
Copies of the Regulations in regard to this examination
can be obtained from the Government Printing Office.
By order of the Board,
9 a .75. J. W. HOLLIMAN, Secretary.

THE AUSTRALIAN GEM TRADE.
H. Newimn, the well-known jeweller of 175
Elizabeth-street, Melbourne, is the Largest buyer
of olrilin Australia, and opal miners will find it
to their advantage to send their opal direct to
him. The firm has a reputation for straight deal-
ing extending over a jjeriod of 40 years, which is
a sufficient guarantee to consignors that they will
be honorably treated. H. Newman is also a
buyer of all other Australian gems.*

POINTS
The tune for the Cuban army of invasion-
Yankee Boodle.
A member of N.S.W. Govt, wagered
tin 0 1900 St Weo k *kat the Reid Ministry will last
The two Sydney syndicate dailies—D.T.
and Star—oppose the Convention Bill. The two
privately-owned dailies - Herald and E. News—
support it.
The Victorian Anti-billers don’t want a
-reaeration ; they want a Revolution. It is im-
possible to teach them the difference between the
two things.
So dissatisfied are officers of N.S.W. Gen-
eral Post Office with their treatment under the
Public Service Board that they are expected to
vote almost to a man for the Federal Bill, in hopes
of better treatment from the Commonwealth’s
Joe (Jook.
Vic. miners refuse to answer the call of
the Melb. Trades Hall and arm against the
federation Bill. An anti-Billious public meeting
convened at Bendigo the other night was attended
by six persons, conveners ineludpd. And that
even after Ben Tillett had orated there !
As illustrating what may happen when
I ’ed crated Australia wants a Governor-General,
it may lie noted that George Grey and Frederick
Wold were Premiers and also Governors of Aus-
tralasian provinces.
The anti-Federal-bill cry at Melb. Trades
Hall : —“ We want different politicians.” But,
unfortunately, they have only indifferent poli-
ticians in stock, barring Trenwith, who seems to
be losing his Hall-mark.
C. M. R. Dumas, proprietor of Mt. Barker
(S.A.) Courier, is regarded as a certainty for
Agent-General Cockburn’s vacated seat. S.A.
Assembly will soon consist almost entirely of
country journalists and local preachers.
Higgins, M.L.A. (Vic.); “A member of
N.S. W. Parliament has written to me that if the
Bill is carried there his House will refuse to send
it home.” Will Higgins furnish the name of the
member who proposes that Parliament should
override the decision of its masters, the people ?
Brisbane Courier, which is a stirring advo-
cate of Federation, nullifies its advocacy by boom-
ing Byrnes and his anti-Federal party. Editor
Ward has a free hand on academic questions ; but
Boodlewraith spells Courier bread-and-butter,
and the rats don’t yet judge it time to desert the
ship.
The men who are elected at the Narrabri
and Fitzroy (N.S.W.) bye-elections will only keep
their seats for about six weeks before the dissolu-
tion throvvs them out again on a cold world. Yet
five politicians are after the Narrabri seat, and
three or four are racing after M'Elhone’s old shoes
at Fitzroy.
Is “ moral suasion ” what it is cracked up
to be ? An umbrella-poke seems to have knocked
more sense into Premier Forrest than all the
deputations. What might have resulted from a
judicious half-brick? Perhaps manhood suffrage,
re - distribution of seats, and the Norseman-
Esperance railroad.
A devout anti-Federalist discovers the
working of Providence in the almost simultaneous
recovery of the brothers Heydon from the land of
silence. C. G. Heydon was gagged with an
Acting-Judgeship and L. F. Heydon with in-
fluenza. Both can now curse the Convention Bill
without restraint. Ain’t it Providential ?
Premier Reid promised at Goulburn last
week that if N.S.W. will reject the Federation
Bill he will make ‘arrangements for joining the
Federal Council. What a fall, after all the pre-
vious promises that Reid would put his fleshy
shoulder to the wheel, and never leave off till he
died, or burst, or Federation was accomplished !
The Federal Council has no money ; no authority ;
no mission. It meets for a few days every second
year at Hobart, bee:.:.: c if it doesn’t meet at least
every second year its constitution provides that it
shall cease to exist altogether. And the little
handful of delegates talk things over, and have a
few drinks, and then separate.
T.D. ; “This Yankee-Spanish conflict is
next to valueless as a test of modern naval arma-
wonts the first-class fighting nations have each
such a distinct set of trump inventions up their
sleeves. In conversation with a naval officer the
other day. lie told me that talk of shelving the
Brennan torpedo was sheer rot. It is the best (or
worst) messenger of death now held by the British
Admiralty, arid no other nation is known to
possess any instrument of such possibilities in
destruction. My informant said the Brennan
would even surprise the men who have to handle
it.”
Bulcock, the great Q. election-engineer, is
making no effort to put new life into his Political
Ass. For years, Bulcock was the organising
genius of the Boodlewraith, and his present inac-
tivity suggests indifference to the fate of the
Byrnes Ministry. Bulcock is very yellow and
Byrnes very green—which p’raps explains.
Lewis Cohen, the pushing ex-politician
who has apparently appointed himself leader of
the S.A. Anti-Billious party, was not eagerly wel-
comed by that miscellaneous crowd. The fact
that he rims a bank, where the hard-up citizen
can obtain accommodation on easy terms, has pro-
voked the remark that Cohen was only “ discount-
ing the Federal “ Bill ”as an advt.
More Sydney Telegraph : “ The remission
of mterprovincial duties will relieve us (N.S.W.)
of no taxation, because they are remitted already.”
In view of the fact that levies duties on
the sugar, brandy, wine, tobacco, ale, biscuits,
candles, cigars, jams, jellies, &c., &c., of the other
provinces, and raised about £lOO,OOO from this
source last year, this is another yarn that wants
disinfecting.
S.A. anti - Federationists disagree in a
marvellous manner as to the loss to S.A. under
Federation. Coghlan places that province’s loss
at £150,000- a figure which exceeds Treasurer
Holders estimate by £120,000; but the climax
was reached when oppositionist Lewis Cohen
(financier, etc.) placed it at £358,000 ! Adelaide
people are wondering what kind of a “system”
prevails at Cohen’s loan-and-discount bank.
Charleston, M.L.C., who resigned from
S.A. Labor-party owing to hospital dissensions,
is working with the Federal delegates, whereas
his old colleagues, without exception, solidly
oppose. Hie S.A. anti-Billites are under the
, ac * ers hip °f Batchelor (Labor), Cohen (ex-
M.P.) and H. R. Dixon (who is a big tobacco
manufacturer), with the Advertiser half-
heartedly barking at the tail-end of the procession.
As Bishop Moorhouse cabled his
sympathy to the Yanks, he doubtless
prays for their success, and is therefore
guilty of instigating a British subject—
to wit, God—to aid the enemy of “ a
country with which Britain is at peace.”
If the Yanks do win, it will mainly
with the assistance of a British Bishop
and the British God ; and that’s neither
neutrality nor fairplay.
A thing not generally known,
mentioned in Duffy’s autobiog. One
reason for stopping transportation was
the action taken by Edward Wilson,
of the Argus. He got up a subscrip-
tion for returning the very worst ex-
pirees to England. Some were actually
sent to Liverpool, at an expense of
<£ls a head. It was announced that
subscriptions would be forthcoming
to send a thousand.
It is only too common in Queens-
land to see a Govt, grant for cleaning
public offices spent in this wise : Civil
. servant A or Policeman X (more fre-
quently the latter) receives a grant of
J 340 per annum for above-mentioned
purposes. Forthwith Policeman X hires
Ah Lillie Gel, Celestial sweater, to do
the necessary duties for £2O per an-
num. Then Ah Lillie Gel hires hap-
less Ah Cow to do the bullocking for
various sums ranging from .£5 to £lO
a year. The Govt, never troubles—no
Govt, ever does.
Renmark (S.A.) has the one hotel
in Australia which is run as a munici-
pal enterprise. It is a great success as
a hotel, being clean and well-managed;
also, it sells good grog, because the
manager would gain nothing by se’ling
bad grog instead; and it does no afier-
hours or Sunday trading, because if it
did the hired boss would lose his billet.
The first year’s profits were £450. At
this rate a State or municipal public-
house monopoly, either in N.S.W. or
Victoria or Maoriland, should be worth
from £500,000 to £1,500,000 per annum
clear profit. As an unexplored source
of revenue the State or municipal grog
system has magnificent possibilities.
Apparently “Tom” Slattery is
left again. He undertook to contest
Fitzroy (Sydney) on the purely anti-
Federal ticket, therefore Protectionist
Federalists are, to a great extent, not
taking any Slattery. And now the
Freetrade anti-Federalists are putting
up candidates of their own ; and the
Freetraders who go on the fiscal issue
only are warned by advt. not to be
“ misled,” because"-a well-known Free-
trader (Macleav-stm-t) will contest the
corning election, receiving the Govern-
ment support.” Even the public-house
interest seems to be divided between
Slattery and grocer Chapman. The
most probable result of it all will be that
when the lost Slattery keeps his pro-
mise to contest King Division against
Reid at the general election, that acrid
Fat Man will allude to him as “the
cast-off gentleman from Woolloomoo-
loo.” And the gibe will kill Slat-
tery.
The Maoriland law has awarded £7225
damages to the widows and families of the men
killed by the Brunner coal-mine explosion in ’96.
M. law, like that of N.S.W., and like the law
which once prevailed in Victoria, holds that in
ease of a mine accident it is for the owners to
prove that there was not negligence on their part
—not for the penniless widows to prove that there
was after the principal witnesses are dead. In
ML., however, the law is a reality so far; in
N. the courts simply blew the provision out
of the statute and refuse to take any notice of it
whatever.
NO DEMAND FOR TARIFF.
NEW COMMERCIAL: “I suppose you have special
tariff for travellers here?”
PIRKEEPER : “ No, we haven't; but we've got plenty
o’ whisky en’ any gorsquantity beer—good
enough fer blokes up this way !”
Broken Hill Miner, which of all Aus-
tralian daily papers comes nearest to thorough-
going support of the Parliamentary Labor-party,
has vigorously taken the other side on the Federa-
tion question. And the Bill needs battling for
over there, too, since the Far Western miners and
station-hands answer (usually without a question)
to the Labor-party’s whip. Moreover, while no
N.S.W. Convention delegate or first-rate follower
can spare time to go round to this detached part
of the province, neither the S.A. nor Vic. dele-
gates, who are nearer and have been appealed to,
think it politic or prudent to speak out of their
own States—a decision come to in Premier
Kingston’s case with much regret. Further,
Barton was “ the man that gaoled the leaders and
burst up the strike”—-and this is the first chance
Sleath and co. have had to get even with him.
Last week the Sydney anti-Federal organ
expressed a foreboding that if Federation got a
majority on June 3, yet lost for want of the 80,000
minimum vote, the unprincipled national party
may try to get the minimum abolished by Parlia-
ment so as to have the matter decided by a bare
majority. This is mentioned as a rumor, but
there is no need to rumor about anything so
obvious. If there are, say, 70,000 votes for Feder-
ation and 60,000 against it, it will be lost for the
time being for want of the needful 80,000. But
the Federal party, going on the sacred principle
that the majority should rule, will fight next elec-
tion on the straight-out issue that Parliament
should abolish the 80,000 minimum, and either
have a new election or declare the thing settled by
the election already held. And the Provincial
mob must either cave in, or fight that election on
the plea that the minority, and not the majority,
should rule.

tionists :
A £7,000,000 to £7,500,000 tariff would oe required. . . .
A tariff like that meant an additional payment (in N.S.W.)
of 255. per head. — Postmaster-Gene ml Cook (N.S.W.), re-
ported in Daily Telegraph, of 16th May.
It would mean £3 10s. of increased taxation per head of
population in N.S. Wales.—Lyke, M.L.A., reported in
same paper, same dap.
The necessities of the other provinces foredoomed our
fiscal system. ... It had heen shown that the tariff of
the Commonwealth would be a highly Protective one.—
Cook, some paper.
Many Protectionists would probably be found voting
for this Bill, believing that it would give them Protection,
but this was a fallacy.—-lask, same paper.
The population of N S.VV. must increase in greater
ratio than the other provinces, and then the principle of
equal State representation would become more dangerous.
■ —LiYne, some paper.
The ratio of population increase last year was : West
Australia, 17’37 per cent. ; Tasmania, 3'37 per cent.;
Queensland, 2'65 per cent. ; Maoriland, 2‘09 per cent. ;
N.S.W;, 1-99 per cent.; S.A., 0.78 per cent.; Victoria, OTI
per cent.— Cogulan’s statistics.

From the Newcastle Morning Herald’s
current-events column :
Mr. J. N. Brunker will sell by auction this morning
three trucks of chaff and one of wheaten hay.
Daddy has been selling chaff to N.S.W. for a
long time now; when is the country to get its
“ one of wheaten hay ” ? Anyhow, it’s cheering
to hear that Sunday Brunker sometimes deals in
the other stuff.
Hunk of execration from the Sydney
Catholic Press :
Thk Bulletin, the pink infidel of Sydney, is ayain on
the warpath. On this occision it waxes wrath over the
conduct of the late Mr. Janies Crotty, who bequeathed
£llO,OOO to religious and charitable purposes in Mel-
bourne. Judas Iscariot, who, il he were alive to-day,
might find a place on the staff of The Bulletin, was also
opposed to the waste of worldly goods even when these
were employed to honor God. The “pink one” laments
and weeps because this money was not expended on road
and railway making or in giving employment to the
unemployed. Infidels are not remarkable for their keen-
ness of logic. Even if £lO,OOO is expended in erecting a
tower on St. Patrick’s Cathedral will not the working
men become the recipients of the legacy ? The rest of the
legacy will go direct, without official grasping, to find
homes and bread for the poor and afflicted over whom
IHE Bulletin sheds crocodile tears. Even Voltaire said
that he would never trust an infidel, because the
said infidel could have no scruples in cutting your
throat to get your money if he saw a safe chance of
escape from the law. The attempt of The Bulletin to
pose as the friend of the poor is a screaming farce. Its
poisonous pages are weekly devoted to the baneful and
diabolical work of trying to rob the poor of what is
dearer and more consoling to them than worldly goods—
the belief m a bright, and happier world. And all good
Christians should prefer to see a snake on their table
rather than the weekly Bulletin.
The above is possibly excusable from the unprac-
tical builders of useless, empty cathedrals, and
the mediaeval dreamers who spend old Crotty’s
money in teaching the young about the lives of
Irish saints. But The Bulletin could build an
execration at ten seconds’ notice that would
simply shrivel up the weak effort of the Conse-
crated Press. There was a time when the church
was the boss curser of Christendom. Now the
ages have gone by and left it dozing among its
relics, and all the curse there is left in it wouldn’t
get a bullock out of a creek.

The foolishness of any hard-and-fast rule
of personal habit is well instanced by the fact that
Gladstone, the man of life-long machine-like
regimen, isn’t likely to see out his fellow-octo-
genarian, Bismarck—the scorner of all hygienio
rule ; the tremendous but casual worker ; the man
of enormous gorges and huge potations of weirdly
mixed victuals and drinks ; the man of a daily
quart of brandy, of ditto beer by the gallon, of
everlasting strong cigars. The “way we’re
built ! ”
Melb. Mayor M'Eacharn, in reply to Rev.
Prof. Gosman’s stricture—“ it was rather shabby
for the Mayor to refuse them the use of the Town
Hall,” &c., on the occasion of his lecture under
the auspices of the Anti-Sweating League—re-
marks that “it is the custom to avoid any Sun-
day work for the Corporation officials.” After
which, of course, the ships of the Mayor’s firm
will not leave port late on Saturdays to get all
possible work out of the crews.
If the Australian Governments desire to
quash the current New Guinea land-grab scheme,
they have plenty of precedents to build on. In
’7B, the master of a trading vessel bought 700 a. at>
Port Moresby. He alleged he gave <£ooo for the
land, but, whatever he gave, his claim was quashed
by the British Government. A claim to 15,000 a.
in the Kabadi district was similarly disallowed.
Later on, a German company, and, still later, a
London company, set up claims to extensive
tracts, and both were unceremoniously ousted.
“The London County Council,” writes
The Bulletin’s London correspondent, “ intends
spending ,£7BOO on bands to give free public per-
formances in and around the metropolis during
the coming summer. Over 30 are already en-
gaged playing two or three times a week in the
open air, and the scheme is so popular that last
season nearly 150,000 penny programmes were
sold. The poorer localities are specially looked
after, and here the listeners, women and children
especially, turn up in thousands. The idea at
present is to introduce, under the control of the
Council, the continental or bier-garten system in
the gardens along the Embankment. Why some-
thing of the kind doesn’t obtain in Australia is an
unsolvable problem to all foreigners acquainted
with your climate and circumstances.”
Extract from the manifesto of the Vic-
torian anti-Federal gang :
The present Constitution, however, whilst building up
the credit of the Commonwealth at the expense of the
States, by giving the former control of all our urincipal
sources of revenue, such as customs, excise, and un-
limited powers of direct taxation, leaves the impoverished
States, with their reduced revenues, diminished credit,
and impaired borrowing powers, the sole responsibility of
providing for the payment or conversion of our national
debts.
This is about as much inaccuracy as could be put
into so small a space. The Commonwealth hasn’t
anything like absolute control of customs and
excise, for it is to return three-fourths of the cus-
toms and excise revenue to the States. And
the Constitution distinctly provides for the Com-
monwealth taking over and converting the debts.
Besides, if there is anything in this world which
wants to be not only impaired, but killed quite
dead and buried in some place where nobody will
ever find it again, it is the borrowing powers of
the Australian States, which have already the
biggest and most awful debts on earth. The pity
is that the Federal Constitution quite forgot to
impair them.


TUESDAY, 21st JUNE.
BY AUCTION.
Tenham Cattle Station-
TITO be SOLD pursuant to a judgment of the
1 Supreme Court of Queensland, made in an
action, Hammond v. Hammond and Others, num-
bered 55 of 1807, with the approbation of the
Honorable Mr. Justice Cooper, by Messrs. B. D.
MO REHEAD & CO-* of Brisbane, Auctioneers,
in conjunction with Messrs. HILL, CLARK &
CO., of Sydney (the persons appointed by the said
Judge), at Mary-street, Brisbane, in the Colony of
Queensland, on the 21st Day of JUNE, 1898, at
Eleven O’Cloek in the forenoon, in one lot,
TENHAM STATION
(Outside Tick-Infested Area),
Situated in Gregory South district of Queensland, con-
sisting of 276 square miles-of country (more or less), of
which 190 square miles are Leasehold, having 17 years to
run from the Ist July, 1897, and 86 square miles of country
held under Grazing Right, under “Crown Lands Act of
1884.” Together with
6500 CATTLE (more or less), Fifty (50) HORSES (more
or less), STATION PLANT, STORES and IM-
PROVEMENTS thereon or belonging thereto.
The Country is situated on Cooper’s Creek and Kyabra
Creek, about 40 miles south of Windorah and about 230
miles west of Charleville railway station. It is very rich
fattening country for cattle, consisting of lightly timbered,
•open, mulga country, with occasional black-soil plains,
a mndarice of salt-bush, blue-bush, cotton-bush, and other
edible shrubs. TENHAM is splendidly watered by the
main channel of the Cooperand its many branches, be-
sides springs. The Cattle are quiet, and have been well
•worked. In the herd there are about 1000 head of Bul-
locks and Cows fit to start for market. The Improvements
:are sufficient for the efficient working of the station.
Particulars and Conditions of Sale may be had gratis at
the office of Messrs. Macdonald-Paterson and Hawthorn,
Ed ward-street, Brisbane, Solicitors for the Plaintiff ;
Messrs. J. F. Fitzgerald and Power, Queen-street, Brisbane,
Solicitors for the Defendants ; or at the offices of the said
B. D. Morehead & Co., Brisbane ; Messrs. Hill, Clark &
'Co., Sydney ; Messrs. Ryan, Hammond & Mates, Mel-
bourne ; and Messrs. Barker Bros., Adelaide, and at the
place of sale.
Dated this twenty-fifty day of April, 1898.
(L.s.) W. A. DOUGLAS,
Deputy Registrar.

“I thought I had a Stamp.”
Many inquiries havihg been made at this office for
the above humorous picture by “Hop” which
appeared in The Bulletin as a coloured supple-
ment some years ago (and which has been long out
■of print) Mr. Hopkins has, by special permission of
'The Bulletin, executed an etching of the work,
and a limited number of signed artist’s proofs
may now be had only on application to this
office. The proofs are printed on Whatman’s
“Creswick ” drawing paper, 14 x 174? inches ; size
■of plate, x 12. As every detail has been carried
out by the artist himself (including the printing)
these proofs have all the value of an original
drawing. This will be an exceptional opportunity
to possess an example of “ Hop’s ” work, which is
rarely to be seen outside the pages of this paper.
On receipt of Three Guineas we will send by
post or parcel delivery to any address.
