SPORTING NOTIONS.
Mr. J. Gubbins’ name may be agin him, but it
doesn't spoil his luck. Cabled that he has sold the
English Derby winner, Galtee More, to the Russian Govt,
for £25,000. As a three-year-old Galtee More raked in
upwards of £23,000 in stakes alone.
* • • •
S.T.C. brings off the first instalment of its Q.
Birthday Meeting at Randwic-k to-day (Saturday) when
good fields will be seen, as owners are out for “ hay and
corn ’’ stakes. The Hurdles look like falling to Cornet or
Jack Rattling, and Othello or St. Ilario may account for
the Steeple. The Tola, Heinpie, Strathrov, and Precau-
tion are a likely quartet for the winner of Royal Stakes
to spring from, and of these Precaution may’ prove the
best. The Birthday Cup (on Tuesday) is already provok-
ing speculation, and True Blue reigns as first favorite.
His most dangerous opponents should be Insulalor,
Syerla and Glendennon, and when ’tis all over perhaps
the rather uncertain Insulator will be found “ there."
* * * *
Under the totalisator regime S.A. racecourses
seem as decorous as a church congress, and the falling-off
in attendances at recent Cup meeting was ascribed by
some to that fact. There is little of the excitement that
used to prevail in the old days. The tote receives and
pays without wearing a blue-and-yellow coat and a glossy
belltopper, and yelling the odds through a gigantic
broken nose, and the crowd is deprived of the pleasure of
chasing it over the fences and ditches for about three
miles, because it always “parts” after each race. The
good old times when the less important scions of the race
of Mosestein and co. used to sneak into town through
back alleys, clothed in one sock and the ruin of a belltop-
per, and with a damp sensation all over them, are, alas,
gone for ever!
* * * *
Paul Pry, who won Adelaide Cup for Rupert
Clarke last week, did the mile and five furlongs in 2.51 J.,
a record for the race. Weighted at Bst. 51b. the race was
a gift for the Lochiel gelding, and he only returned even
money in the tote.
• • * *
“ Big Jim ” Wilkinson, who put up a bit of a
reputation as the VVestralian goldfields handicapper,
though he had much “ sandgroping ” prejttflice to over-
come at first, has just been “ farewelled ’’ at Kalgoorlie.
Wilkinson has roved over most parts of Australia, and
has hung on to little besides a clear name and a devotion
to A. L. Gordon, whom he knows from cover to cover.
He was “ coaching ” in the awful Western Never-Never
of N.S.W. when Broken Hill was discovered, and that
was the beginning of his connection with uncertain
mining. From Kalgoorlie he goes East.
• * * «
With Newhaven and Aurum, far and away the
best of their years, in England, and Mr. Wilson asked to
put a price upon Bobadil, who, on paper, has only to
start to win this year’s Derby, it looks as if the very best
of the Australians of the future will make their homes in
England. This makes the outlook for the Australian
horse serious, as when the present crack sires die out,
where are their successors to come from, if the cream of
their progeny are expatriated ?
•' * * •
N.S.W. trotting men are to^have still another
show, the Dubbo Trctting Club having decided to bring
off a meeting early in July, probably on the Kensington
course, Sydney. Mr. Fitzgerald’s horse, Huon Junior,
has improved out of knowledge since his last Sydney trip,
and good judges of trotting in Dubbo go so far as to say
they are not even afraid of the champion, Fritz. If the
horse is as good as the Dubbo people think him a meeting
between’ the pair would go far towards making
the meeting a success. The writer’s little hit, however,
would go on Fritz.
• * • *
At a pony course lately a boy rode queerly and
almost let his owner heavily in. But the owner was nob
one to take thii g; lying down. The lad was scarce!v past
the post half-a-oozen lengths ahead of the field before his
employer was up the track, dragged him out of the
saddle and chucked him over the fence to be “dealt with ”
by some friends of the stable. Then the owner and his
“ push ” chased the riderless horse into a corner of the
course and forced it to jump a fence into the street. Of
course when the stewards met they unanimously wiped
out the resourceful owner, but, as disqualifications have a
maximum currency of only three months on the pony
course, he considered his money well saved.
* * * •
Surely the English pony-market' is worth Aus-
tralian owners’ attention. At a recent sale at Rugby
(Eng.) 30 polo-ponies averaged the goodly sum of i 231;
four of the best selling at 750, 650, 610, and' 480 guineas.
* • * *
“ H.G. ” to The Bulletin, from Christchurch,
Maoriland : “Bookmaking in M.L. is entirely a thing of
the past. The totalisator, with its fair and legitimate
odds, has crushed the old game of laying dead-nns right
out of existence. About the last semblance of the old
bookmaker is the occasional lone, sorrowing Ikey, who-
lays the tote odds—or says he does. There isn’t much of
him, and he is a miserable fraud. Nowadays, if you put
a pound on a winner, and Ikey has laid the same horse
several times, he will only pay you £5, including your
own pound, even though the horse has paid over £lO on
the machine, which is the recognised limit. When you
go to collect, he will produce a card, which he has ready
for such emergencies, stating, ‘This meeting, £5 limit;’’
and I’ve known several cases where they were very badly
struck, and would only pay £3 for each pound invested,
though the winner had paid £7 or £8 on the totalisator.
If you object, they simply tell you it’s against the law,
and that you are as liable as themselves to be sent to gaol
—a place they have no particular antipathy to. A good
proportion of them are used to it. They mostly spend'
the winter months there. Of course, the layer can’t make
a fuss. He generally has an objection to prison."
* * * *
Jimmie Jockey Hayes had to produce his fair
young face at Melb. City Court, last week, on a charge of
smiting a hotel barman. It was one of those curious,
nocturnal cases which complainant rarely proceeds with
after discussing the matter with his—er—friends. The
smitten of the pub. merely came into court to-
state that he didn’t wish to prosecute because he was
suddenly leaving for Westralia, and Mr. J. Hayes wore
very thick gloves when he dealt the blow, and' the wit-
nesses of the transaction had been called out of town on
important business, and a peaceful end to the “slight-
difference ’’ would be best for all parties, including the
Bench. The exact bearing of this last remark wasn’t very
evident. The Bench thought it best to hear the apolo-
getic barman's evidence, and fined Jimmy ten “ bob.”
Whereupon that ornament of the turf offeied to put two-
ten “ bobs ’’ into the poor-box rather than be fined at all,
but the offer was coldly refused.
* * * •
It was a cold, wet night after Dungan’s Onka-
paringa Steeple day, and a number of trainers and’
jockeys at a farm-house near the course were sitting close-
to a tremendous log-fire when a hesitating knock came to
the door. Mick Carey, remarking that it wasn’t a fit-
night for a dog to be out, opened, disclosing a long,
lanky, hatless man, with the water pouring from his-
clothes and beaid in streams. He stepped inside and
stood, fascinated by the fire, till someone pulled him
near the blaze and poured hot coffee into him from a jug.
Then, with chattering teeth, he told his tale, shaking the
water from him like a retriever at every few words. Had
been drinking at a booth till it was dark, and, crossing
one of the numerous streams on his way home, had
slipped from the narrow footway and fallen into the
water. “ That was cold. Got out pretty quick, I’ll bet,”
said one. “ No,” was the solemn reply; “I thought I
was in bed, and—who-o-o ! —I pulled the clothes over my-
self t ” The long wet man looked surprised at the roar
of laughter, but after half an hour by the fire and some
quarts of coffee he said he’d make a start; his house was
only a short way off. A hat and overcoat were offered
him, but he declined them, pausing in the doorway to ex-
plain : “ There’s a gutter near my place, you see, and /
fall into it every night ! ” “ Boys,” said Carey, when the
scarecrow had vanished—“ take it from me—that mam
is a ghost! ’’
• * • •
“ Lesna is coming back to Australia,” so says
Daine Rumor, and the visit is well-timed if Rumor lieth
not. They do say that the French sprinter is coming to-
pick up the crumbs of his past long-distance Australasian-
records, but such a job would be now difficult of accom-
plishment. In the first place, there aren’t any crumbs-
left; Swift Relph took the entire record cake left by the
French crack, arid ate so heartily that not a single con-
lolatory fraction was left.
• • • «
The change of venu from the Sydney Cricket
so the Agricultural Ground proved of no benefit to the
N.S.W. League of Wheelmen, the meeting resulting in a
loss of about £lOO. Zimmermann's failure did the sport
a lot of harm, and later on the crowd would not stand
Platt-Betts and his exhibitions. The game requires a
few importations of the plugger-Martin type, who would
race a cross-cut saw to give it a fillip. Record-breakers
and machine-advertisers are not wanted by Australians,
who w p ould rather see a dead-game finish by a couple of
fat pigs than an exhibition-mile by Galtee More.
* • * •
The bike is an unreliable political machine, after
all. Labor-member Sleath, wheeling overland from Cobar
to Wiloannia and Broken Hill, came to grief near Louth,
with the result that all his meeting arrangements were
upset.
» * * *
Dear Bulletin. — Re the cycling animal and the
lady pedestrian. Other day I noticed a lady pedestrian
drop her boa on the tram-line when crossing Oxford-st.,
Sydney. She looked neither to right nor left, but darted
back and stooped, when the cycling animal sat on the
ground facing her, and thinking hard. He apologised,
but the lady fixed him with her stoniest stare, and strode
away indignantly. If a tram had run over her instead of
the bicycle, the driver would probably have to do time
for manslaughter.—T.D.

Macgowan, of Melbourne, and Tweedie, of Syd-
ney, are to box for a £4O purse offered by the Q. Athletic
Club, Brisbane. The set-to should be one of the best seen
in Australia for many a day, but writerexpects Macgowan
to prove too much of a fighter for the very clever boxer
from Sydnev.
* • *
It is seldom the gift of boxing descends from
father to son, the most notable exception being the
English pugs “old ” and “young” Dutch Sam, both un-
beatable at their weights. They hailed from Petticoat
Lane, where “ young’ Sam was fairly idolised. To come
nearer home, it looks as if the old-time clever Melbourne
boxer, Peter Newton, has a good one in his son Joe. The
youngster, who has not yet met defeat, last week knocked
out Billy Smith, of Sydney, under four rounds.
* * * *
Kid M‘Coy has gone the way of all the big
latter-day pugs, his wife having obtained a divorce.
M‘Coy married again the day after the decree. Of the
lot, however, our own Bob Fitzsimmons takes the cake.
Bob was divorced from his wife and married a variety
actress named Rose Julian. Her brother, who is Fitz.’s
manager, then married the first Mrs. Fitz., and the whole
bilin’ now travel the country together a happy family.
Sydney football proper began last Saturday,
and a moist time they had of it. Great things were ex-
p.eted of the newly-formed Sydney Club, but they only
just won their match with Wallaroo, superior condition
pulling them through in the end. At half-time the game
stood Wallaroo 8, Sydney 3, and, for the first quarter-of-
an-hour when play was resumed, it looked Wallaroo’s
game, but Sydney lasted longest, and won by 9
points to 8. The Randwick-Paddington game on the
University Oval might have been changed with advantage
to water-polo—the ground was a lake. Randwick won by
8 points to nil.
* * * *
A not unlikely spec, of the near future is a
lacrosse team for England or Canada. The game has
made immense strides in Adelaide and Melb., and a team
from Melb. visits Sydney in July or August to awaken
more interest there. Already five clubs exist in Perth
and Fremantle, and in Adelaide there is a junior grade
of eleven clubs. A recent Canadian visitor was greatly-
impressed with the high standard of the game in Ade-
laide.

“Coursing Chatter” in Melb. Paunch is ex-
quisite literature. The chatterer starts with the glad
tidings that he has recovered from his attack of tvphoid,
and then goes on in this style : “I was in hopes that by
the time I was once again about the parched earth would
have been thoroughly soaked, but such is denied us. . . .
This is a splendid programme, and I quite hope owners
wdl recognise it as such. ... It is my intention to com-
mence a round of visits to the various public kennels as
soon as possible. This should have received attention
ere this, but my readers all know the reason such was not
done. The Bendigo meeting is proceeding to-dav. I
should have liked to be present, but such was not'pos-
sible.” Such beingthe quality of “Coursing Chatter,” ft is
not surprising to read that the author intends to make
Paunch “ what he made it last season, the coursing paper
of Victoria." Anyway, Tiib Bulletin will always recog-
nise it as such.

The weight-lifting feats of Klein, the German
athlete now showing in London, are the more astounding
in that he is a young fellow of only sft.. Biin. and Hist.
Squared on his hands and feet, face-upwards, he supports
a platform on which are five men and 4 half-cwts., and can
also sustain a 2561 b. bar-bell across his shoulders, with a
man hanging from each end !
Over the back-door of the Footscray (Vic.)
house, where the recent terrible domestic tragedy
occurred, was nailed a horseshoe, and in nearly
every room were hung religious pictures. But
the modern superstition was as powerless as the
ancient one against the devil of neuraligia.
We much regret that a paragraph appeared
in last Bulletin alluding to Mr. W. Hanson,
former N.S.W. Government printer, as “recently
deceased.” Mr. Han Son, we are glad to learn,
still lives, and, thanks to the care of a devoted
daughter, is quite likely to go on living for some
years. Possibly he may yet be able to write our
obituary, which, under the circumstances, might
justly afford him considerable satisfaction.

Moore, M.P., at, Bingara, enlightening
the electors. Unreportcd query: “You have
several times told us, Mr. Moore, that the Con-
stitution is for all time, and not a line of it can be
altered. What do you mean by ‘ for all time ?’ ”
Mr. Moore : “ I mean what Isay. The Constitu-
tion is for all time.” “Nonsense, nothing is for
all time, not even the drought and the present
price of wheat.” Mr. Moore: “ I mean that it
is for all time until it is dissolved.” “Exactly:
you mean that we are all immortal until we
die ! ”
The Agitated Female.
Editor Bulletin, —I ponder the mysterious in-
fluences of Moisture nnd Temperature. Our
Agitated Female is warmest where the weather is
coldest—down in Maoriland. Her Australian
activity begins in the cool parts—she was very
lively in Tas. last month, though as visitor rather
\than as resident. Then she seems to skip Vic.
(why does she skip Vic.?) and come to S.A.,
where she votes vigorously. But as you go
toward the tropics her vigor lessens. In N.S.
Wales her Agitation is spasmodic and ineffectual.
In Queensland you may say that every Female is
Calm. Is it partly due to this tranquillity that
the Q. birth-rate is highest in the group? Is it
partly due to this Agitation that the M.L. birth-
rate is lowest (bar W.A.). I fear so. Yet the
M.L. birth-rate is not too discreditable. The
M.L. Female is not all Agitated: quite a
lump of her must be Sub-agitated ; she must be
Calm in large sections at the right seasons.
* • * *
You pointed out the other day that M.L.
literary effort tended to the Average and Com-
monplace. As compared with Australian it
seemed less good and less bad. I imagine that is
partly a consequence of the more regular rhythm
of M.L. life. People proceed more equably there,
without our sudden elevations and depressions.
Too healthy, I judge, for the highest head-work-
eating and sleeping too well, encouraging temper-
aments ovine or bovine. From M.L. the histories
and biographies will come—the large, slow, low-
tension works .which argue an author’s capacity
for pegging-away. For acute spasms of creation,
poetic jets at high pressure, we must look to the
febrile Continent. And here please note that the
characteristic of the Agitated M.L. Female is a
moderate persistency. The N.S.W. Female
shrieks louder; but she shrieks spasmodically.
We have a week of welkin-rending followed by a
year of silence. Over in M.L. Moisture and Tem-
perature strike a lower keynote more continuously.
* * * *
For example : at the recent Female-eruption in
Tas. a certain Grand Kerosene-Tin Banger for
“ Purity’’—whatever that may be—was reported
as saying that “ persons who were leading impure
lives were unable to carry it on without frequent
use of strong drink.” But, as Mr. Chick sug-
gested when there was question of artificial
nourishment for the little Dombey, “Couldn’t
something temporary be done with a tea-pot?”
The Grand Kerosene-Tin-Banger's experience may
justify a different conclusion ; but mine goes to
show that the scientific rake must be as careful of
his liquor as an athlete in training. Didn’t
Balzac warn husbands to beware of water-
drinkers? In vino Venus, of course; but not in
much vino. Bacchus and Venus together are
harder to serve than God and Mammon. From
the general tenor of the Grand Kerosene-
Tin-Banger’s remarks I guess that she is
moderately young, about 23 or so. As she
grows older and tabulates her observations
1 think she will agree with Balzac and me that
women have little to fear from the drunkard, a
little from the moderate drinker, but most of all
from the total-abstainer. For -why : wine is a
stimulant; but in the end it is an assommeur, a
stupefier. So tobacco. Yet the “ purity ” erusa-
deresses wish to end the use of wine and tobacco !
Do they ever realise that—like -the man in the
fable who undermined hismill in search of imagin-
ary treasure—they are really endeavoring to
destroy the foundations of female “ virtue ”? The
man who drinks and smokes blunts his pas-
sions : the tea-person’s passions are edged like
razors. If I had my way, total-abstainers
would be legally compelled to carry a red flag by
day and a red lamp by night, so that at every
Sunday-school .picnic, every Factory Girls’ ball,
they could signal “ Danger ! Stand off ! I’m a
total-abstainer ! ’’—and the girls could get under
chaperon-cover. Not that they would, of course ;
their pluck would take them where the bullets fly
thickest. But at least they would be warned.
♦ * * *
Now, as I scan the report of proceedings of
M.L. National Council of Women, which met at
Wellington the other day, I cannot imagine one
of the speakers espousing the disastrous dogma of
the Grand Kerosene-Tin-Banger. Maybe one of
them would espouseit; but thereport, at least, gives
no warrant for thinking so. Thereport is Agitated
only by zephyrs : there is not a gale in half-a-
dozen columns. Mrs. Tasker considered there
must not be two codes of morals for the sexes.
Mrs. Shepherd wished for the repeal of the C.D.
Act. Mrs. Cunnington had two sons, who, she
proudly mentioned, were “handsome fellows.”
And so on—in the mildest way. As a mere male,
I protest that the M.L. National Council of
Women does not alarm me in the least. Its ob-
jects appear to be strictly honorable, and with
some of the speakers I would even trust myself
alone in a railway-carriage—and no man can say
more than that. Nevertheless, I think the
National Council is wrong in pressing for seats in
Parliament, as well as votes (the Council met in
M.L. Parliamentary Buildings, in order to get some-
thing like the desired atmosphere). For consider our
manly weakness. It is bad enough when female-
voters yield their reason to the charms of male
candidates ; but how will the country be governed
when male-voters are liable to be fascinated by the
blandishments of female candidates? What
chance will plain honesty, aged 45, in thick boots,
have against delicious, ambitious, incapable
Eighteen-or-so ? particularly if Eighteen-or-so
plays all she knows. Does the M.L. National
Council of Women wish to see the country ruled
by a Govt, named Flossie ?
* • * *
Take my personal attitude. If a plain woman
with large teeth wanted a vote I would un-
hesitatingly refuse her. But’ if it was a lovely
creature with small regular glistening teeth I
couldn’t refuse her anything—even if her teeth
were not her own and came unscrewed when you
kissed hard. Of course I would be wrong. It
takes a lovely creature all her brains to keep
lovely, and she has none to spare for the considera-
tion of the C.D. Acts. In politics she would
either be a failure or lose her loveliness. On the
other hand, to confine the plain women to domestic
life is to help perpetuate bony necks and dumpy
figures with flour-bag skirts, and so to ruin the
aesthetic ideals of the race. But how conquer
one’s natural inclinations ? Michael Davitt met
a S.A. village-settlement where the women were
given votes in a chairmanship contest bet ween (l)an
ordinary-looking male with a fine intelligence
and a fine policy, and (2) a male with no intelligence
and no policy and a splendid pair of moustaches.
The moustaches were simply irresistible —and the
women voted no more in that settlement. Now,
is Man less susceptible? For my part, I swear
that a dear thing in blue with languishing eyes
would get iny vote every time, though she had no
more brains than an atmospheric gas speculator.—
Yours, Brigham Brown, M.D., F.R.C.S. . :
The mining of Havana harbour was carried
out by Capt. Bustainento. The right name in the
right place !
Mr. Pilcher, Q.C., who spends about as
much of his time at one Antipodes as at the other,
complained, the other day, that Australia seems
to be “ rushing ” Federation. Seeing that Federa-
tion has had active life for 40 years and that it has
occupied the middle of the political canvas for the
last ten, the invitation to the people now for the
first time to express their opinion on a coherent
scheme doesn’t look like an evidence of mad haste.
By the way, The Bulletin hears that Mr. Pilcher
now expresses his regret at having voted for the
80,000-minimum clause.
One reason why N.S.W. shouldn’t feder-
ate, the Sydney Telegraph alleges, is the proba-
bility of increased postage rates. Which is very
likely theonesincere observation that paper has got
off its mouth since the scuffle began. The protected
Freetrade organ, which is now carried through
the post gratis at the expense of the taxpayer,
might then have to pay its way like tombstones or
whisky or boots or books or any other merchan-
dise. And it would heave Australia overboard
rather than sacrifice the bounty which it now
receives from the N.S.W. taxpayer.
Premier Reid, that tired wobbler on the
rail, in bis recent anti-Federal speech at Goulburn,
absolutely gave the lie to the Sydney Telegraph’s
invention about bow the Victorian Government
was scheming to grant a vast number of bounties
between now and June 30—after which date the
power of granting bounties is entirely taken from
the Provincial Governments :
There is no deep laid plot in Victoria to establish
bounties between the 3rd and the 30th of (he month, in
order to give them a start over their brother Australians,
because even if ttie vote in this province were in favor of
the Bill, Parliament has to pass the Address to the Im-
perial Parliament, and even if some of our provincial neigh-
bors are capable, which I do not think they are, of taking
an advantage of that sort common-sense would tell them
that we still have the opportunity of declining their
closer acquaintance.
The programme of Q. Ministerial oppon-
ents appears to be: Shortly after Parliament meets
Drake will move want-of-eonfidence in Govt. The
Labor-party and malcontent Ministerialists—now
clamoring and increasing—will lie low, and pre-
serve an eloquent and impressive silence. An
attempt will be made to keep Labor-men M‘Don-
ald, Hardacre and Dunsford in irons during the
coup, otherwise their dreadful habit of garrulously
“explaining themselves” might blow over the
show. Govt., being thus left with all the talking,
can’t orate perennially on its own virtues in the
face of opposition that is mysterious, stubborn,
and—silent. Hoped-for result: Defeat of Minis-
try by 10 votes, and collapse of the most slippery
political ring in Australasia. If something like
this doesn’t happen—well, it ought to.
New Guinea’s relation with the Australian
Governments has been crooked from the start.
The original <£15,000 a year subsidy was granted
by Australia for a protectorate over all save
the Dutch section of the island, and it was
not till he reached Albany that Scratchley learned
that Germany had made an annexation and that
Australia and he were both in the cold. A little
after Sam Griffith, who was then a practising
Q.C., overhauled Scratcliley’s commission, and
proved it was so worded as to leave that official
without status to make laws or regulations.
Scratchley himself admitted Griffith’s reading to
be sound. Then the Australian provinces began
to fall away from the subsidy. But they should
all have dropped paying till their joint control
and the extent of their rights were embodied in a
charter.
George Black, M.L.A. (N.S.W.):
Mr. Barton had called them all sorts of names for
altering the minimum vote from 50,000 to 80,000, hut he
wished, as there were nearly 300,000 voters on the roll,
that the minimum was 18<i,000: they should have an
absolute majority of voters on the roll.
George Black’s idea is apparently that if his
opponents have 17!),000 votes at the Referendum
and his own crowd has 500 or so, his crowd
should win. And yet George Black professes to
believe in the Referendum as a means of enforcing
the will of the majority! The N.S.W. Labor-
members all profess to believe in the Referendum as
a means of securing the rule of the majority ; yet
the very first time that there has been a Referen-
dum in N.S.W. a great part of it is loudiy de-
manding that its own view of the case shall have
80,000 votes or more the start of the other fellow’s
view.


A Govt, supporter will move N.S.W. Par-
liament early next session to get a full account of
how N.S. Wales became possessed of Norfolk
Island, and in what constitutional capacity it
affects to rule the community there. It looks to
this member and others that Reid, Hampden, and
Spalding worked, in conjunction with the Home
Office, a piece of island grabbing at the back of
N.S.W. Parliament and public. If, they contend,
a Premier and a Governor can, without consulting
Parliament, appropriate one island, they may ap-
propriate any number. Anyhow, the debate will
show by what constitutional tight N.S.W. can
ilow force Norfolk Islanders to obey its laws.
There is very little left How of Coghlan’s
famous calculations on Federal finance. They
were mostly based on the amount of customs and
excise revenue which each province would hand
over to the Commonwealth, the estimate being
necessarily built on these revenues as they were
last financial year and for a certain time pre-
viously. Now the Westralian revenue has dimin-
ished fully 10 per cent., and that of Tasmania has
increased about 10 per cent., and Victoria’s
revenue shows a large expansion, and the whole
calculation wants to be done over again. And
before the ink was dry on the new calculation the
rush of circumstances would probably have over-
set it also.
The Australasian has dropped an apology
and -£400 legal costs on account of a clumsy para-
graph allegation that a certain Vic. M.P. suffers
from a humpback. The journal paid full price for
its ‘‘fun,” considering that the member had no
apparent case. To call a man a “ humpback ” is
not holding him up to ridicule and contempt. It
is merely an unintentional means of inviting sym-
pathy for him, insofar that his feelings are sup-
posed to have been gratuitously hurt. But the
law doesn’t protect public men’s feelings concern-
ing their personal appearance. Uncalled-for
ridicule is left to condemn itself in the opinion of
the public.
Early one recent morning an official at one
of the Melbourne cemeteries was shocked to find
alow, unkempt drunk spread out on his back on
the well-tended grave of a gentleman who was
once of considerable local consequence. The
beat had his head in a white wreath under the
shadow of a most respectable monolith, and his
limbs were thrown far and wide over the sacred
lot, which was surrounded with the usual gilt
iron-railing. The official could not awaken the
new lodger, and the job gave a stentorian police-
man much trouble. The only excuse the man of
no importance offered was: “Well, it was the
softest bed I’ve had for years, an’ old W
didn’t object to my sharing it with him.” Old
W was the man down under.

AN ABSOLUTE FACT.
PLAY ; in Three Acts.
PERIOD— 2Ist APRIL, 1898.
Act I.—The boss of Mohonga Station put down his paper with an anxious look
on his face. He was thinking of his wool on its way to ’Frisco and of the
Spanish cruisers on the Pacific. There was only one course to be followed,
. and that was to get a wire off to his agent immediately. “Whom shall I send
with the wire ? Of course, there is Bob and the brown mare ; but though Bob
is here, the brown mare was turned out into the horse paddock this morning,
and she is always bad to catch at any time. Then there is Jim with his brand
new ‘ SWIFT ’ bicycle that he is always talking about. Perhaps it would be
safest after all to send them both, and then it will be settled, once for all,
which is the most reliable—the horse or the bicycle.”
Act ll.—Jim gets away at once, and Bob proceeds to try and catch the mare,
while the boss shouts instructions in forcible language.

From the Shield, Sydney “ Christadel-
phian ” magazine :
preceding one of the announcements of
the advent of Christ, allusion is made to “frog-like
spirils of devils’’ active in the preparation of the nations
for the war of the great day of God Almighty. Who can
be blind to the abnormal preponderance of French
activity in mixing the ingredients of the world’s disorder
just now ? Far and near, by direct interference or in-
sidious engineering, France is fanning the flame of
national hostility.
Let not the unregenerate laugh ! What more
natural than that God, as an undoubted Britisher,
should symbolically associate France with the
good old British frog-gag. Probably, if Revela-
tion had needed to refer to Germany, there would
have been something said about “ sauer-krout ” or
liver-wiirst.
Maryborough (Q.) is proud of several
things. In the first place, Maryborough is desper-
ately proud of its river, which is about the most
sinuous and cantankerous stream on the Q. coast.
Secondly, Maryborough is inordinately vain of its
member, the aitchless Jack Annear, Chairman of
Committees. But Maryborough’s chief crown of
glory is that it is “ absolutely the largest city in
the world, sir,” inasmuch as its 10,000 inhabitants
are spread over a municipality of 22 sq. miles.
And now some iconoclast has gone and dis-
covered that Maryborough’s claim is invalid even
as against Queensland, let alone the world,
inasmuch as Gayndah, on the Upper Burnett,
numbers 500 inhabitants on a municipal area of ’
31 sq. miles ! Poor Maryborough ! Ichabod !
Ichabod !

To an inland N.S.W. district where spin-
sters predominate came, the other day, a new
Anglican curate handsome, bashful, and 23.
The dear girls were greatly excited, and enquired
anxiously if he would be present at the next local
birthday-party. He came, and the boldest girl
bagged him for herself at an early stage of the
proceedings, and fixed him in a small room away
from the large one where the rest were playing
forfeits. But that curate wouldn’t talk. He
was so distressingly shy that he blushed when-
ever the girl spoke to him, and her best efforts at
conversation only brought “ Oh, yes, Miss ! ”
or “ Oh, no, Miss ! ” At last she grew dis-
gusted and desperate. Drawing her chair up very
close to that of the young shepherd, who
shrank away in alarm, she suddenly squealed :
“ Oh, don’t, Mr. ! Please, don’t, Mr.
! ” The forfeits were suddenly suspended,
and every ear turned to the little room where the
curate was agonisedly searching for the reason of
tins remarkable conduct. But his hair stood
right up as the girl suddenly printed half-a-dozen
sounding kisses on the back of her hand, and fol-
lowed them up with a shriek of “Mother!
mother ! he’s kissing me! ” which brought the
forfeit-party in a rush to the room. The curate
didn’t wait to receive them. He ran out of
another door, dodged through a bedroom, and out
of the house —hatless and umbrellaless, but with
his virtue, thank God ! intact. That was two
months ago, and he has never been seen out of
church since.

While on tour once, a certain smart young Australian
comedian, convinced that he had impressed a young
wife staying at the same hotel, found the zealous supervi-
sion of the husband an effectual bar to personal address. On
Sunday, the husband was absent from tea, and the come-
dian promptly slipped into the vacant seat. The menu
was written on a piece of note-paper. This the charmer
absent-mindedly folded, and pushed along the table to her
neighbor, who, scenting a billet-doux, slil.v pocketed it,
much to the astonishment of the lady, who was greatly
embarrassed by queries as to where the menu had irot to.
Imagine the actor-man’s chagrin when, on retiring to his
room, he read “ Pig’s cheek, cold ham and tongue, salad,”
Ac.
The ludicrously gross personal abuse extracted from
toe actor-fraternity hy Nugent Robertson’s forceful and
good-humored criticism in Sydney D.T. of W. Barrett’s
Ilamlet lends color to the notion that an innate soul-vul-
ganty goes with the mummer temperament, and that an
essential pettiness of character is grained in the man who
takes by choice to the sham chivalry and footlight mag-
nanimity of the boards. And, after all, the only result of
calling N.R., in effect, “liar,” “thief,” “drunkard,”
“ coward,” has been to convince those who haven’t seen
Barrett’s Hamlet that all this screeching means simply—
“No case ! —abuse the other side.” But, anyhow—what
a clumsy figure the unghosted mummer cuts when he
engages in literary fence with a man who can write
with a knife!

Avon : “The third act of ‘Hamlet’ is the climax of
the world’s acting drama. Strange to remark, I liked
Bahdmann’s setting—the German setting—of the Cham-
ber scene better than any. The walls show life-size por-
traits of the kings. The late king and Claudius face
one another. Hamlet apostrophises the Usurper’s pic-
™re with ‘ A king of shreds ar d patches ! ’ Lights down.
The Ghost flashes from the picture of the dead king.
Apparently it supports the idea taken up by Miln that
the Ghost in this scene is only the Eidolon of Hamlet's
brain, wherefore Miln had no ‘Jhost there, only a kind of
spot of electric-light.”
T.D. : “ Conductor Hazon a tv. ays impresses me, at Syd-
ney philharmonic or orchestral concerts, as a sort of
embodiment of the human deity-ideal, wielding the
‘ stick ’ for angelic choirs. The Continentals, by virtue of
their tireless energy of limb, and facial intensity of ex-
pression will always boss musical Bull, and, also (it
seems), his Australian brood. Waving of arms will fire
the coldest—the musician's power is the orator’s strength,
and if he can stamp his feet as well (as Hazon sometimes
does) so much the better.”

Tried Baith : “ How much more delightful is the
Variety Stage than the Regular ! Unless vou are a top-
svwyer the record of the utility actor is ‘mostly wasted
t me. The best part of the day you are dog-holed at the
playhouse for rehearsal, and have to hang about the
coulisses all the evening. Few can study at such times
even if there be aught to study, and the time goes in

Clement Scott’s climb-down over his strictures upon

City people stand on end when some members of a depu-

she dumbfounded her auditors by an exclamatory
use of the name of the Deity. She was very anxious to
know how many wives they had, and if she was likely to
receive any proposals of marriage while in Salt Lake. The
visitors were not ungallant enough to discourage her bv
n negative reply, but they might have very safelv assured
her that she could possess her sweet soul ’in patience on
that, score. She was curious to know whether there were
any Mormon ladies on the train, and, on being informed
t hat there were, she was asked if she would not like to
meet them, in view of the fact that they had come out to
welcome her. ‘ Oh, dear, no 1 ’ she exclaimed ; supple-
menting the declination with the confession that she was
entirely ‘too piggy ’—whatever that, may mean. The
acoustic properties of the Tabernacle were deplored, be-
cause, forsooth, ‘I won’t be able to say “damn” under
my tireath in the event that anything goes wrong.’ It is

i :«ult—she seemed to believe she was actually jocular.

t'leir tickets entitle them to hear her sing—not to watch
her rudeness nor listen to her conversation.” Which all
r ids like a very improbable Mormon joke.

Cloth rises on darkened stage. Discovered leaning on’
reversed rifle, in uniform of Jameson's Mounted Police,
over rough grave. Comes down and sings song describing
death of his chum. Chorus—
My Chum Dick was true as the steel he wore,
Never he failed hiscomradeas the battle brunt we bore ;
Fell like a soldier fighting, gallant and true to the end,
My Chum Dick died trying to save his friend.
After last cborus, kneels over the grave—picture ! Limes
shut off, and in darkness prop, is moved and singer
changes. 2nd song.—Lights up, full stage set, back
cloth and wings, forest. (Australian pastoral scene in
preparation). Introduction—orchestra. During sym-
phony enter L O.E. and gallop down to O.P. proscenium,
pulling up with a jerk, one stride from footlights, swing
and crack stockwhip (eleven feet long), and calls Coo-oo-
ee-ee-ee ! (Australian bush-cry). Song descriptive of life
of a stock-rider. Second chorus, accompanied by swing
and crack of the whip. Chorus—
Out in the wild bush riding, mounted on stockhorse free
Lightly bounding, stockwhip sounding, that is the life
for me ;
Danger ennobles labour, so have the sages told, ,
Hand, eye, and heart, each bear their part in the life of a
stockman bold.
Between verses, passage about stage, and gallop down to
conductor. After last chorus, swing and race off into
wings. Where circumstances allow, a hurdle jump will
be placed to exit over."
Its the fashion now in Melb. to advertise your encores

ance of the encore-fiend.
Holdar M.: “In the theatre, scenery, actors’ ‘ make-
up,’ lowered lights, &c., are all carefully calculated to
trick the observer’s eye, yet the powers who do this deli-
berately defeat their own purpose by leasing opera-glasses
to their clients, with which to dispel the illusion. More-
over, the play altogether is damaged by the distraction
of attention caused by the use of this remorseless disillu-
sioniser. I denounce the instrument as alien to the
interests of the ‘ perfesh ’ and destructive of the artistic
appreciation of the audience.” But how about people
who can t see ? And who can see anything through the
kind of opera-glass they lease at the theatre ?

Showmen take an absurdly selfish view of their news-
paper transactions. A draper will buy eight columns of
space in one issue without asking for a jammy criticism
of his spring show. Where’s the difference between seats
for sale and socks for sale, as far as the advertising of
them is concerned ?

the bulk of citizens. He has no fixed status in the com-
munity, and no prying neighbors to spot his ‘ goings-on ’

His private movements are unobserved amid t the

the vagabond, and as he cqntinually • moves on,’ he
gathers not the moss of Respectability.”
A gossip concerning Duse, Bernhardt’s rival orsuperior :
“ She is very sad, the saddest woman I have ever known
She cannot even bear people’s voices. After the si rain of
her performance she drives home quite alone, and sits
down to supper in solitude and silence. Duse, chez elle
dresses almost always in white satin. She wears no cor-
sets, and does not powder or paint, even on the stage
She bundles her pretty back hair up anvhow, and is quite
proud of the one startling white look that sweeps across
her temple. She is a charming woman, highly cultured
sincere, brave, and good.”

Queensland is, as provinces go, a fairly
prosperous province, and yet it contains a vast
amount of poverty-bred misery within its borders.
A mother at Tsisford cut her baby’s throat, the
other day, because “ I am too poor to maintain
another child.” How often does a mother commit
this form of infanticide ? Not once in a decade.
George Black, M.L.A., on the unalter-
ableness of the Federal Constitution :
Mr. Barton had stated that the American Constitution
had been altered 15 times, but three of these amendments
were after the Civil War, when the N_>rth held bayonets
at the breasts of the Southern States.
Then it follows that the other 12 amendments
were made in time of peace, and the U.S. Con-
stitution is capable of amendment, and the Aus-
tralian Federal Constitution, being much more
elastic than the U.S. one, is much more capable of
amendment.
Premier Byrnes: — “l do not believe
that all the constitutional changes under the sun
would really increase the wealth, or the produc-
tion, or add to the ultimate happiness of the
people the true things for which a Government
exists.” Why. then, should T. J. Byrnes go
about humbugging his Irish supporters by advo-
cating Home Rule ? Surely that’s a constitutional
change—yet Byrnes says it won’t make Ireland
one whit the happier !

“Old Jack” :
A former residence in Manila enables me to confirm the
assertion that the place reeks with sacerdotalism of the
rankest brand. Not only do the ecclesiastics import
their influence into the Government, but they constitute
a most mischievous social element. Their liv es are openly
corrupt, and when I was there a very high ecclesiastic
was daily seen driving abroad with his acknowledged
mistress a fat mestiza—by his side. And among the
sacerdotal rank-and-file are manv of (he type of Roderick
Random’s reverend pilot from Calais to Paris. If you
want to see a certain kind of “ life ” in Manila, you will
get your best gmde from the army of ecclesiastical
beggars that infests the place. For a dollar he will intro-
duce you to the local Tot-tie, and royster with you as long
as you like—at your expense. At the cock fights, huil
fights, and theatres, you will always see the shovel-hat,
or the cowl and shaven crown, well represented. The
apostle of ignorance and superstition and the exemplar
of gross immorality, the Spanish ecclesiastic will appear
to future historians to explain much of the rapid deei-
dence of the Spanish nation. To Manila, at any rate, he
has always been a curse. Of course there are exceptions
I have met some noble ones—but the rule is that the
ecclesiastic of every kind, as represented in the Philip-
pines, is a bad lot,. And yet, in justice, it must be
admitted that in cholera times, the priests and monks
and friars do fearless service in hospital and house. They
are, in a word, still exactly what they were in the days
of Torquemada and the Auto da Fe.

Lazarus : “Again, re sleeping out, and what
not. lam scientific on the loafe. It is my grand
panacea, the fantee, for which see one of Kipling’s
yarns, the * Haroun Al-Raschid ’ business, getting
out of yourself, losing your identity, getting, as it
were, a pawn-ticket for yourself. Edmund Kean,
in America, disappeared for months. ‘ Where
have you been ? ’ asked an excited manager.
‘Been? Why I have been an Indian chief!’
And what a little giant refreshed he was, acting his
Othello, Shylock, King Dick. At the city loafe,
suburban loafe, and bush loafe I am equally at
home. They re-sane me, like a dog medicining
on grass. A glorious loafing book is_ Jefferies’
* Nature Near London,’ but, indeed, his inimitable
power is all in the gentle loafe.”
“By and by there was a result; and I
think it was the finest thing in Australasian
history. It was a revolution —small in size, but
great politically ; it was a strike for liberty, a
struggle for a principle, a stand against injustice
and oppression. It was the Barons and John over
again pit was Hampden and Ship-money; it was
Concord and Lexington ; small beginnings, all of
them, but all of them great in political results, all
of them epoch-making. It is another instance of
a victory won by a lost battle. It adds an honor-
able page to history ; the people know it and
are proud of it. They keep green the memory
of the men who fell at Eureka Stockade, and
Peter Lalor has his monument.” — Makk Twain,
in “ More Tramps Abroad.”
M.L. Police-Commissioner Tunbridge went
down badly before a cold-tea parson who, in
a certain small town, deposed before the Com-
mission of Inauiry that the police had winked at
the existence m a local pub of a second, and there-
fore illegal, bar. Tunbridge had meanwhile
quietly dispatched a constable to make inquiry,
and, when the officer returned with the assurance
that the illicit bar had not been open for 12
months, the Commissioner fired this trifling
“ fact ” into the holy man, who thereupon, amidst
“much amusement,” departed in apparent dis-
comfiture. In a quarter of an hour, however,
back he came, got himself re-sworn, and, when
everybody was expecting him to “take it all
back ” —popped upon the table a flask of whisky
which he had just bought at the illegally non-
existent bar. Tableau !—and a rough time for the
publican.

A wealthy Sydney citizen (a British sub-
ject, by the way) is appealing to the courts to
have it declared that, because he is consul for
a one-horse foreign power, he is free from all
personal taxation, and needn’t pay his income-
tax like a common mortal. If he wins his case,
and it is more than possible that the law
is on his side, many other tieh men in Australia
will probably seek to be consul for some place
or other. To be a consul carries with it the right
to wear a lovely uniform and have the entree of
Government House ; and if it also means exemp-
tion from taxes the local capitalist will promptly
write to some impecunious republic and offer to be
its consul gratis. As far as The Bulletin knows,
Guatemala, Honduras, Monaco, Andorra, Bok-
hara, Abyssinia, Afghanistan, Baluchistan, Bul-
garia, Congo, Corea, Havti, Dominica, Morocco,
Servia, Orange Free State, Transvaal, &c., &c.,
are all unrepresented, and probably even the
tiredest of them wouldn’t be too poor or too tired
to let any gilded person (even if he could speak
no language but some sort of English) supply
his own uniform and office, and put his consular
shingle outside the door at his own cost, ana
escape his income-tax. The Bulletin has got its
own eye on the consulship of Baluchistan.
Nervous sufferers will find it worth their
while to peruse Herr Rassmussen’s advt. on page
27 of this issue.*

An Unsought Meeting.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The down-train had passed through Boltfon Junc-
tion and whirled on towards its terminus, and
there would be nothing further until the side-
line trains came in from Shawton and Holmby to
pick up stray passengers. So the station-master,
after glancing curiously at the two unfortunates
in the waiting-room, dropped in this waste by the
Central whose distant lights were fast disappear-
ing, pulled his collar up and his hat down, and,
with anathemas on the “ beastly night,” proceeded
to wade across to his own shanty, the light of
which gleamed like a star across the intervening
swamp:
It was a beastly night! Further up the swamp
one of the culverts had given way, and only the
slight elevation of the station protected it from
the flood. The wind shrieked and howled round
the little waiting-room so that other sounds were
subdued ; but between the gusts the rain could be
heard in a steady downpour, and some of it, fal-
ling down thachimney between whiles, succeeded
in partly quenching the fire and filling the place
with smoke.
Now and then a flash of lightning through the
unblinded window would make the quiet figure in
the solitary chair start and shiver a little in her
furs. An incongruous figure in that desolate
place, with a refined and delicate, rather than
beautiful, face, and a certain grace of movement ;
a figure indeed—somewhat strange to Maoriland
primitiveness—of a woman of culture and travel,
who had seen much and had not bought her clothes
“in the provinces.”
Her fellow-traveller, regardless of lightning-
flash, paced moodily up and down the room—up
and down, up and down, making his footfall
blend with other sounds of the night, in desolate
accompaniment to his thoughts.
Gray he was beyond his years, and with that in-
describable droop of the shoulders that brands the
man who has dropped out of the swim. Still,
spite of unkempt beard and slop-made clothes,
unmistakably a gentleman, but without any of
the old buoyant grace that had distinguished him
the last time these two had met. And so it is
not surprising that after the first involuntary
greeting of these two wayfarers, blown from the
train, as it were, into rude shelter by the storm,
his fellow-traveller had shown no sign of recogni-
tion. The soft brown eyes, the man remembered
with a pang, had not been wont to be short-sighted
in the past.
Fifteen years before, these two, fellow passen-
gers then from England to Wellington, had become
betrothed. But at Wellington there had been
some slight misunderstanding, and they parted—
she going to Australia, while he found his way
to Taranaki, where Te Whiti was playing Bible
stories with sticks and trumpets and things.
Had she ever cared, he wondered —for he had
known her at first glance. He rather fancied not,
but he did not blame her —women were made that
way, he supposed He had volunteered for the
“ war,” and afterwards “ gone the pace,” money
and prospects (he had been heavily gilded) and
self-respect fast vanishing ; and she had repented,
bitterly at first, and had then forgotten, and
married happily after all. Her present visit to
Maoriland was to her brother, a Wealthy run-
holder up the line; and her present uncomfortable
experience belonged to the flood —the train usually
reached Holmby long before nightfall. She was
glad, as she had to be locked up with him, that
this man was a gentleman, but she had not the
faintest remembrance of ever having seen him
before.
Suddenly the door opened, letting in, together
with a gust of rain and wind that extinguished the
light of the little swinging lamp, a Maori girl or
woman—in the dim light it was hard to distin-
guish which. There was an exclamation from the
man as he struck a match, and his eyes fell on the
new-comer. She grunted back something in
Maori, either of greeting or of ejaculation, and
then squatted on the floor in front of the fire,
drawing her shawl over her head till her face was
hidden. Gradually she sank forward and slept,
oblivious to all the sounds of the strife raging so
fiercely outside. As she lay there unconscious, her
shawl fell back, revealing in the flickering light
traces of rude beauty which must have at one
time been marked, but which was now marred and
disfigured by bad passions.
She had come from some neighboring pah—the
odoiir peculiar to the Maori whare proclaimed that.
The added fumes of stale tobacco and staler
whisky told that she was even now probably
sleeping off the effect of some late potations.
The man, after re-lighting the lamp, resumed
his march across the floor—a funeral march over
dead joys, dead ambition, dead passion, dead
A grunt of discomfort from the sleeper on the
floor broke in on his musing, and with a shudder
he passed out into the storm.
An hour later, as the station-master hurried
down to meet the incoming train, a dark figure
moved out of the rain towards the open door of
the waiting-room. It was, to eyes inured to dark-
ness, a blaze of light in which the two women
stood framed for an instant—the one drawing up
her shawl, with a gesture of disgust, ere stepping
into the rain ; the other pale enough, for the smell
of those wet garments before the fire had been
stifling, but, as she peered with short-sighted eyes
into rue darkness, unmistakeably an English-
woman of refinement —good and pure and clean.
On the steps she slipped, and would have fallen,
but a hand out of the darkness caught her, and,
with a muttered “ Pardon me ! ” guided her across
the platform. At the carriage-door he paused a
moment, and then, at last, in the light of the
train, she saw and recognised him. Something in
his attitude, in his haggard face, in the whole
hopeless appearance of the man, broke down any
reserve she might have felt, and she held out her
hand with the old winning graciousness. “ I have
only this moment remembered you,” she said.
“Will you not, for the sake of old times, let me
hope you are prospering and—and happy,” she
added, for the expression in the tired eyes shocked
her.
The window of a smoking-compartment in the
other train ran up with a bang, and the shawled
head of the lately-recumbent figure in the waiting-
room reached out into the night. “ Ered ! ” called
a voice, singularly sweet and refined. “Fred ! ”
she called again; “wherever are you? You’ll
miss the train ! ”
“Fred” stepped back, lifting his hat with a
strange smile, as he looked for the last time at the
kind eyes he had seen in dreams. Then he crossed
to the other train and rejoined the Maori woman
-.-whom, in a drunken fit, years before, he had
married.
And the two trains steamed out of the station —
one going east, the other west.
A.D.B.
Mates.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Mick and Bob were mates. Mick was well-known
in the township—none better. The priest af-
firmed Mick “ the makin’s of a man, I tell ye ” ;
the flock, divided on most points, was unanimous
in declaring Michael Francis Casey a true imp of
Gehenna—a credit to his country, and a curse to
his locality. He was red ; red-haired youngsters
are always devils.
When Sunday eve’s church-goers were scattered
by old Nolan’s “ Cherry ” in full career through
Balbarrup’s only road, with all Balbarrup’s count-
less cows and curs full-tilt in close pursuit, they
“ wondhered ” whence she got that “ karosene-
tin.” But young Bob Nolan’s deep-rooted anti-
pathy-born of numberless milkings and drivings
in frosty winters—to the order of Ruminantia in
general, and to Cherry in particular, was well-
known to the “ old man,” who, partly from sheer
paternal affection, and partly from desire to be at
issue with ould Dan Casey, rejected Dan’s theory
(likewise influenced by paternal love), and main-
tained “’Tis my own Bob’s divilcraft; an’ —an’
Dan ! Howld hard ! mark me—after Rosary ! ”
Mick, too, “ wondhered,” and at nightfall am-
bushed in anticipation “near Bob’s place.” Nor
was the vigil vain. The whacking that awaited
him for being out late, being as yet but prospec-
tive, mattered little. Besides, Bob wouldn’t know,
while ’twould be sweet on the morrow to ask:
“ How d’ it fit lars nite, Bob?” At the gleeful
idea he chuckled.
Bob was Mick’s mate.
• * * *
Next morning Mick was late for school. “ Pie’,
sir, mother was bad, an’ I had to go to Mrs.
Nolan’s without no brekfust, sir.” He grinned,
and winked significantly as— scathless—he sat by
Bob. Then they yarned, each with a keen eye on
the Desk. “ Mother ain’t bad at all—on’y Spider
Brien’s Lass had pups ’smornin’ an’ so I went
t’ave first pick.”
“ Who’s their father, Mick ? ”
“ Think mine's by Bunny Gray’s Rajah ; he’s a
beauty, Bob—black, with brown spots over the
eyes. Goin’ to cut his tailnex’ week. The others
is all mongrels, tho’ —they all squealed when I
lifted ’em by the ears.”
“ Er ver crissened him yet ? ”
“ Think I’ll call him ‘Ranji,’ after that Injin
bloke that licks ’em at cricket.”
Bob was interested, and while Mick muttered
mechanically “ seven nines is fifty-four,” he hur-
riedly made a mental inventory of negotiable
assets; then broke in on the calculation. “I’ll
give yea knife, Scotch alley, ’n a bit o’ string.”
“No! wouldn’t take a poun’ for him; ye see
he’s .” He stopped abruptly, cast a searching
eye at the Desk ; then continued : “ Oh cripes !
didn’t the ole man lay it on lars nite, Bob? Ye
didn’t think I was lis’nin. Cripes, I did larf—
‘ Father, father ! I never done it, not this time,
father.’ Did you dive for the blanket ?”
Bob winced under the lash of Mick’s mimicry ;
but slowly his puzzled brain unravelled the tangle
of the mystery—and promptly up shot his hand.
“ Pie’, sir, Mick Casey’s mother ain’t bad at all ;
he jes’ tole me.” Mick, cornered, lied manfully,
but uselessly, to the joy of Bob, whose eyes glit-
tered supreme delight as his victim, mingling
howls with groans, wriggled vigorously into gro-
tesque yet protective attitudes.
The ordeal past : “ What yer rubbin fer,
Mick? Cripes, you can squirm—them pants is
pretty thin too! ” Mick, glowering revenge,
passed a sleeve across his now half-cleaned face,
and continued the ameliorating friction.
Mick was Bob’s mate.
* * * *
For a full week the feud flared —Bob ever on the
alert to evade the strategies skilfully laid by his
mate; the mate ever planning new plots to decoy
the wary foe. But as they met, one evening, the
energy usually bubbling in him of the red hair
seemed stilled and silent. Despondently he
drooped his head, and two white guttered streaks
contrasted plainly against the grimy cheeks.
“ Bob ! Bob ! Come here, I want yer ! ”
Distrustfully Bob neared—Cherry answering
the lever-like movement of her tail as a yacht the
helm. “Got’nuther hidin’, Mick?”
But the taunt brought no retort; and, his sus-
picions fast vanishing, Bob came closer —and
closer yet when bitter sorrow forced honest ex-
pression. Mick’s big blue eyes were set in crystal
lakes. Came the genuine query: “ What’s wrong,
Mickey ? ”
“ Ranji’s dead ! ” Mick gulped.
Cherry’s tail fell flick against her heels, and the
two mates slowly crossed the paddock, yarning
mournfully as they walked. Pulling two palings
off the fence, they squeezed through into the yard,
and behind the fowlhouse, to where Ranji, like a
pendulum, swung and swayed from the clothes-
line. A long silence spoke their sorrow.
“ Mother said he et th’ eggs.” (A pause.)
“ Hung ! ” said Bob, reflectively ; and then, im-
pulsively, “Don’t mind, Mick, yer can have one
of our Nip’s—when she has ’em. I don’t s’pose
she’ll be long.”
Then the tw'o mates cut him down, and buried
him.
W.A.
SPRAT.
Maurice Low, the brainy Washington
(U.S.) journalist whose articles on American
affairs in National Observer, and other front-
rank English papers, have lately attracted wide
attention, is a brother of EL 1. Low, of S.M.
Herald, and of S. Low, late editor of St. J ames’s
Gazette.
Chamberlain’s proposal that, in the event
of Federation being delayed, the permanent artil-
lery of all the Australias should be amalgamated
under the title of the “ Royal Australian Artil-
lery,” the force in each province forming a regi-
ment or battery of that body, contains the pith of
a sound Federal idea, but is inaccurate in the use
it makes of English words. Why Royal ? Is
there, or is there likely to be, any other Aus-
tralian Artillery from which this: one would need
to be discriminated ? To put the word “ Royal ’
before the Australian military body contemplated
is inane surplusage.
An eminent English doctor, recently de-
ceased, was once between the horns of a cruel
dilemma in respect of some hospital doctors, who
ha ving ignorantly treated for arsenical-poisoning
a man who hadn’t taken arsenic, but who never-
theless died—had afterwards pumped some of the
poison into the corpse’s stomach. The doctor in
question, having arrived at this conclusion from
the fact that no arsenic had passed into the intes-
tines, found himself compelled either to expose
the “cloth ” or to permit the ruin of the chemist
who had prudently palmed off upon the deceased,
as arsenic, a harmless drug. The “cloth’’went
down !

and had at last been cured almost miraculously, a reporter called to get particulars of her case, and
the following is the lady’s story :

pains in my chest and between my shoulders. I was so bad that I found it impossible to get through

A Maoriland Slaughter.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I'he Maoris had been giving the Government a lot
of trouble in the Upper Thames district. They
had hampered the operations of the men who were
doing the triangular survey; indeed, they had
gone so far that shots had been fired, and some of
the survey-party had been wounded.
It was Easter-time, and the Government ar
ranged that the Waikato Cavalry—about 200
strong—should ride through the disaffected coun-
try down to the Thames to take part in the
Easter encampment, thus killing two birds with
one stone. The troopers would get a fortnight’s
training, and the Maoris a fright.
We started on our 200-mile ride with fresh
horses and light hearts. Everything in proper
military style ; advance and rear guards, baggage-
waggons, and all the rest of it
W® marched by way of Te Aroha, but, as the
golaneids had not been discovered, there were no
bridges over the rivers, and the roads were little
better than Maori tracks.
We were expecting to be stopped in some of
the many bushes and defiles on the way, and when,
after passing through the old mining township of
Uhinemuri, we reached the scene of the native
trouble we were more than ever prepared for hos-
tilities, because at camp the night before we had
been visited by natives, both men and women,
who, we learnt afterwards, had come to ascertain
it we were too numerous to deal with. They evi-
dently us so, for when we passed
through their village next day not a Maori was
to be seen. The whole place was given up to
Oaptain Cook pigs and mongrel dogs.
We arrived at the Thames in due course ; and,
when the manoeuvres were over, started on our
homeward ride. On coming again to the border
ot the native country the alarm was given. The
advance-guard had been stopped by an old chief
and a whole tribe of Maoris, and word came
hastily back to the main column that the Colonel
was vvanted at the front. A halt was called, and
we all made ready for the fray. We were ordered
to advance in single file, and moved forwards in
a long line. “ Halt! Dismount! Couple horses !
Draw swords ! Attack ! ’’-and slash went two
hundred sabres, cutting and thrusting like mad,
tor we had got so worked up with excitement that
we were determined to give no quarter ; and pre-
senthy on all sides lay our victims—shedding the
life s-blood with which our swords were also drip-
ping. But worse remained—for we now proceeded
to make preparations for devouring the slain—
water-melons !
Yes, there they were; one for each man in the
troop, and placed about six feet apart in a straight
line, along which we had been drawn up. Only
those who have had a long and dusty march can
tell how we appreciated the result of that skir-
mish.
The Maoris were our guests at a dance in camp
that evening.
That was the only time my sword was drawn on
active service.

The most fetching printer’s error up to
date comes from a Victorian provincial paper.
I* rom an obituary : “ His endeavor was always to
make Ins wife a useful one to his neighbors.” The
wretched editor wrote it “ life,” of course.
The Writer’s Dream-
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A writer wrote of the hearts of men, and he fol-
lowed their tracks afar;
For his was a spirit that forced his pen to write
of the things that are.
His heart grew tired of the truths he told, for his
life was hard and grim ;
His land seemed barren, its people cold ;— yet the
world was dear to him ;
So he sailed away from the Streets of Strife, he
travelled by land and sea,
In search of a people who lived a life as life in the
world should be.
And he reached a spot where the scene was fair,
with forest and field and wood,
And all things came with the seasons there, and
each of its kind was good ;
There were mountain-rivers and peaks of snow,
there were lights of green and gold,
And echoing caves in the cliffs below, where a
world-wide ocean rolled.
The lives of men from the wear of Change and
the strife of the world were free : —
For Steam was barred by the mountain-range and
the rocks of the Open Sea.
And the last that were born of a noble race—when
the page of the South was fair—
The last of the conquered dwelt in peace with the
last of the victors there.
He saw their hearts with the author’s eyes who
had written their ancient lore,
And he saw their lives as he’d dreamed of such—
ah ! many a year before.
And “ I’ll write a book of these simple folk ere I
to the world return,
And the cold who read shall be kind for these—
and the wise who read shall learn.
Never again in a song of mine shall a jarring
note be heard:
Never again shall a page or line be marred by a
bitter word ;
But love and laughter and kindly hours will the
book I’ll write recall,
With chastening tears for the loss of one, and sighs
for their sorrows all.
Old eyes will light with a kindly smile, and the
young eyes dance with glee—
And the heart of the cynic will rest awhile for my
simple folk and me.”
The lines ran on as he dipped his pen—ran true to
his heart and ear—
Like the brighter pages of memory when every
line is clear.
The pictures came and the pictures passed, like
days of love and light—
He saw his chapters from first to last, and he
thought it grand to write
And the writer kissed his girlish wife, and he
kissed her twice for pride:
“ ’Tis a book of love, though a book of life ! and
a book you will read ! ” he cried.
He was blind at first to each senseless slight (for
shabby and poor he came)
From local “ Fashion ” and mortgaged pride that
scarce could sign its name.
What dreamer would dream of such paltry pride
in a scene so fresh and fair ?
But the local spirit intensified-—with its pitiful
shams —was there ;
There were cliques wherever two houses stood
(No rest for a family ghost!)
They hated each other as women could —but they
hated the stranger most.
The writer wrote by day and night and he cried
in the face of Fate—
“ I’ll cleave to my dream of life in spite of the
cynical ghosts that wait.
’Tis the shyness born of their simple lives,” he
said of the paltry pride—
(The homely tongues of the simple wives ne’er
erred on the generous side) —
“ They’ll prove me true and they’ll prove me kind
ere the year of grace be past,”
But the ignorant whisper of “ axe to grind ! ”
went home to his heart at last.
The writer sat by his drift-wood fire three nights
of the South-east gale,
His pen lay idle on pages vain, for his book was a
fairy tale.
The world-wise lines of an elder age were plain on
his aching brow,
As he sadly thought of each brighter page that
would never be written now.
“ I’ll write no more ! ” But he bowed his head,
for his heart was in Dreamland yet—
“ The pages written I’ll burn,” he said, “ and the
pages thought forget.”
But he heard the hymn of the Open Sea, and the
old fierce anger burned,
And he wrenched his heart from its dreamland
free as the fire of his youth returned :
“ The weak man’s madness, the strong man’s
scorn—the rebellious hate of youth
From a deeper love of the world are born ! And
the cynical ghost is Truth ! ”
And the writer rose with a strength anew wherein
Doubt could have no part;
“I’ll write my book and it shall be true—the
truth of a writer’s heart.”
“ Ay! cover the wrong with a fairy tale—who
never knew want or care—
A bright green scum on a stagnant pool that will
reek the longer there.
You may starve the writer and buy the pen—you
may drive it with want and fear—
But the lines run false in the hearts of men—and
false to the writer’s ear.
The bard’s a rebel and strife his part, and he’ll
burst from his bonds anew,
Till all pens write from a single heart! And so
may the dream come true. ”
“ Tis ever the same in the paths of men where
money and dress are all,
The crawler will bully whene’er he can, and the
bully who can’t will crawl.
And this is the creed in the local hole, where the
souls of the selfish rule :
Borrow and cheat while the stranger’s green, and
then sneer at the simple fool.
Spit your spite at the men whom Fate has placed
in the head-race first,
And hate till death, with a senseless hate, the
man you have injured worst!
“ There are generous hearts in the grinding street,
but the Hearts of the World go west;
For the men who toil in the dust and heat of the
barren lands are best!
The stranger’s hand to the stranger, yet—for a
roving folk are mine—
The stranger’s store for the stranger set—and the
camp-fire glow the sign !
The generous hearts of the world, we find, thrive
best on the barren sod,
And the selfish thrive where Nature’s kind (they’d
bully or crawl to God !).
“ I was born to write of the things that are ! and
the strength was given to me ;
I was born to strike at the things that mar the
world as the world should be !
By the dumb heart-hunger and dreams of youth,
by the hungry tracks I’ve trod—
I’ll fight as a man for the sake of truth, nor pose
as a martyred god.
By the heart of ‘Bill’ and the heart of ‘Jim,’
and the men that their hearts deem ‘white’;
By the handgrips fierce, and the hard eyes dim
with forbidden tears ! —l’ll write !
“I’ll write untroubled by cultured fools, or the
dense that fume and fret —
Por against the wisdom of all their schools I would
stake mine instinct yet!
Por the cynical strain in the writer’s song is the
world, not he, to blame,
And I’ll write as I think, in the knowledge strong
that thousands think the same ;
And the men who fight in the Dry Country grim
battles by day, by night,
Will believe in me, and will stand by me, and will
say to the world, ‘ He’s right! ’ ”
M.L.
HENRY LAWSON.
Captain Phillip, Australia’s first Governor,
owes his reputation as a naval commander to his
prowess in the war between Portugal and Spain.
He hired himself out to Portugal, and in a num-
ber of engagements so acquitted himself against
the Spanish enemy that the British Government
later on selected him to found the colony of
Botany Bay.
A discussion as to whether Clough’s “ ’Tis
better to have fought and lost than never to have
fought at all,” published 1849, was cribbed from
Tennyson’s “ Better to have loved and lost,” &c.,
published 1850, was enlivened by a quotation
from Campbell (“ The Jilted Nymph ”) —“Better
be courted and jilted than never be courted at
all.” And it was irrelevantly observed that
Tennyson just managed to miss the point. What
is really better, is to have been loved, not merely to
have loved.
A recent smellful and unsuccessful court
case against a N.S.W. policeman is said to have
originated in the animosity of certain brothers in
blue. The constable’s transfer was consequent
upon another serious charge brought against him
a year or two back, and whatever the merits of
either prosecution, the readiness with which his
comrades—usually so prone to “stick” to each
other—came forward to bear witness against him
was really astonishing. Meanwhile, residents of
the district—the scene of more than one recent
police scandal—are sighing for the appointment of
John Norton’s inquiry commission.
